10 min
Hudud: The Distinctive Mark of Islamic Penal Law
Legal philosophy argument on the necessity and application of hudud

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why hudud punishments are indispensable in an Islamic penal code and identify the principle of adopting the most restrictive fiqh school for their application.

Core Idea
Hudud punishments are the defining feature of an Islamic penal code; their strict conditions safeguard society.

This passage by the contemporary Hanafi jurist Mustafa al-Zarqa emphasizes the central role of hudud (fixed punishments) in any legal system derived from Sharia. He argues that hudud are the distinctive mark of an Islamic penal code and that their physical nature is justified by the grave societal harm of the crimes they address. Crucially, al-Zarqa recommends adopting the broadest of the considered fiqh schools, meaning the most restrictive in terms of conditions, to ensure just and cautious application. This lesson unpacks his reasoning and its implications for the mufti's practice.

Guided Path
1
Identify the role of hudud in Islamic law
Read the passage and note how al-Zarqa describes hudud: as essential, physical punishments that serve as the distinctive sign of an Islamic penal code. Understand why he considers them indispensable.
2
Analyze the rationale for physical punishments
Examine the author's reasoning: hudud crimes threaten the very foundations of society (individuals, families, morals, security, economy). Physical punishment is the prescribed deterrent.
In Hanafi usul, this connects to the principle of sadd al-dhara'i' (blocking means to evil).
3
Understand the principle of adopting the broadest school
Al-Zarqa recommends adopting the broadest of the considered fiqh schools, i.e., the most restrictive in conditions for applying hudud. This is a form of takhayyur (selection) but with a specific criterion: maximal caution.
In practice, the Hanafi school often has the most stringent conditions (e.g., minimum nisab for theft, four witnesses for zina).
4
Recognize the defensive argument
Note that al-Zarqa believes hudud can be defended strongly against critics, including Westernized Muslims and foreigners. This reflects a modern apologetic approach to Islamic criminal law.
The author references his own work on qisas and the hadd of theft.
5
Relate to the mufti's role
Consider how a mufti might apply this principle in a modern fatwa: when asked about hudud, he should refer to the most restrictive conditions to ensure justice and avoid over-application.
This aligns with the Hanafi maxim 'al-hudud tudra'u bi al-shubuhat' (hudud are warded off by doubts).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
٢٢/ ١٦ - وأما عقوبات الحدود فلا يمكن أن يخلو منها قانون مستمداً عقوبات ويعتبر من الشريعة. فعقوبات الحدود تكاد تكون هي الشارة المميزة لقانون العقوبات أنه إسلامي وهي كلها عقوبات بدنية لأنَّ جرائمها جميعاً ذات تأثير عظيم في زعزعة ركائز المجتمع أفراداً وأُسراً وأخلاقاً وأمناً واقتصاداً. وإذا طبقت بشرائطها الشرعية في المجتمع استقرت ركائزه إلى أقصى حد ممكن. وأرى أن يُؤخذ مبدئياً في الحدود والقصاص بأوسع المذاهب الفقهية في المعتبرة، أي بأكثرها تضييقاً في شرائط إنزال هاتين العقوبتين. وفي رأيي أنَّ جميع الحدود يمكن الدفاع عنها بقوة وحجج دامغة وجه خصوم الإسلام من أبنائه المتحللين المتغربين، ومن الأجانب. وقد كتبت في هذا الكتاب في موضوع (القصاص دفاعاً عنها، وخاصة حد السرقة بقطع اليد ما يفحم كل مكابر (ر) نظرية المؤيدات التأديبية في أول الجزء الثاني من هذا الكتاب).
22/16 - As for hudud punishments, no law that derives its punishments from Sharia can be devoid of them. Indeed, hudud punishments are almost the distinctive mark of a penal code being Islamic, and they are all physical punishments because their crimes all have a great impact in shaking the foundations of society: individuals, families, morals, security, and economy. If they are applied with their legal conditions in society, its foundations will be stabilized to the maximum extent possible. I am of the opinion that, in principle, one should adopt in hudud and qisas the broadest of the considered fiqh schools, meaning the most restrictive in conditions for applying these two punishments. In my view, all hudud can be defended strongly and with conclusive arguments against the opponents of Islam from its own apostate, westernized individuals and from foreigners. I have written in this book on the subject of qisas in defense of them, especially the hadd of theft by cutting off the hand, which silences every stubborn opponent. See the Theory of Disciplinary Sanctions at the beginning of the second part of this book.
Source pages p. 304

Commentary
This passage argues that hudud (fixed punishments for certain major crimes) are essential to any Islamic penal code and serve as its distinguishing feature. Hudud are all physical punishments because the crimes they address threaten the core of society: families, morals, security, and economy. Proper application of hudud with their strict conditions ensures societal stability. The author recommends adopting the most restrictive school of fiqh (i.e., with the tightest conditions) for applying hudud and qisas, which in Hanafi context often means requiring the highest evidentiary standards. The author also notes that hudud can be defended against critics, mentioning his own defense of the hadd for theft (amputation of hand). This reflects a pragmatic approach to implementing hudud in modern times: insistence on their necessity but caution in application.

Key Terms
الحدود (al-hudud): Fixed punishments prescribed by Sharia for specific crimes (theft, zina, qadhf, drinking, robbery, apostasy).
الشارة المميزة (al-sharat al-mumayyizah): Distinctive sign or mark; here, hudud are the feature that marks a law as Islamic.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the author, what approach should be taken regarding the conditions for applying hudud and qisas?
A) The most lenient school to encourage application.
B) The broadest school, meaning the most restrictive in conditions.
C) A single school without variation.
D) No hudud should be applied until a Caliphate is established.

Key Takeaway
Hudud are essential to an Islamic penal code; the cautious mufti applies the most restrictive conditions among schools to ensure justice and protect society.

Critique of the Western Distinction in Qisas
Criticizing Western distinction in qisas and asserting Islamic remedy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to critically evaluate the Western legal distinction between premeditated and sudden murder from an Islamic perspective and explain the social consequences of adopting such distinctions.

Core Idea
Intent to kill is what matters; distinguishing by premeditation is a foreign innovation that undermines qisas and fuels vengeance.

Dr. Mustafa al-Zarqa, a prominent Hanafi jurist, critiques the adoption of Western legal distinctions in Muslim penal codes. He argues that differentiating between premeditated and spontaneous murder is flawed because the killer's intent to take life is present in both cases. This lesson unpacks his critique and its implications for the Islamic penal system.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Western distinction
Distinguish between premeditated murder (with prior planning) and sudden murder (without premeditation). Note that some legal systems exempt the latter from the death penalty.
This distinction is not found in classical Hanafi law.
2
Recognize the author's critique
The author argues that this distinction is wrong because in both cases the killer has the intent to kill ('qasd' to take life). The presence of intent makes both equally subject to qisas.
Intent is the key element, not the degree of planning.
3
Understand the rationale
The existence of intent (qasd) is what justifies qisas in Islamic law. Premeditation does not change the moral or legal gravity of the act.
4
Note the practical consequence
Adopting this distinction has led to increased 'tha'r' (vengeance) in societies, as victims' families feel justice is not served.
The author claims it has caused 'many ills of vengeance'.
5
Conclude with Islamic remedy
The solution is to apply qisas without such arbitrary distinctions, as prescribed by Sharia, which is the effective cure.
The author emphasizes 'shar' Allah' as the remedy.

Source Text

ملحق ثالث بالفصل (٢٢) النظام الجنائي وفق الشريعة
الضحايا وزجر العادين سجن مهما طال أو أي تدبير آخر، بل كل عدول عن القصاص هو دفع إلى الثأر الذي طالما ضجت منه ضمائر الإنسانية في التاريخ (ر ) في الجزء الثاني من هذا الكتاب نظرية المؤيدات التشريعية - بحث المؤيدات التأديبية - القصاص).
جميع قوانین
ومن أهم ما يجب في هذا الشأن عدم الانسياق النظريات الأجنبية مع التي دلت التجارب الطويلة على عدم جدواها ولا سيما التمييز في القتل العدواني بين أن يكون عن سابق تصور وتصميم أو يكون بفكرة مفاجئة غير مبيتة. فلا يحكمون بالإعدام في الحالة الثانية كما فعلت العقوبات في البلاد الإسلامية بدءاً من الدولة العثمانية في أواخر عهدها وانتهاءً بالبلاد العربية جميعاً فهذا التمييز خطأ بعد وجود قصد القاتل إلى إزهاقه الروح. وقد قاست البلاد كثيراً من ويلات الثأر بسبب هذا التمييز المستورد من الجهات الأجنبية وشرع الله هو العلاج الناجح.
٢٢/ ١٦ - وأما عقوبات الحدود فلا يمكن أن يخلو منها قانون مستمداً عقوبات ويعتبر من الشريعة. فعقوبات الحدود تكاد تكون هي الشارة المميزة لقانون العقوبات أنه إسلامي وهي كلها عقوبات بدنية لأنَّ جرائمها جميعاً ذات تأثير عظيم في زعزعة ركائز المجتمع أفراداً وأُسراً وأخلاقاً وأمناً واقتصاداً. وإذا طبقت بشرائطها الشرعية في المجتمع استقرت ركائزه إلى أقصى حد ممكن.
وأرى أن يُؤخذ مبدئياً في الحدود والقصاص بأوسع المذاهب الفقهية المعتبرة، أي بأكثرها تضييقاً في شرائط إنزال هاتين العقوبتين. وفي رأيي أنَّ جميع الحدود يمكن الدفاع عنها بقوة وحجج دامغة وجه خصوم الإسلام من أبنائه المتحللين المتغربين، ومن الأجانب. وقد كتبت في هذا الكتاب في موضوع (القصاص دفاعاً عنها، وخاصة حد السرقة بقطع اليد ما يفحم كل مكابر (ر) نظرية المؤيدات التأديبية في أول الجزء الثاني من هذا الكتاب).
Third appendix to chapter (22): The penal system according to Sharia. The victims and deterrence of aggressors [with] imprisonment however long or any other measure. Rather, any deviation from qisas is a push towards vengeance, from which the consciences of humanity have long groaned in history (see in the second part of this book the theory of legislative sanctions - study of disciplinary sanctions - qisas). All laws. Among the most important things required in this regard is not to follow foreign theories, which long experiences have shown to be futile, especially the distinction in homicide between whether it is with prior thought and intent or is a sudden unplanned idea. So they do not impose the death penalty in the second case, as the penal codes in Islamic countries have done, starting from the Ottoman Empire in its late period and ending in all Arab countries. This distinction is wrong after the existence of the killer's intent to end the life. The countries have suffered many ills of vengeance because of this imported distinction from foreign parties, and the law of God is the effective remedy. 22/16 - As for the hudud punishments, no law derived from Sharia can be devoid of them. The hudud punishments are almost the distinguishing mark that a penal code is Islamic, and they are all bodily punishments because their crimes all have a great impact in shaking the foundations of society: individuals, families, morals, security, and economy. If they are applied with their Shar'i conditions in society, its foundations will be stabilized to the utmost extent possible. I see that in principle, in hudud and qisas, the broadest of the considered juristic schools should be adopted, i.e., the most restrictive in the conditions for applying these two punishments. In my opinion, all hudud can be defended forcefully with conclusive arguments against the opponents of Islam from its dissolute westernized sons and from foreigners. I have written in this book on the subject of 'qisas' in defense of them, and especially the punishment of theft by cutting off the hand, which confounds every obstinate person (see the theory of disciplinary sanctions at the beginning of the second part of this book).
Source pages p. 304

Commentary
This passage is a legal-philosophical argument by Dr. Mustafa al-Zarqa in his book 'Al-Madkhal al-Fiqhi al-'Aam'. He discusses the Islamic penal system, specifically qisas (retaliation) and hudud (fixed punishments). He begins by critiquing the Western legal distinction between premeditated and sudden murder, noting that such distinctions have been adopted by Muslim-majority countries starting from the late Ottoman era. He argues that this distinction is flawed because the killer's intent to take life is present in both cases, and it has led to increased vengeance in society. The proper Islamic remedy is qisas without such distinctions. Next, he addresses hudud punishments, stating they are indispensable for a Sharia-derived penal code. Hudud are bodily punishments because the crimes they punish severely undermine the foundations of society. He advocates for adopting the strictest juristic opinions regarding the conditions required to apply hudud and qisas, meaning the narrowest interpretations that make execution of punishment most difficult. He asserts that all hudud can be robustly defended against critics, referencing his own previous work on qisas and the cutting-of-hand punishment for theft. The author's tone is defensive and reformist, emphasizing the wisdom and necessity of these punishments in maintaining social order.

Key Terms
القصاص (al-qisas): Retaliation in kind for homicide or bodily harm, a fixed punishment under Islamic law.
الحدود (al-hudud): Fixed punishments prescribed by Sharia for specific crimes, considered rights of God.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to al-Zarqa, why is the distinction between premeditated and spontaneous murder flawed?
A) It is against international law.
B) It ignores the killer's intent, which is present in both cases.
C) It was invented by Western colonizers.
D) It makes it harder to prove murder.

Key Takeaway
The Islamic legal system does not distinguish between premeditated and sudden murder when the killer's intent is present; both are subject to qisas.

Defending Hudud: Necessity and Strict Conditions
Defending hudud as indispensable and recommending strictest conditions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to argue for the indispensability of hudud punishments in an Islamic penal system and explain the principle of adopting the most restrictive school conditions.

Core Idea
Hudud are essential for societal stability, but must be applied with the strictest conditions to avoid misuse.

In the second part of the passage, al-Zarqa emphasizes that hudud are the hallmark of an Islamic penal code. He advocates for adopting the broadest (most restrictive) juristic schools regarding conditions, making it difficult to apply punishments, which serves to protect individuals and defend the system against critics.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claim
The author states that no law derived from Sharia can be devoid of hudud punishments. They are the distinguishing mark of an Islamic penal code.
Hudud are fixed, bodily punishments.
2
Understand the rationale for hudud
Hudud crimes severely shake the foundations of society (individuals, families, morals, security, economy). Their physical nature serves as a strong deterrent.
3
Note the recommendation
The author advises adopting the 'broadest' (most restrictive) juristic schools regarding conditions for applying hudud and qisas, meaning the most difficult to satisfy.
This minimizes the risk of wrongful application.
4
Recognize the defensive value
By using strict conditions, hudud become defensible against critics, both internal (westernized Muslims) and external (foreigners).
The author states all hudud can be defended with conclusive arguments.

A) Use the strictest conditions from among the schools.

Definition of Ihsan: A Critical Analysis of the Majority Position
Critique of majority definition of ihsan

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the majority Hanafi definition of ihsan, evaluate al-Zarqa's critique, and state the thamarah al-khilaf.

Core Idea
Ihsan requires a current spouse who suffices from unlawful acts — not merely a past marriage.

The punishment of stoning (rajm) for adultery hinges on the status of ihsan. While the majority of jurists define ihsan as having ever engaged in valid marital intercourse, Mustafa al-Zarqa challenges this, arguing from Quranic usage that ihsan requires a current spouse. This lesson unpacks the dispute and its practical consequences for fiqh and fatwa.

Guided Path
1
Identify the punishment and its evidentiary basis
Note that the author acknowledges stoning is established in the authentic Sunnah with near consensus, but surrounded by stringent evidentiary requirements making conviction rare without confession.
This sets the context for the definitional debate.
2
State the majority definition of ihsan
The majority of fuqaha (including Hanafis) hold that ihsan is attained by one act of valid intercourse in a lawful marriage, and once attained it remains even after divorce or widowhood.
This is the view the author criticizes.
3
Present al-Zarqa's critique and his proposed definition
Al-Zarqa argues that the Quranic term 'muhsanat' in 4:24 means 'married women,' and by analogy, ihsan for stoning should mean having a current spouse who suffices for lawful needs, not merely a past marriage.
He also rejects interpretations that stoning was ta'zir.
4
Analyze the practical difference (thamarah al-khilaf)
Under the majority view, a divorced or widowed person who commits zina is still muhsan and liable for stoning. Under al-Zarqa's view, they would only receive flogging.
This difference has major implications for contemporary fatwa.

Source Text

أن عقوبة واحدة في تقديري من الحدود، لا يمكن إقناع غير المؤمنين بها هي الرجم للمحصن، فالزنى وإن لم يرد في القرآن عن عقوبته سوى الجَلْد دون تفريق بين مُحْصَن وغير محصن، قد ثبت فيه رجم المحصن في السنة النبوية الصحيحة ثبوتاً لا يقبل الريب.. وتكاد تجمع عليه المذاهب الفقهية سوى الخوارج.
لكن يلحظ أن هذه العقوبة، وهي من أقسى العقوبات على الإطلاق، على جريمة من أعظم الجرائم زعزعة لكيان الأسرة الإسلامية، قد حفت بشرائط في الإثبات من أبعد ما يكون عن إمكان التحقق - حتى إنه يمكن القول بأن جريمتها غير ممكنة الإثبات إلا من طريق الإقرار فيكون وجودها في النظام اسمياً فقط للهيبة. ومثل ذلك يقال في عقوبة الجلد للزاني غير المخصن. وهناك اليوم من يحاولون أن ينفوا وقوع الرجم في عهد الرسول وبأمره . ولكن هذا مكابرة. وهناك من يحاول أن يعتبروا أنَّ الرجم الذي أمر به النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم قد كان استعمالاً منه لسلطته في التعزير، وهذا محل نظر. على أن هناك ناحية مهمة جداً في شرائط الرجم يجب التنبيه إليها أن الإحصان قد فسره الفقهاء في معظم المذاهب أنه يتحقق بمجرد الوطء ولو مرة واحدة في نكاح صحيح حيث يصبح الشخص من رجل وامرأة محصناً ولو كان عند زناه غير متزوج.
وهي
وفي نظري أن هذا التفسير للمحصن لا تطمئن إليه النفس، بل التفسير السليم للمحصن أنه من زنى من رجل أو امرأة وله زوج يستغني به عن الحرام، ولا سيما بعد أن استعمل الإحصان بهذا المعنى في القرآن تعداد المحرمات في الزواج حيث قال الله تعالى :
حين
والمُحْصَنَاتُ مِنَ النِّسَاءِ ﴾ (سورة النساء (٢٤/٤) وقد اتفقت كلمة الفقهاء والمفسرين على أن معناها هنا المتزوجات فلا يجوز لرجل أن يتزوج امرأة ذات زوج. (ر: ف ۸/٢١)
One punishment in my estimation from among the hudud that cannot convince non-believers is stoning for the muhsan (married adulterer). For zina (fornication/adultery), although the Quran mentions only flogging without distinction between muhsan and non-muhsan, stoning of the muhsan is established in the authentic prophetic sunnah with undeniable certainty. Almost all juristic schools agree on it except the Khawarij. However, it is observed that this punishment — which is among the harshest punishments altogether — for a crime that is among the greatest in destabilizing the Islamic family structure, is surrounded by conditions of proof that are farthest from the possibility of verification — so much so that it can be said that the crime is impossible to prove except by confession, so its existence in the system is merely nominal for deterrence. Similar can be said about the flogging punishment for the non-muhsan fornicator. Today there are those who try to deny the occurrence of stoning during the Prophet's time and by his command. But this is stubbornness. And there are those who try to consider that the stoning ordered by the Prophet (peace be upon him) was an exercise of his authority in ta'zir (discretionary punishment), and this is a point of consideration. However, there is a very important aspect in the conditions of stoning that must be pointed out: that ihsan has been interpreted by jurists in most schools to be realized by mere intercourse, even once, in a valid marriage, so that a man or woman becomes muhsan even if at the time of his/her zina he/she is not married. In my view, this interpretation of muhsan is not reassuring to the soul. Rather, the correct interpretation of muhsan is someone who commits zina from a man or woman who has a spouse that suffices him/her from unlawful acts, especially after ihsan was used in this meaning in the Quran in the enumeration of prohibited women in marriage, where Allah said: 'And the muhsanat among women' (Surah al-Nisa 4:24) — and the words of jurists and exegetes agree that its meaning here is married women, so a man may not marry a woman who has a husband. (See: Fiqh 8/21).
Source pages p. 305

Commentary
In this passage, Dr. al-Zarqa addresses the punishment of stoning (rajm) for adultery committed by a muhsan (married person). He acknowledges that stoning is a harsh punishment, but emphasizes its strong basis in the authentic Sunnah, with near consensus of the schools. However, he notes that in practice, the evidentiary requirements are so stringent that conviction is nearly impossible without confession, making the punishment more symbolic for deterrence. He then criticizes those who deny the historical occurrence of stoning or reinterpret it as a discretionary ta'zir punishment. The core of his argument is about the definition of ihsan (the status that makes one liable for stoning). The majority of jurists (including the Hanafi school) define ihsan as having ever engaged in valid sexual intercourse within a marriage; thus, a person once married remains muhsan even if currently divorced or widowed. Al-Zarqa rejects this, arguing that ihsan should mean having a current spouse who suffices for one's lawful sexual needs, citing the Quranic usage of 'muhsanat' in 4:24 to mean 'married women.' He thus proposes a more restrictive definition that would limit stoning to those who commit adultery while still married and able to satisfy their desires lawfully. This passage reflects a modern reformist attempt to narrow the application of a classical punishment while affirming its principle.

Key Terms
الرجم (al-rajm): Stoning to death as a punishment for a muhsan adulterer.
المحصن (al-muhsan): A person who has experienced valid marital intercourse, rendering them liable for stoning if they commit zina.
الإحصان (al-ihsan): The legal state of being muhsan, contested between requiring current marital status or mere past experience.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the author, what is the correct definition of ihsan for the purpose of stoning?
A) Having ever had valid sexual intercourse in a marriage.
B) Being currently married and having a spouse who suffices for lawful needs.
C) Having children.
D) Being older than 18.

Key Takeaway
The definition of ihsan determines whether a divorced or widowed person is liable for stoning or flogging, and this ikhtilaf has significant practical consequences for modern fatwa.

Redefining Ihsan: A Critique of the Classical Condition for Rajm
Critique of ihsan definition for rajm

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to evaluate the classical Hanafi definition of ihsan and construct a counter-argument based on Quranic usage and contemporary legal philosophy.

Core Idea
Ihsan for rajm requires current marital satisfaction, not just past intercourse.

This passage from Mustafa al-Zarqa's Al-Madkhal al-Fiqhi al-Amm challenges the widely accepted definition of ihsan (the condition for stoning punishment). While affirming the establishment of rajm in the Sunnah, al-Zarqa argues that its proof conditions are nearly impossible to satisfy and that the definition of ihsan itself must be reconsidered. He proposes that true ihsan requires the existence of a current spouse who fulfills one's sexual needs, not merely a past lawful intercourse. This critique has profound implications for the application of this severe punishment in contemporary contexts.

Guided Path
1
State the classical Hanafi definition
Identify the classical position: ihsan is achieved by one act of lawful intercourse in a valid marriage, regardless of current marital status.
2
Identify the author's challenge
Note al-Zarqa's argument that this definition is unsound and contradicts Quranic usage of the term 'muhsan'.
3
Examine the Quranic evidence
Analyze how al-Zarqa uses Quran 4:24 ('wa al-muhsanatu min al-nisa') to claim ihsan implies current marriage.
He draws an analogy between the term in the verse and its use in the context of rajm.
4
Evaluate the practical difference
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf: under the classical definition, a divorced person who commits zina is stoned; under al-Zarqa's definition, only flogging applies.
5
Assess philosophical and practical implications
Consider al-Zarqa's broader point about the symbolic nature of hudud and how redefinition aligns with his legal philosophy.

الباب الرابع: استمداد التقنين المعاصر من الفقه
أن عقوبة واحدة في تقديري من الحدود، لا يمكن إقناع غير المؤمنين بها هي الرجم للمحصن، فالزنى وإن لم يرد في القرآن عن عقوبته سوى الجَلْد دون تفريق بين مُحْصَن وغير محصن، قد ثبت فيه رجم المحصن في السنة النبوية الصحيحة ثبوتاً لا يقبل الريب.. وتكاد تجمع عليه المذاهب الفقهية سوى الخوارج.

وهناك اليوم من يحاولون أن ينفوا وقوع الرجم في عهد الرسول وبأمره . ولكن هذا مكابرة.
وهناك من يحاول أن يعتبروا أنَّ الرجم الذي أمر به النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم قد كان استعمالاً منه لسلطته في التعزير، وهذا محل نظر.
على أن هناك ناحية مهمة جداً في شرائط الرجم يجب التنبيه إليها أن الإحصان قد فسره الفقهاء في معظم المذاهب أنه يتحقق بمجرد الوطء ولو مرة واحدة في نكاح صحيح حيث يصبح الشخص من رجل وامرأة محصناً ولو كان عند زناه غير متزوج.
وفي نظري أن هذا التفسير للمحصن لا تطمئن إليه النفس، بل التفسير السليم للمحصن أنه من زنى من رجل أو امرأة وله زوج يستغني به عن الحرام، ولا سيما بعد أن استعمل الإحصان بهذا المعنى في القرآن تعداد المحرمات في الزواج حيث قال الله تعالى :
والمُحْصَنَاتُ مِنَ النِّسَاءِ ﴾ (سورة النساء ٢٤/٤) وقد اتفقت كلمة الفقهاء والمفسرين على أن معناها هنا المتزوجات فلا يجوز لرجل أن يتزوج امرأة ذات زوج. (ر: ف ۸/۲۱)
٣٢٤
Chapter Four: Derivation of Contemporary Legislation from Fiqh. One punishment in my estimation that is among the hudud, which cannot convince non-believers, is stoning for the married adulterer. For although zinā (adultery) has no punishment in the Qur'an other than flogging without distinction between married and unmarried, the stoning of the married adulterer has been established in the authentic Prophetic Sunnah with a proof that admits no doubt. And almost all fiqh schools agree upon it, except the Khawārij. But note that this punishment – one of the harshest punishments ever, for a crime that is among the greatest in shaking the foundation of the Muslim family – is surrounded by conditions of proof that are as far as possible from being verifiable, such that it can be said that the crime cannot be proven except through confession, so its existence in the system is only nominal for deterrent effect. The same can be said about the flogging punishment for the unmarried adulterer. Today, some try to deny the occurrence of stoning at the time of the Messenger and by his command, but this is obstinacy. And some try to consider that the stoning ordered by the Prophet, peace be upon him, was a use of his authority for discretionary punishment (ta'zīr), but this is questionable. Moreover, there is a very important aspect in the conditions of stoning that must be pointed out: that the jurists in most schools have interpreted ihsān (being muhsan) as being achieved merely by intercourse even once in a valid marriage, so that a person, male or female, becomes muhsan even if at the time of zinā he is not married. In my view, this interpretation of muhsan does not satisfy the soul. Rather, the sound interpretation of muhsan is that a person, male or female, commits zinā while having a spouse that satisfies his need for the unlawful, especially since ihsān is used in this sense in the Qur'an in the enumeration of prohibited women, where Allah says: 'and the muhsanāt from among women' (Sūrat al-Nisā' 4:24). And all jurists and exegetes agree that here it means married women, so no man may marry a woman who has a husband. (Ref: F 8/21). 324
Source pages p. 305

Commentary
This passage is a legal philosophy analysis by Dr. Mustafa al-Zarqa. He begins by acknowledging that the punishment of rajm (stoning) for the muhsan (married adulterer) is established by definitive Sunnah and accepted by all schools except the Khawarij. However, he then critiques the practical feasibility of implementing this punishment due to its stringent proof requirements, essentially making it symbolic. He also rejects attempts to deny its historical occurrence or reclassify it as ta'zir. The core of his argument is a redefinition of ihsan: contrary to the majority opinion (including the Hanafi school, where ihsan is attained by one act of lawful intercourse in a valid marriage, even if currently unmarried), al-Zarqa insists that true ihsan requires the existence of a current spouse who can satisfy sexual needs. He supports this by analogy with the Quranic usage of 'muhsanat' meaning married women in the context of prohibited marriages. Thus, in his view, a divorced or widowed person who has had intercourse in the past would not be muhsan for purposes of the stoning punishment. This is a significant departure from the classical Hanafi position.

Key Terms
الإحصان (al-iḥṣān): حالة الشخص الذي له زوج يستغني به عن الحرام عند الزنا (حسب المؤلف)، أو من سبق له وطء في نكاح صحيح ولو لم يكن متزوجاً (عند الجمهور).
الرجم (al-rajm): عقوبة القتل بالحجارة على الزاني المحصن.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) Having had lawful intercourse once in the past
B) Being currently married and having a spouse who satisfies one's needs
C) Being legally competent and free
D) Being married at the time of the first intercourse

Key Takeaway
The definition of ihsan is pivotal for rajm; a shift from past intercourse to current marital satisfaction would drastically limit the application of this severe punishment.

Key Lines for Codifying Islamic Penal Law: The Hanafi Paradigm
Five principles for unifying penal legislation based on Islamic law

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the five main lines for unifying penal legislation based on Islamic law and explain the Hanafi position on the effect of pardon in qisas.

Core Idea
A pardon in qisas nullifies execution but not the ruler's right to ta'zir.

This passage from Mustafa al-Zarqa outlines practical principles for codifying Islamic penal law in contemporary states. Each line balances strict legal theory with pragmatic application, reflecting a deep understanding of Hanafi fiqh. Particularly important is the third principle, which corrects a common misconception: the pardon of a victim's family does not fully absolve the killer, because qisas involves both a private and a public right.

Guided Path
1
Identify the five main lines
Read the passage carefully and list the five numbered points. Each point addresses a different aspect of penal codification: hudud, qisas, role of ta'zir, sources, and contemporary crimes.
Point 3 is key for Hanafi distinctiveness.
2
Analyze the hudud vs. qisas approach
Compare the first two points: for hudud, adopt the broadest (most lenient) schools to minimize application (dar' al-hudud). For qisas, adopt the strictest schools to also minimize application, and if conditions fail, default to ta'zir.
Both strategies aim to restrict severe punishments while preserving legal principles.
3
Examine the Hanafi principle on qisas and pardon
Focus on point three: the pardon by the victim's family only prevents execution. The ruler retains the right to ta'zir because qisas includes a public right (haqq Allah / haqq al-sultan).
This counters the common belief that pardon absolves all punishment.
4
Understand the role of ta'zir
From points four and five, note that ta'zir must be proportionate, guided by classical authorities (al-Mawardi) and modern scholars (Abd al-Qadir Ouda), and should target prevalent crimes like alcohol and drugs.
Ta'zir is flexible and can be adapted to contemporary needs.

17/22 - خطوط رئيسية :
ملحق ثالث بالفصل (22) النظام الجنائي وفق الشريعة
وختاماً ألخص تصوراتي للخطوط الرئيسية في عملية توحيد التشريع الجنائي للبلاد العربية على أساس الشريعة الإسلامية بما يلي:
١ - إعطاء الاهتمام الأول لتقنين عقوبات الحدود، وبمنتهى الدقة والوضوح في استيعاب شرائطها، لأنها هي التي تظهر حكمتها وعدالتها. والأخذ في ذلك بأوسع المذاهب درءاً لها.
٢ - عدم التردد في الأخذ بأحكام القصاص في النفس وما دونها وبأضيق المذاهب في شرائطها أي أكثرها درءاً لها. فمتى لم تتوافر شرائطها في جميع المذاهب تنقل عقوبتها إلى نطاق التعزير.
٣ - يجب التبيين بوضوح أن سقوط القصاص بعفو ولي القتيل ينحصر تأثيره في عدم تطبيق الإعدام، وليس معناه عدم مسؤولية القاتل فإن في القصاص حقاً عاماً يمثله السلطان فيبقى له بعد عفو ولي الدم أن يعاقب الجاني تعزيراً بما يكفي للزجر. وقد أوردت أدلة ذلك في الكتاب، خلافاً لما يتصوّره كثيرون. (ر) نظرية المؤيدات التأديبية الآتية في أول الجزء الثاني من هذا الكتاب).
٤ - مراعاة المبادىء العامة في التعزير الشرعي، ومن أهمها تناسب العقوبة مع الجريمة وتراعى أيضاً في هذا الشأن التوجيهات التي قررها فقهاء المذاهب.
ويعتمد في هذا الشأن من المراجع القديمة كتاب «الأحكام السلطانية» للماوردي، ومن الكتب والبحوث الحديثة كتاب التشريع الجنائي في الإسلام للأستاذ الشهيد عبد القادر عودة رحمه الله وأكرم مثواه.
٥ - إعطاء أولوية في نطاق التعزير للاهتمام بالجرائم المتفشية في هذا العصر بتأثير المدنية المادية الزائفة وفساد الذمم والأخلاق كجرائم الشرب وتعاطي المخدرات استعمالاً، والتشديد بالعقوبة على تجارتها السرية.
٣٢٥
17/22 - Main Lines: Third Appendix to Chapter (22): The Penal System According to Sharia. And in conclusion, I summarize my perceptions of the main lines in the process of unifying the penal legislation of the Arab countries on the basis of Islamic law as follows: 1- Giving primary attention to codifying hudud punishments, with utmost precision and clarity in encompassing their conditions, because they are what demonstrate their wisdom and justice. And in that, adopting the broadest (most lenient) schools to avert them (i.e., to minimize application). 2- Not hesitating to adopt the rulings of qisas (retaliation) for life and less than life, and adopting the strictest schools in their conditions – i.e., those that most avert them. So whenever their conditions are not met in all schools, their punishment is transferred to the sphere of ta'zir (discretionary punishment). 3- It must be clearly explained that the dropping of qisas due to the pardon of the legal guardian of the slain is limited in effect to not applying execution, and it does not mean the absence of responsibility of the killer; for there is a public right in qisas represented by the ruler, so he retains after the pardon of the blood guardian the right to punish the offender by ta'zir sufficiently for deterrence. I have presented evidence for that in the book, contrary to what many imagine. (Ref: The theory of disciplinary supports to come in the beginning of the second part of this book). 4- Observing the general principles of legal ta'zir, the most important of which is proportionality of punishment to the crime, and also in this regard the directives established by the jurists of the schools are to be observed. And relied upon in this regard from the old references is the book 'al-Ahkam al-Sultaniyya' by al-Mawardi, and from modern books and research is the book 'al-Tashri' al-Jina'i fi al-Islam' by the martyred professor Abd al-Qadir Ouda, may Allah have mercy on him and honor his abode. 5- Giving priority within the scope of ta'zir to paying attention to crimes prevalent in this age due to the influence of false material civilization and corruption of consciences and morals, such as crimes of drinking and drug use, and severity of punishment on their secret trade. 325
Source pages p. 306

Commentary
This passage presents a modern jurist's (Mustafa al-Zarqa) practical recommendations for codifying Islamic penal law in contemporary Arab states. Each point reflects a balance between strict legal theory and pragmatic application. Point 1 recommends codifying hudud with precision but adopting the most lenient (broadest) school interpretations to minimize their application (درء الحدود بالشبهات). Point 2 does the opposite for qisas: adopt the strictest school conditions to also minimize application, and when conditions are not met, default to ta'zir. Point 3 clarifies a critical Hanafi principle: a pardon by the victim's family only prevents execution but does not absolve the killer; the ruler retains the right to impose a discretionary ta'zir punishment because qisas also involves a public right (حق الله/السلطان). This counters a common misconception. Point 4 emphasizes proportionality in ta'zir and references classical (al-Mawardi) and modern (Abd al-Qadir Ouda) authorities. Point 5 focuses on combating contemporary crimes like alcohol and drug abuse with severe ta'zir. The author does not provide detailed evidence here but refers to his own book and other sources.

Key Terms
التعزير (al-ta'zīr): عقوبة تقديرية يحددها الحاكم أو القاضي لتحقيق الزجر والتأديب، غير محددة بنص شرعي.
القصاص (al-qiṣāṣ): عقوبة المماثلة في الجناية على النفس أو ما دونها، كالقتل قصاصاً.
الحدود (al-ḥudūd): عقوبات مقدرة شرعاً منصوص عليها في القرآن أو السنة، كالحد للزنا والسرقة.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) No punishment at all
B) The killer must still be executed
C) The ruler can impose a discretionary ta'zir punishment
D) The killer must pay diya

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi distinction between private and public rights in qisas ensures that pardon by the victim's family does not leave serious crime unpunished.

Basic Legal Theories: The Systematic Frameworks of Hanafi Fiqh
Introduction to systematic legal theories in fiqh

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define basic legal theories and distinguish them from general legal maxims using Hanafi examples.

Core Idea
Basic legal theories are the conceptual nervous systems of fiqh, each governing a key branch like ownership, contract, or capacity.

This passage marks a shift from the historical overview of Islamic jurisprudence to its systematic structure. The author introduces 'basic legal theories' as comprehensive frameworks that organize entire branches of rulings, much like the nervous system in the human body. Understanding these theories is essential for a mufti to navigate fiqh coherently, as they are distinct from the concise legal maxims (qawā'id kulliyya) compiled in the Majalla.

Guided Path
1
Identify the shift in focus
Note that the author moves from a historical presentation to a systematic one. Recognize that the second section of the book will examine the structural elements of fiqh.
This is a conceptual pivot from diachronic to synchronic analysis.
2
Grasp the metaphor
The author compares theories to the nervous system. Understand that each theory (e.g., ownership, contract) functions as an integrated system that governs a distinct domain of rulings, and its influence permeates all related issues.
The metaphor emphasizes interconnectedness and systematic governance.
3
List the examples given
The passage lists nine theories: ownership, contract, legal capacity, agency, nullity/invalidity/suspension, suspension/restriction/addition in verbal dispositions, guarantee, and custom. Commit these to memory as they form the core of the second section.
These are not exhaustive but representative.
4
Distinguish theories from maxims
The author explicitly states that these theories are other than the general legal maxims (qawā'id kulliyya). Theories are broader systems, while maxims are succinct rules that summarize many cases. For example, the maxim 'al-ghunm bi al-ghurm' is a rule, but the theory of guarantee is the larger framework that includes causes, types, and effects.
This distinction is crucial for proper legal analysis.
5
Understand the modern classification
The term 'nathariyya' is used in a modern academic sense, not as classical terminology. Recognize that this approach is a hallmark of 20th-century reformist usul thinking, which seeks to present fiqh as an integrated legal science.
This classification aids systematic teaching and fatwa derivation.

وهذه النظريات هي غير القواعد الكلية التي صدرت مجلة الأحكام
The Second Section: Basic Legal Theories in the Principles of Rulings

Preamble to this second section of the book
1/23 - We have finished that historical introductory presentation in which we presented a sufficient picture of Islamic jurisprudence, its sources, stages, and phases that it went through from its inception until today in the first section of this general jurisprudential introduction. And we now move to the second section, in which we will present the various basic legal theories in the foundations of rulings, which are the theories upon which the edifice of Islamic jurisprudence actually stands and are considered like the engineering element in its great eternal structure.
2/23 - Meaning of these theories:
By basic legal theories we mean those major constitutions and concepts, each of which separately constitutes an objective legal system permeating Islamic jurisprudence like the permeation of the parts of the nervous system in the regions of the human body, and the elements of that system govern everything related to its subject among the branches of rulings, such as the idea of ownership and its causes, the idea of contract and its rules and consequences, the idea of legal capacity and its types, stages, and impediments, the idea of agency and its divisions, the idea of nullity, invalidity, and suspension, the idea of suspension, restriction, and addition in verbal dispositions, the idea of guarantee and its causes and types, the idea of custom and its authority in determining obligations, and other such major theories upon which jurisprudence as a whole is based, and one encounters the effect of their authority in solving all jurisprudential issues and incidents.
And these theories are other than the general legal maxims that were issued by the Majalla of Rulings.
Source pages p. 308

Commentary
This passage is the preamble to the second major section of the book. The author, Mustafa Ahmad al-Zarqa, signals a shift from the historical overview of Islamic fiqh to a systematic examination of its foundational legal theories. He defines these theories as comprehensive concepts that operate like a nervous system, each governing a distinct area of fiqh such as property (al-milkiyya), contract (al-‘aqd), legal capacity (al-ahliyya), agency (al-niyāba), nullity/voidness (al-buṭlān wa al-fasād), suspension/restriction (al-ta‘līq wa al-taqyīd), guarantee (al-ḍamān), and custom (al-‘urf). He explicitly distinguishes these theories from the general legal maxims (al-qawā‘id al-kulliyya) codified in the Ottoman Majalla. This clarifies that the book's second half will focus on macro-level doctrinal structures rather than piecemeal rules. The term 'nathariyya' here is used in its modern academic sense of a systematic theory, not the speculative philosophy of kalām. This is a hallmark of the 20th-century reformist approach to usul al-fiqh as an integrated legal science.

Key Terms
النظريات الفقهية الأساسية (al-nathariyyāt al-fiqhiyya al-asāsiyya): Major comprehensive legal concepts each forming an objective system that governs a branch of rulings, such as ownership, contract, capacity, etc.
القواعد الكلية (al-qawā‘id al-kulliyya): General legal maxims that summarise many rulings, such as those contained in the Majalla.

Basic legal theories are the foundational conceptual systems of fiqh, each governing a distinct branch of rulings, and they are distinct from general legal maxims.

The Composite Definition of Ownership (al-Milk)
Parsing the composite definition of ownership

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the composite juristic definition of milk (ownership) and identify its five key components.

Core Idea
Ownership in Islamic law is a legally restricted allocation: it permits disposition unless a legal preventer exists.

This lesson introduces the foundational definition of ownership (al-milk) in Islamic fiqh. The author, Mustafa al-Zarqa, presents a composite definition that synthesizes earlier juristic formulations. We will dissect its elements: allocation (ikhtisas), restrictiveness (hajiz), legality (shar'an), permission to dispose (yusawwighu al-tasarruf), and the caveat of legal impediments (illa li mani'). Understanding this definition is crucial for muftis who must distinguish ownership from mere permission (ibaha) and apply it in property-related fatwas.

Guided Path
1
Identify the lexical meaning
Look at the linguistic definition from Lisan al-Arab: containment and ability to control.
Linguistic meaning grounds the juristic term.
2
Extract the composite definition
Parse the author's concise definition: ikhtisas hajiz shari'an yusawwighu al-tasarruf illa li mani'.
This is the core formulation.
3
Break down each component
Analyze each term: ikhtisas (allocation), hajiz (restrictive), shari'an (legally), yusawwighu (permits), tasarruf (disposition), mani' (preventer).
Each component adds precision.
4
Note the synthetic nature
The author compiled from multiple definitions; appreciate the strengths of each and the final synthesis.
The definition corrects deficiencies of previous ones.
5
Apply the definition
Consider a scenario where ownership is claimed; test each component to verify if the claim holds.
Practical application solidifies understanding.

Chapter Five: The Theory of Ownership in Islamic Fiqh. Outline of Chapter Five: 1 - Definition of ownership and its causes: we will cover it in Chapter 23. 2 - Division of ownership and its causes: we will cover it in Chapter 24. 3 - Characteristics of ownership: it is the subject of Chapter 25. 4 - The difference between tamlik (transfer of ownership) and ibaha (permission), and the difference between ownership of usufruct and right of usufruct: these are the subjects of Chapter 26 with which we conclude the theory of ownership. We will explain below the theory of ownership in these branches and their offshoots, state the basic fiqhi principles in them, and clarify their practical results that emerge in the branches of rulings. Chapter Twenty-Three: Definition of ownership and its causes. Section One: Definition of ownership. 3/23 - Al-Milk (1) in the linguistic sense means the containment of a thing and the ability to control it (Lisan al-Arab). As for the terminological sense of the jurists (shar'an), milk is: a legally restrictive allocation that permits its owner to dispose [of it] unless there is a preventer. And this is the briefest and most comprehensive definition that we have extracted, composed from several definitions mentioned by the jurists (2), in each of which there is an advantage and a deficiency, so it gathered their advantages and rectified their deficiencies. (1) Al-Milk: the letter mim can have three vowels (fatha, kasra, damma). But it is used with kasra and fatha for ownership of things, and with damma for sovereignty. One says: malaktu al-shay'a mulkan (with kasra) and malaktu 'ala al-nas amrahum mulkan (with damma). The adjective from the first is malik, plural: mullak (with damma and tashdid of lam). The adjective from the second is malik (with fatha and sukun of lam), plural: muluk and amlak (as in al-Misbah). And al-malaka (with two fatha) also means ownership. One says: lahu 'alayhi malaka, i.e., ownership; not in his malaka, i.e., he does not own anything (as in al-Lisan and al-Misbah). So the word 'milkiyya' with shadda used by scholars may be derived from milk with kasra and sukun, or from malaka with two fatha. (2) Those various definitions of milk were transmitted by Sheikh Muhammad Abu Zahra in his book al-Milkiyya, p. 333.
Source pages p. 311

Commentary
This passage opens the fifth chapter of the book, which is dedicated to the theory of ownership (milkiyya) in Islamic fiqh. The author, Mustafa al-Zarqa, first provides an outline of the chapter's four sections, then begins Chapter Twenty-Three with a definition of ownership. The passage is a combination of a lexical definition from Lisan al-Arab and a synthetic juristic definition that the author compiles from multiple earlier definitions. The definition itself is concise: 'Ownership is a legally restrictive allocation that permits its owner to dispose unless there is a preventer.' The key components are: (1) ikhtisas (allocation), meaning exclusive assignment to a person; (2) hajiz (restrictive), indicating a legal barrier that excludes others; (3) shar'an (legally), meaning the allocation is recognized by divine law; (4) yusawwighu (permits), indicating permissive capacity for action; (5) al-tasarruf (disposition), any legal act of use or transfer; (6) illa li mani' (unless a preventer), clarifying that the right is not absolute but limited by legal impediments. The author notes that this composite definition synthesizes advantages of earlier definitions while correcting their deficiencies. The footnote provides valuable linguistic clarification about the word 'milk' with its different vowelizations, distinguishing it from 'mulk' (sovereignty). It also explains derivations like malik and mulk. This careful definition of fundamental concepts is essential in usul al-iftaa, as fatwas often hinge on precise meanings of terms like 'ownership'.

Key Terms
الملك (al-milk): Ownership: a legally recognized exclusive allocation that permits the owner to dispose of the object unless prevented by a legal bar.
اختصاص حاجز (ikhtisas hajiz): A restrictive allocation that prevents others from interfering.

Key Terms from Source Passage

D) It is the same as ibaha (permission) in all respects.

Key Takeaway
Ownership in Islam is a legally restricted exclusive allocation: it grants the right to dispose unless a halal impediment exists.

Defining Ownership (al-Milk) in Hanafi Jurisprudence
Defining the term 'ownership' linguistically and terminologically

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi technical definition of al-milk and identify its four constitutive components: ikhtiṣāṣ, ḥājiz, idhn al-shāri‘, and māni‘.

Core Idea
Ownership is a Shariah-recognized exclusive right, not a material thing.

Ownership (al-milk) is a foundational concept in Islamic law. Before we can discuss transfer, sale, or inheritance, we must understand what it means to 'own' something. This lesson unpacks the precise Hanafi definition, distinguishing ownership from mere possession and clarifying its scope.

Guided Path
1
Distinguish linguistic from technical meaning
First, separate the dictionary sense of ‘milk’ (possession and control) from the juristic definition. The technical definition adds a crucial element: recognition by the Lawgiver (shar‘an).
The linguistic meaning is broader; the technical meaning is a legal construct.
2
Parse the technical definition into its four components
The definition ‘ikhtiṣāṣ ḥājiz shar‘an yusammiḥu ṣāḥibuhu al-taṣarrufa illā li-māni‘’ contains: (1) ikhtiṣāṣ – exclusive relationship; (2) ḥājiz – barrier against others; (3) idhn al-shāri‘ – permission granted by the Lawgiver; (4) māni‘ – impediment that can restrict the owner.
Each component is necessary; the definition is concise but comprehensive.
3
Analyze the term ‘ḥājiz’ (barrier) and ‘māni‘’ (impediment)
The ḥājiz prevents non-owners from interfering; the māni‘ can limit the owner's own power to dispose. Examples: minority (lack of capacity) and rights of others (co-ownership, mortgage).
Impediments do not negate ownership; they are incidental restrictions.
4
Distinguish ‘milk’ from ‘māl’ (property)
Māl in Hanafi law is restricted to material objects; milk includes rights over usufructs and debts. This distinction is crucial for understanding later rules on sale and transfer.
The Hanafi position differs from the Shafi‘i school, which includes usufructs in māl.

والباب الخامس : نظرية الملكية في الفقه
والمراد بكونه حاجزاً أنه يحجز غير المالك عن الانتفاع والتصرف دون إذن المالك .
وأما المانع الذي يمنع المالك نفسه عن التصرف فيشمل حالتين :
- نقص الأهلية، كما في الصغير إذ يتصرف عنه وليه .
- وحق الغير كما في المال المشترك والمال المرهون، إذ تتقيد فيهما تصرفات الشركاء والراهن رغم ملكيتهم.
فوجود هذا المانع لا ينافي الملك لأنه عارض.
وهذا التعريف يتناول جميع أنواع الملكية الآتي بيانها من ملكية الأعيان، أو المنافع، أو الديون.
ومن هذا التعريف يتضح أن الملك هو عبارة عن علاقة الإنسان بالمال وما في حكمه من المنافع فهو التصوير التشريعي لهذه العلاقة وثمرتها وحدودها.
وبذلك يتبيَّن أن الملكية ليست شيئاً مادياً، وإنما هي حق من الحقوق والحق نوع من الاعتبار الشرعي.
فحيثما أقرّ الشرع هذه العلاقة الاختصاصية بين الإنسان والمال ثبت الملك، وحيثما نفى الشرع هذه العلاقة انتفى الملك.
وذلك بخلاف المال فإنه ذو مفهوم مادي يقع على الموجودات ذات المنافع، حتى إنَّ المال في الاجتهاد الحنفي يختص بالأعيان المادية، فلا يشمل المنافع في نظر الحنفية، كما سنرى في تقسيم الملك (ف ٥/٢٤) .
ونظرية العقد في الشريعة الإسلامية، ف ١٥ - ١٦.
وجاء في كتاب البيوع في فتح القدير (٤٥٦/٥): الملك هو قدرة يثبتها الشرع ابتداء على التصرف :
Chapter Twenty-Three: Definition of Ownership and Explanation of its Causes. Section One: Definition of Ownership. 3/23 - Ownership (al-milk): in the language it means possessing something and having the power to dispose of it independently (Lisan al-Arab). As for the technical terminology of the jurists in Islamic law, ownership is: an exclusive right that is a barrier established by the Lawgiver, permitting its owner to dispose [of the object] except when there is an impediment. This is the most concise and comprehensive definition that we have derived compiled from several definitions mentioned by the jurists (2), each having merit and deficiency, so it combines their merits and rectifies their shortcomings. (1) The word milk has three possible vowelings of the letter mim; however it is used with kasr and fath of the mim for ownership of things, and with damm for sovereignty. One says: malaktu al-shay'a milk-an (with kasr) and malaktu 'ala al-nas amrahum mulk-an (with damm). The adjective from the first is malik, plural mullāk (with damm of mim and tashdid of lam). The adjective from the second is malik (with fath of mim and kasr or sukun of lam), plural mulūk and amlāk (as in al-Misbah). Also al-malakah (with two fathahs) meaning ownership: yā qālu: lahu 'alayhi malakah, i.e., ownership; mā fī malakati-hī shay', i.e., he owns nothing. So the word 'milkiyyah' with a shaddah yā' used by scholars can be said with kasr and sukun of the mim (from milk) or with two fathahs from malakah. (2) The various definitions of ownership were transmitted by the scholar Shaykh Muhammad Abu Zahra in his book 'al-Milkiyyah' from page 333. Section Five: Theory of Ownership in Fiqh. By 'barrier' (hājiz) is meant that it prevents non-owners from benefiting and disposing without the owner's permission. As for the impediment (māni') that prevents the owner himself from disposing, it includes two cases: - deficiency of capacity, as in the minor whose guardian acts for him; - the right of another, as in jointly owned property and mortgaged property, where the dispositions of the partners and the mortgagor are restricted despite their ownership. The existence of this impediment does not negate ownership because it is incidental. This definition covers all types of ownership to be explained later: ownership of physical objects, or usufructs, or debts. From this definition it is clear that ownership is a relationship between a person and property or what is analogous to it from usufructs; it is the legislative representation of this relationship, its fruit, and its limits. Thus it becomes evident that ownership is not a material thing; rather it is one of the rights, and a right is a type of legal consideration. Wherever the Lawgiver acknowledges this exclusive relationship between a person and property, ownership is established; and wherever the Lawgiver negates this relationship, ownership is negated. This is contrary to property (māl), which has a material concept applying to beneficial entities, to the extent that in Hanafi jurisprudence property is restricted to material objects and does not include usufructs in the view of the Hanafis, as will be seen in the division of ownership (section 5/24). And the theory of contract in Islamic law, sections 15-16. In the book of sales in Fath al-Qadir (5/456): ownership is a capacity that the Lawgiver establishes initially for disposal.
Source pages p. 312

Commentary
This passage is a definition of the foundational legal concept of 'ownership' (al-milk) in Hanafi fiqh. The author, Mustafa Ahmad al-Zarqa, provides both a linguistic and technical definition. Linguistically, ownership means possessing something and having independent power over it. Technically, his definition is: 'an exclusive right that is a barrier established by the Lawgiver, permitting its owner to dispose except when there is an impediment.' The term 'hājiz' (barrier) means that ownership prevents anyone other than the owner from using or disposing of the property without permission. The 'māni'' (impediment) refers to legal restrictions on the owner's own right to dispose, such as lack of capacity (e.g., a minor) or the rights of others (e.g., co-ownership or mortgage). These impediments do not negate ownership itself; they are merely temporary or incidental. The definition is comprehensive, covering ownership of physical objects, usufructs, and debts. Al-Zarqa emphasizes that ownership is not a physical thing but a legal right—a relationship between a person and property recognized by the Shariah. He further distinguishes between 'milk' (ownership) and 'māl' (property), noting that in the Hanafi school, 'māl' is confined to tangible objects, whereas usufructs are not considered 'māl' (though they can be owned as rights). This sets the stage for the detailed discussion of ownership types. The passage also cites Fath al-Qadir defining ownership as 'a capacity established by the Lawgiver for disposal.'

Key Terms
الملك (al-milk): اختصاص حاجز شرعاً يسوع صاحبه التصرف إلا لمانع (an exclusive right that is a barrier established by the Lawgiver, permitting its owner to dispose except when there is an impediment)
المال (al-māl): الموجودات ذات المنافع المادية (beneficial material entities)
الاختصاص الحاجز (al-ikhtiṣāṣ al-ḥājiz): العلاقة الخاصة التي تمنع غير المالك من التصرف (the exclusive relationship that prevents non-owners from disposing)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The property is stolen
B) The owner is a minor
C) The property is old
D) The owner is a non-Muslim

Key Takeaway
Ownership in Hanafi fiqh is a Shariah-recognized exclusive right that permits disposal unless a temporary impediment exists; it is a legal relationship, not a material thing.

Ownership in Hanafi Fiqh: Definition, Nature, and Causes
Defines ownership as a shari' relationship and enumerates its recognized causes

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi definition of milk as a shar'i relationship, distinguish it from mal, list the four recognized causes of ownership, and explain why acquisitive prescription is rejected.

Core Idea
Ownership is a non-material legislative capacity to dispose; Hanafis limit mal to tangibles and reject acquisitive prescription.

In this lesson, we explore the foundational concept of ownership (milk) in Hanafi fiqh. Milk is not a physical object but a legally recognized relationship between a person and property that grants the capacity to dispose. We will dissect its definition, contrast it with the narrower concept of mal, and examine the four causes through which ownership is legitimately acquired. Special attention is given to the Hanafi position on prescription (taqadum), which distinguishes between a procedural bar and an acquisitive cause—a nuance critical for muftis addressing long-standing property claims.

Guided Path
1
Deconstruct the definition of milk
Identify the key components from the passage: milk is a relationship between a person and property (including usufructs), it is a legislative construct (shar'i), and it grants capacity to dispose. Note that obstacles like lack of capacity or third-party rights do not negate ownership.
Refer to the legal_definition_dissector block for a structured breakdown.
2
Distinguish milk from mal
The passage emphasizes that mal in Hanafi ijtihad is confined to physical entities (a'yan), while milk includes usufructs and debts. Understand the practical consequence: selling usufructs separately is invalid in the Hanafi school.
The madhhab_dispute_map block elaborates on the differing view of the majority.
3
List the four causes of ownership
Memorize the four causes: (1) acquisition of ownerless things (ihraz al-mubahat), (2) contracts (uqud), (3) inheritance (al-khalafiyya), and (4) offspring of owned property (tawallud min al-mamluk). These are exhaustive in Islamic law.
Rejected causes like internal conquest or enslavement for debt are not part of the Shari'a.
4
Analyze the Hanafi position on prescription
Understand the two types of prescription: barring claim (taqadum mani') and acquisitive (taqadum muksib). The Hanafi school accepts only the former as a procedural obstacle, not as a means to transfer ownership.
This distinguishes Islamic law from Roman and Western legal systems.

Chapter Five: The Theory of Property in Jurisprudence. And by being a barrier, it means it prevents non-owners from benefiting and disposing without the owner's permission. As for the obstacle that prevents the owner himself from disposing, it includes two cases: lack of legal capacity, as in the minor whose guardian disposes for him; and the right of another, as in joint property and mortgaged property, where the dispositions of partners and the mortgagor are restricted despite their ownership. Thus the existence of this obstacle does not negate ownership because it is incidental. This definition covers all types of property to be mentioned: ownership of physical objects, usufructs, or debts. From this definition it becomes clear that ownership is an expression of the relationship between a person and property and what is in its ruling of usufructs; it is the legislative depiction of this relationship, its fruit, and its limits. Thus it is evident that property is not something material, but rather a right, and a right is a type of shar'i consideration. So wherever the law establishes this special relationship between a person and property, ownership is established; and wherever the law negates this relationship, ownership is negated. This is unlike property (mal), which has a material concept applying to existent things with benefits, to the extent that in Hanafi ijtihad, property is confined to physical entities, so it does not include usufructs in the view of the Hanafis, as will be seen in the division of ownership (section 24/5). And from the theory of contract in Islamic law, section 15-16. It is stated in Kitab al-Buyu' in Fath al-Qadir (5/456): Ownership is a capacity that the law initially establishes for disposal. Chapter (23): Definition of ownership and statement of its causes. Second Section: Causes of Ownership. 23/4: Indeed, the causes of ownership recognized by Islamic legislation are limited to four: 1) Acquisition of ownerless things, 2) Contracts, 3) Inheritance (al-khalafiyya), 4) Offspring of owned property. In the pre-Islamic Arab Jahiliyya and among other nations there were causes for ownership rejected by Islamic legislation: some it denounced and considered among the most severe crimes, and some it regarded as forbidden (maharem). The first type includes internal conquest among the Arab tribes and others, and the enslavement of a debtor unable to pay among the Romans and in Arab Jahiliyya. The second type includes acquisitive prescription (taqadum muksib) that appeared in later Roman legislation and was inherited by European laws and still remains. Islamic jurisprudence accepted the rule of prescription not as an acquisitive cause for ownership, but rather as a bar to hearing a claim for a right over which time has passed. (Footnote 1): Prescription, also called passage of time, is the expiration of a specified period (e.g., fifteen years, or more or less) over a right held by a person or over a physical object belonging to another in his possession without the owner claiming it while able to do so. This prescription is either considered a bar to hearing the claim for the right before courts, while considering the ownership or right in reality remaining as before—this is prescriptive bar (taqadum mani'), because it prevents the claim; or it is considered a cause for ownership for the possessor and a complete termination of the neglected owner's right—this is acquisitive prescription (taqadum muksib), because it causes a person to acquire ownership of something originally belonging to another.
Source pages p. 313

Commentary
This passage defines the nature of ownership (milk) in Islamic law from a Hanafi perspective and then lists its recognized causes. The author begins by explaining that ownership acts as a barrier preventing non-owners from using or disposing of property without permission. However, obstacles that restrict the owner himself (such as lack of capacity or third-party rights) do not negate ownership; they are merely incidental. The definition of ownership is then given: it is the legislative depiction of the relationship between a person and property (including usufructs). It is not a physical entity but a shar'i right. The passage emphasizes that mal (property) in Hanafi usage is limited to tangible assets, a distinct view from some other schools. The author then transitions to the causes of ownership, listing four recognized sources: acquisition of ownerless things, contracts, inheritance (al-khalafiyya), and offspring of owned property. Other systems (pre-Islamic and Roman) had causes like internal conquest or enslavement for debt, which Islam rejects. The passage particularly discusses prescription (taqadum): Islamic law does not accept it as an acquisitive cause but only as a procedural bar to hearing claims after time has passed. A footnote clarifies the two types of prescription — barring the claim vs. transferring ownership — and confirms that Hanafi fiqh adopts only the former.

Key Terms
الملك (al-milk): علاقة الإنسان بالمال وما في حكمه من المنافع، وهي صورة تشريعية غير مادية (the relationship between a person and property/usufructs, a non-physical legislative construct)
المانع (al-mani'): عارض يمنع المالك من التصرف، مثل نقص الأهلية أو حق الغير، ولا ينافي أصل الملك (an incidental obstacle that prevents the owner from disposing, such as lack of capacity or third-party rights, without negating ownership itself)
التقادم (al-taqadum): انقضاء زمن معين على حق دون مطالبة صاحبه مع قدرته، وهو نوعان: مانع (يمنع سماع الدعوى فقط) ومكسب (ينقل الملكية) (the expiration of a specified period on a right without the owner claiming it while able, two types: barring claim vs. acquisitive)

Key Takeaway
Ownership in Hanafi fiqh is a legislative capacity to dispose, distinct from material property, and prescription only bars claims, never transfers ownership.

Acquisition of Permissible Things (Iḥrāz al-Mubāḥāt)
Explains two conditions for owning permissible things.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the two conditions for owning permissible things and apply them to simple scenarios.

Core Idea
Ownership of permissible things requires both prior non-acquisition and intention to own.

This passage from Mustafa al-Zarqa's introduction outlines the two conditions that must be met for a person to acquire ownership of a permissible (mubāḥ) object. Understanding these conditions is essential for distinguishing between mere possession and valid ownership in Hanafi jurisprudence.

Guided Path
1
Define mubāḥ and iḥrāz
Identify what qualifies as permissible (mubāḥ) and the act of acquisition (iḥrāz). Mubāḥ is wealth not in protected ownership and without legal impediment. Iḥrāz is taking possession with intention to own.
Examples: water, pasture, wild game.
2
State the first condition: no prior acquisition
If someone has already acquired a permissible thing, it leaves the state of permissibility and becomes owned. No one else may take it.
Maxim: 'Whoever precedes to a permissible thing owns it.'
3
State the second condition: intention to own
Physical possession alone does not create ownership if the person did not intend to own. Example: hunter's net spread for drying vs. hunting.
Maxim: 'Matters are judged by their intentions.'
4
Apply the maxims to examples
Work through the rainwater, firewood, and hunter net examples to see how the two conditions operate together.
The intention condition qualifies the precedence condition.

Chapter Five: The Theory of Ownership in Jurisprudence. It is an organizational measure for the judiciary, to avoid the difficulties of proof after lapse of time, and due to doubt about a right that has been dormant without claim. As for the original right, if it remains in the debtor's liability, it must be fulfilled in the religious judgment. Therefore, if the opponent admits the right, the statute of limitations collapses due to the removal of doubt, and the claim is heard. (Ref. 28/83) This is an effect of the distinction in Islamic law between judicial judgment and religious duty, as previously indicated at the beginning of the introduction (3/2). We will now explain the four causes of ownership recognized by Islamic law: 23/5 - First: Acquisition of permissible things (Iḥrāz al-Mubāḥāt): Mubāḥ (permissible) is wealth that has not entered into protected ownership and for which no legal impediment to ownership exists, such as water in its sources, pasture in its growth, trees in unowned wilderness, land game and sea game, etc. So every person may take possession of as much of these permissible things as they can. Whatever they take possession of with the intention of ownership, they own it exclusively. This taking with intention of ownership is called iḥrāz. Ownership of permissible things through iḥrāz depends on two conditions: First: That no previous person has already acquired it. If a person collects rainwater in a container and leaves it, no one else may take it, because it has left the state of permissibility due to the first acquirer's possession and has become owned by him. This is based on the maxim: 'Whoever precedes to a permissible thing owns it.' Similarly, if a person collects firewood from the wilderness and leaves it, no one else may take it, and so on. Second: Intention of ownership. If a permissible thing comes into a person's possession without his intention to own it, he does not own it. Thus, if a hunter spreads his net and game falls into it: if he spread it for drying, he does not own it, and anyone who sees it may take and own it, for the taker is considered the acquirer, not the owner of the net. But if he spread it for hunting, he is the acquirer of whatever falls into it, and he owns it upon its fall, and no one else may take it. The same applies to one who places a vessel and rainwater collects in it, or builds a tower in the wilderness and pigeons nest in it such that they can be taken without hunting. All of this: if he prepared it for this purpose, he owns what enters it; otherwise, not. This is according to the maxim: 'Matters are judged by their intentions.' (M/3) Acquisition of permissible things, which is a legitimate cause of ownership, includes war spoils, because the wealth of combatants is considered permissible, as their ownership is not protected. Islamic law only prohibits internal aggression; as for legitimate war against external enemies, it is permissible and may even be obligatory to preserve the entity and repel aggression, and it is a cause of ownership in international custom and the laws of nations.
Source pages p. 315

Commentary
This passage is from a contemporary introduction to Islamic jurisprudence, explaining the theory of ownership. It begins by distinguishing between judicial and religious judgments, then introduces the four causes of ownership. The first cause, iḥrāz al-mubāḥāt (acquisition of permissible things), is detailed. The author defines mubāḥ as wealth not in protected ownership and without legal impediment, giving examples like water, pasture, wild trees, and game. The two conditions for ownership through iḥrāz are: (1) no prior acquisition by another, and (2) intention to own. Examples illustrate these: collecting rainwater and leaving it prevents others from taking it, based on the maxim 'Whoever precedes to a permissible thing owns it.' The hunter's net example shows the role of intention: if spread for hunting, the hunter owns the catch; if for drying, anyone can take it. The maxim 'Matters are judged by their intentions' governs this. War spoils are included as permissible because the enemy's property lacks protected ownership. The passage reflects the Hanafi approach that ownership can arise through physical control with intent.

Key Terms
المباح (al-mubāḥ): Wealth that has not entered into protected ownership and no legal impediment exists to owning it.
الإحراز (al-iḥrāz): Taking possession of a permissible thing with the intention of ownership.
قصد التملك (qaṣd al-tamalluk): The intention to take ownership when acquiring a permissible thing.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
Ownership of permissible things requires both that no one else has already acquired it and that the acquirer intends to own it.

The Hanafi Definition of Contract (al-‘Aqd)
Defines contract and its role in ownership transfer from a Hanafi perspective.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi legal definition of a contract (al-‘aqd) and explain how it differs from the modern legal definition.

Core Idea
In Hanafi fiqh, a contract is the linking of an offer and acceptance in a legitimate manner, not merely an agreement.

This lesson introduces the concept of contract as a cause of ownership. The Hanafi jurists defined it precisely as a linkage of offer and acceptance with a legitimate effect. Understanding this definition is crucial because it determines what transactions are recognized as valid contracts in Islamic law. We will compare this with the modern legal definition and see examples.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic definition
Locate the definition in the passage: 'ارتباط إيجاب بقبول على وجه مشروع يظهر أثره في محله'. Identify its key components: offer, acceptance, legitimacy, effect.
2
Break down the components
Analyze each component: offer (ījāb) is the first declaration, acceptance (qabūl) is the second, 'legitimate manner' (ala wajh mashru‘) means accordance with Shari‘ah, and 'shows its effect on its subject' means the contract produces legal consequences.
The Hanafi definition requires both offer and acceptance to be in the same session.
3
Compare with modern definition
The modern definition focuses on agreement between two or more persons to create, transfer, or terminate rights. Note the differences: Hanafi definition emphasizes the mechanism (offer and acceptance) while modern definition focuses on the outcome.
4
Apply to examples
Consider the examples: sale (transfer ownership), guarantee (create a right), rescission (terminate a contract). Verify that each fits both definitions.
5
Implications for fatwa
A mufti uses this definition to determine if a transaction is a valid contract. If any component is missing (e.g., no proper acceptance), the contract is not valid.
This definition is the foundation for analyzing all contracts in Hanafi fiqh.

23/6 - Second: Contracts: Contracts is the plural of contract, and in the terminology of our jurists it is: the linking of an offer and acceptance in a legitimate manner that shows its effect on its subject. The meaning of offer and explanation of this definition will come in the theory of contracts (3/27). In legal terminology it is: an agreement of two or more persons to create a right, transfer it, or terminate it, such as sale, lease, gift, guarantee, rescission, and the like. For whoever guarantees, he creates a right for the creditor to claim. Whoever sells something, he transfers ownership of the sold item to the buyer. Whoever mutually rescinds a contract between them, they terminate its effect; and so on.
Source pages p. 316

Commentary
This passage introduces contracts as the second cause of ownership. The author provides the Hanafi juristic definition of a contract ('aqd) as the linkage of an offer (ījāb) and acceptance (qabūl) in a legitimate manner, which produces a legal effect on its subject. He contrasts this with the modern legal definition, which focuses on agreement between two or more persons to create, transfer, or terminate rights. Examples illustrate the three functions: guarantee (creating a right), sale (transferring ownership), and rescission (terminating a contract). The passage sets the stage for a detailed discussion of contract theory later in the work.

Key Terms
العقد (al-‘aqd): The linking of an offer and acceptance in a legitimate manner, showing its effect on its subject.
الإيجاب (al-ījāb): The first declaration indicating willingness to enter a contract.
القبول (al-qabūl): The second declaration agreeing to the offer.

In Hanafi fiqh, a contract is technically defined as the linking of an offer and acceptance in a legitimate manner, producing legal effects on its subject.

Legal Philosophy and Theory
