Causes of Ownership and Compulsory Contracts
Legal theory of ownership causes and exceptions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the two essential conditions for ownership through contracts and state the two exceptions recognized in Hanafi fiqh where these conditions are set aside for public interest.

Core Idea
Contracts require capacity and free will, but Hanafi fiqh allows compulsory contracts and acquisitions when public interest demands.

This lesson explores the foundational theory of property in Hanafi jurisprudence. The author establishes that contracts are the primary means of acquiring ownership, but they are conditional upon capacity and free will. However, necessity and public welfare create exceptions: compulsory contracts (e.g., judicial sale of a debtor's property) and compulsory acquisition (e.g., pre-emption and expropriation for mosques). These exceptions demonstrate the flexibility of the Hanafi legal system in balancing individual rights with societal needs.

Guided Path
1
State the general rule
Contracts are the main cause of ownership; they require capacity (ahliyya) and free will (ikhtiyar). Without these, the contract is invalid.
This is the default position; any deviation is an exception.
2
Introduce the first exception: compulsory contracts
The judicial authority can execute contracts on behalf of a reluctant obligor, like selling a debtor's property to repay debt.
This exception is explicitly mentioned for public interest.
3
Introduce the second exception: compulsory acquisition
This includes pre-emption (shuf'a) and expropriation for public interest, e.g., expanding a mosque.
Pre-emption applies to co-owners, khalit, and adjacent neighbor; expropriation for public utilities.
4
Analyze the legal rationale
Both exceptions are justified by public interest (maslaha), overriding the general conditions.
The text explicitly says the exceptions are 'min muqtadayat al-maslaha al-amma'.

الباب الخامس: نظرية الملكية في الفقه
والعقود كلها تصرُّفات قولية يُشترط فيها، لتكون سبباً للملكية شرائط، من أهمها: الأهلية والاختيار. فالإكراه على العقد يسلبه حكمه ونقص الأهلية، كما في عقود الصغار، يؤثر في نتائجها. وسنرى تفصيل ذلك في نظرية العقود، ونظرية الأهلية.
والعقود، بصورة عامة هي من أعظم أسباب الملكية، وأعمها وقوعاً، وأهمها شأناً في الاعتبارات المدنية والوزن القانوني، لأن بها يتجلى الإدراك والنشاط الإنساني في المضمارين الاقتصادي والحقوقي بخلاف الأسباب الثلاثة الأخرى من أسباب الملك فإنها قليلة الوقوع بالنسبة إلى العقود التي تستلزمها حاجات الإنسان في معظم حركاته وحياته اليومية .
ويدخل في العقود من حيث سببية الملكية حالتان هما استثناء من شريطتي الأهلية والاختيار. وهذا الاستثناء هو من مقتضيات المصلحة العامة، وهما:
الأولى - العقود الجبرية التي تقوم بإجرائها السلطة القضائية مباشرة وصراحة بالنيابة عمن تجب عليهم إذا امتنعوا عن إجرائها. وذلك كبيع مال المدين جَبْراً عليه لأجل وفاء الدين من قبل القاضي في الترتيب الفقهي قديماً، ومن قبل دوائر التنفيذ اليوم وهي الفروع التنفيذية للقضاء ومحاكمه في الترتيبات القانونية الحديثة .
ومثل ذلك أيضاً بيع الحاكم للأموال المحتكرة على محتكريها عندما يضر بالناس احتكارها (ر: الدر المختار كتاب الحظر والإباحة. 5/259).
ففي هذه الحالة تستند الملكية إلى عقد بيع صريح أجرته السلطة القضائية بالنيابة الجبرية عن المالك .
الثانية - حالة التملك الجبري المسمى في الاصطلاح القانوني: نزع الملكية الجبري.
ولهذا التملك الجبري في الأحكام الشرعية والقانونية صورتان، بحسب صاحب المصلحة فيه:
أ - فالصورة الأولى الشفعة، وهي حق ممنوح شرعاً لشخص أن يتملك العقار جَبْراً على مشتريه بما قام عليه من الثمن والتكليف، ويسمى صاحب هذا الحق: شفيعاً. ويثبت حق الشفعة شرعاً للشريك والخليط والجار الملاصق.
ب - والصورة الثانية هي الاستملاك لأجل المصالح العامة، فقد أجاز الشرع الإسلامي استملاك الأرض المجاورة للمسجد جبراً على أصحابها إذا امتنعوا عن بيعها وضاق المسجد بأهله واحتاج إليها؛ كما أجازوا مثل ذلك (1).
(1) الشفعة (بضم فسكون) في الاصطلاح الشرعي مشتقة من لفظ «الشفع» (بفتح فسكون) بمعنى الضم لغة، لأن الشفيع يضم مال غيره إلى ماله .
(2) الشريك من كان مشتركاً في ملكية عين العقار. والخليط هو المشارك في الحقوق العقارية (ر: المجلة 954)، أي من كان له في عقار غيره حق عيني بارتفاق جارٍ على ذلك العقار كحق المرور أو التسييل فيه، وحق تحميل الجذوع على جدرانه إلخ... سواء أكان مستقلاً بهذا الحق العيني، أو كان الحق مشتركاً بينه وبين غيره كما لو كان العقارين ممر خاص، أو شرب مشترك بينهما، فصاحب كل منهما خليط للآخر. والجار الملاصق هو من يتصل عقاره بعقار غيره من إحدى الجهات اتصالاً مباشراً دون فاصل. وقد جاء قانون الملكية العقارية لدينا ذو الرقم 3339 بإلغاء شفعة الجار كما سلفت الإشارة إليه آخر المقدمة (ر ف 2/22 خامساً). ثم جاء القانون المدني الجديد سنة 1949م بإلغاء حق الشفعة مطلقاً.
Fifth Chapter: Theory of Property in Jurisprudence. Contracts are all verbal dispositions; for them to be a cause of ownership, conditions are required, the most important of which are: capacity and free will. Coercion in a contract nullifies its ruling, and lack of capacity, as in the contracts of minors, affects its results. We will see details of that in the theory of contracts and the theory of capacity. Contracts, in general, are among the greatest causes of ownership, the most widespread in occurrence, and the most important in status in civil considerations and legal weight, because through them human perception and activity manifest in the economic and legal fields, unlike the other three causes of ownership, for they are few in occurrence relative to contracts which human needs necessitate in most of their movements and daily life. Included in contracts with respect to causation of ownership are two cases that are exceptions to the two conditions of capacity and free will. This exception is a requirement of public interest. They are: First - compulsory contracts that are carried out by the judicial authority directly and explicitly on behalf of those obligated when they refuse to carry them out. That is like the sale of a debtor's property compulsorily against him for the purpose of paying debt by the judge in the classical fiqh arrangement, and by execution departments today, which are the executive branches of the judiciary and its courts in modern legal arrangements. Similar to that is the ruler's sale of hoarded goods to their hoarders when their hoarding harms people (see: al-Durr al-Mukhtar, chapter of al-Hazr wa al-Ibaha, 5/259). In this case, ownership is based on an explicit sale contract conducted by the judicial authority by compulsory representation of the owner. Second - the case of compulsory acquisition called in legal terminology: compulsory expropriation. This compulsory acquisition in sharia and legal rulings has two forms, according to the owner of the interest: A - The first form: pre-emption (shuf'a), which is a right granted legally to a person to acquire real property compulsorily from its buyer at the price and cost paid, and the holder of this right is called the pre-emptor (shafi'). The right of pre-emption is established legally for the co-owner, the participant in rights (khalit), and the adjacent neighbor. B - The second form is expropriation for public interest. Islamic law has permitted the expropriation of land adjacent to the mosque compulsorily from its owners if they refuse to sell it and the mosque becomes too narrow for its people and needs it; and they permitted similar things. (1) Pre-emption (shuf'a, sukun on fa) in legal terminology is derived from the word al-shaf' (fatha on shin, sukun on fa) meaning to join linguistically, because the pre-emptor joins another's property to his own. (2) The co-owner is one who is a partner in ownership of the same real property. The khalit is one who participates in real rights (see al-Majalla 954), i.e., one who has a real right in another's property by way of an easement on that property, such as the right of passage or drainage, the right to place beams on walls, etc., whether he is independent in this real right or the right is shared between him and others, such as if the two properties share a private passage or a common watering place, so each owner is a khalit for the other. The adjacent neighbor is one whose property is directly connected to another's property from one side without a divider. Our real property law no. 3339 abolished the pre-emption of the neighbor as previously indicated at the end of the introduction (see sect. 2/22 fifth). Then the new civil code of 1949 abolished the right of pre-emption absolutely.
Source pages p. 317

Commentary
This passage opens the fifth chapter of the book, which deals with the theory of property in Islamic jurisprudence. The author begins by establishing that contracts are the primary means of acquiring ownership, but they require two conditions: legal capacity (ahliyya) and free will (ikhtiyar). Coercion or lack of capacity (e.g., minors) invalidates the contract's effect. However, the author then introduces two exceptions to these conditions, justified by public interest (maslaha). The first exception is compulsory contracts executed by the judicial authority when a person refuses to fulfill an obligation, such as the sale of a debtor's property to pay debts, or the sale of hoarded goods to prevent harm to the public. The second exception is compulsory transfer of ownership (tamlik jabri), which includes two forms: pre-emption (shuf'a) and expropriation for public interest. Pre-emption allows a co-owner, rights-holder (khalit), or adjacent neighbor to forcibly acquire property from a buyer at the same price. Expropriation for public interest is illustrated by the example of expanding a mosque by acquiring adjacent land compulsorily when the owner refuses to sell. The author also provides linguistic and legal definitions of key terms and mentions later legal changes abolishing pre-emption for neighbors and ultimately the entire right. This passage sets the foundational legal philosophy that contracts are central to ownership but that necessity and public welfare can override normal conditions.

Key Terms
الملكية (al-milkiyyah): Ownership, the legal relationship between a person and a thing that gives the person exclusive rights of disposition within the limits of law.
العقود الجبرية (al-uqud al-jabriyyah): Compulsory contracts: contracts executed by judicial authority on behalf of a person who is obligated but refuses to perform, such as the sale of a debtor's property by the judge.
الشفعة (al-shuf‘a): Pre-emption: the right of a co-owner, participant in rights (khalit), or adjacent neighbor to forcibly acquire real property from its buyer at the same price and costs.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) Capacity and free will
B) Writing and witnesses
C) Offer and acceptance
D) Payment and delivery

Key Takeaway
Hanafi fiqh permits compulsory contracts and compulsory acquisition as exceptions to the general requirement of free will and capacity, based on public interest.

Shuf'a: The Right of Pre-emption in Hanafi Fiqh
Defines shuf'a and its categories with juristic basis

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the three categories of shuf'a claimants according to Hanafi fiqh and explain why the pre-emptor's acquisition is treated as an implied sale contract.

Core Idea
Shuf'a is a compulsory sale contract: the pre-emptor takes the property as if buying from the seller or buyer depending on delivery.

This passage introduces shuf'a as a form of compulsory ownership where a co-owner, easement holder, or adjoining neighbor can forcibly take over a sold property. The Hanafi school bases this right on the concept of an implied contract—the pre-emptor steps into the shoes of a purchaser.

Guided Path
1
Define shuf'a
Identify the term and its legal meaning: a right to compulsorily purchase real estate from a buyer.
2
List the three claimants
State the three categories: shariq (co-owner), khalit (easement holder), and jar mulasiq (adjoining neighbor).
Hanafi only; Shafi'i restricts to partners.
3
Understand the timing rule
Note that taking before delivery is like buying from the seller; after delivery is like buying from the purchaser.
This affects who the pre-emptor pays.
4
Identify the juristic basis
Recognize that shuf'a is considered a compulsory contract (aqd jabri) and ownership is based on an implied sale.
The court may enforce the sale.
5
Compare with Shafi'i
Note the cross-madhhab difference: Shafi'i only allows shuf'a for partners.
This is a legitimate ikhtilaf.

الفصل (۲۳) تعريف الملك وبيان أسبابه
ففي هذه الحالة تستند الملكية إلى عقد بيع صريح أجرته السلطة القضائية بالنيابة الجبرية عن المالك .
الثانية - حالة التملك الجبري المسمى في الاصطلاح القانوني : نزع الملكية الجبري.
ولهذا التملك الجبري في الأحكام الشرعية والقانونية صورتان، بحسب صاحب المصلحة فيه :
أ ـ فالصورة الأولى الشفعة، حق ممنوح شرعاً لشخص أن يتملك العقار جَبْراً على مشتريه بما قام عليه من الثمن والتكليف، ويسمى صاحب هذا الحق: شفيعاً» (۱).
ويثبت حق الشفعة شرعاً للشريك والخليط والجار الملاصق (٢).
ب - والصورة الثانية هي الاستملاك لأجل المصالح العامة، فقد أجاز الشرع الإسلامي استملاك الأرض المجاورة للمسجد جبراً على أصحابها إذا امتنعوا عن بيعها وضاق المسجد بأهله واحتاج إليها؛ كما أجازوا مثل ذلك لأجل توسيع الطريق إذا دَعَت حاجة الناس إلى توسيعه، وذلك بالقيمة التي يساويها العقار المستملك .
حتى لقد نص الفقهاء على أنه يجوز أن يُؤخَذَ لتوسيع الطريق جانب من المسجد عند الحاجة ( ر ) رد المحتار ٣٨٣/٣ - ٣٨٤) .
وقانون الاستملاك للنفع العام لدينا يسوع نزع الملكية الجبري من كل عقار تقرّر السلطة الإدارية العليا وجود النفع العام في استملاكه لمصلحة من المصالح العامة، كمدرسة أو مستشفى أو حديقة، وذلك بقيمته التي تقدرها له لجنة خبراء . فجميع هذه الأحوال تستند الملكية فيها فقهياً إلى سبب العقد :
جبري
فملكية الشفيع للعقار المقضي له بها في صورة الشفعة، وملكية الجهة المستملك لحسابها في صورة الاستملاك، تعتبر مستندة إلى عقد شراء يقدر وجوده بطريق الاقتضاء والاستلزام) ، فيفترض حصول البيع والشراء بين الطرفين بإرادة السلطة الملزمة كنتيجة لقضاء القاضي بتمليك الشفيع، أو لعملية الاستملاك الجاري من الجهة صاحبة الحق فيه، وفقاً للأساليب والمراسم المشروعة.
هذا ما يقتضيه التخريج الفقهي لحالات التملك الجبري.
فيتّضح بناء على ما تقدّم أن العقد في سببية الملكية يكون تارة رضائياً، وتارة جبرياً، وأن العقد الجبري يكون تارة صريحاً، وتارة مقدراً اقتضاء.
(1) يقول الفقهاء أن أخذ العقار بالشفعة قبل تسليمه إلى المشتري هو كشراء من البائع وأخذه بعد التسليم كشراء من المشتري ( ر ) الدرر شرح الغرر كتاب الشفعة ٢/
٣٤٠
.(۲۱۱)
Chapter (23): Definition of Ownership and Explanation of Its Causes. In this case, ownership is based on an explicit sale contract executed by the judicial authority as a compulsory proxy for the owner. The second: the case of compulsory acquisition, called in legal terminology: expropriation (naz' al-milkiyyah al-jabri). This compulsory ownership in Islamic and legal rulings has two forms, according to the person who has the interest in it: a) The first form is shuf'a (pre-emption), a right granted by Sharia to a person to compulsorily own real estate against its purchaser for the price and costs borne by the purchaser, and the holder of this right is called 'shafi'' (pre-emptor). The right of shuf'a is established by Sharia for the co-owner, the participant in easements (khalit), and the adjoining neighbor. b) The second form is acquisition for public interests: Islamic law has permitted the compulsory acquisition of land adjacent to a mosque from its owners if they refuse to sell it and the mosque becomes too crowded for its people and needs it; similarly, they permitted such for widening a road if people's need requires widening it, for the value that the acquired property is worth. Indeed, the jurists have stated that it is permissible to take part of the mosque for road widening when necessary (see Radd al-Muhtar 3/383-384). Our law of acquisition for public benefit permits compulsory expropriation of any real estate determined by the highest administrative authority to have public benefit in its acquisition for a public interest, such as a school, hospital, or park, for its value estimated by a committee of experts. In all these cases, ownership is legally based on the cause of the contract: compulsory. For the ownership of the pre-emptor of the property adjudicated to him in the case of shuf'a, and the ownership of the acquiring entity for its account in the case of expropriation, they are considered based on a purchase contract whose existence is assumed by way of necessity and implication (bi tariq al-iqtida' wa al-istilzam). Thus, it is presumed that sale and purchase occur between the two parties through the binding authority's will as a result of the judge's ruling granting ownership to the pre-emptor, or of the acquisition process carried out by the entity entitled to it, according to the prescribed methods and procedures. This is what the juristic derivation (takhrij fiqhi) of the cases of compulsory ownership requires. It becomes clear based on the foregoing that the contract in causing ownership is sometimes consensual and sometimes compulsory, and the compulsory contract is sometimes explicit and sometimes implied by necessity (muqaddaran iqtida'). (1) The jurists say that taking the property by shuf'a before delivery to the purchaser is like a purchase from the seller, and taking it after delivery is like a purchase from the purchaser (see al-Durar Sharh al-Ghurar, Kitab al-Shuf'a, 2/340).
Source pages p. 318

Commentary
This passage is a legal philosophy discussion on the basis of ownership in cases where property is acquired without the voluntary consent of the original owner. The author, Mustafa Ahmad al-Zarqa', explains that Islamic jurisprudence recognizes two forms of compulsory acquisition: shuf'a (pre-emption) and expropriation for public interest. In shuf'a, a partner, participant in easements, or adjoining neighbor has the right to take over a property that has been sold to a third party by paying the sale price. In expropriation, the state may take property for public needs like mosque expansion or road widening, paying fair compensation. The key juristic point is that in both cases, ownership is held to be based on a contract—either explicit (as when a court executes a sale on behalf of an unwilling seller) or implied by necessity (muqaddar iqtida'). The Hanafi jurists consider the pre-emptor's acquisition before delivery as a purchase from the original seller, and after delivery as a purchase from the buyer. This analysis shows that the cause of ownership (sabab al-milkiyyah) can be a consensual or compulsory contract, and compulsory contracts can be explicit or implicit. The author cites Radd al-Muhtar and al-Durar as references, indicating the Hanafi authority of these positions.

Key Terms
الشفعة (al-shuf‘a): A legal right granted to a co-owner, participant in easements, or adjoining neighbor to compulsorily acquire a property sold to a third party by paying its price.
الخليط (al-khalīṭ): A person who has a real right in another's property, such as a right of passage or watercourse, either exclusively or shared.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Hanafi fiqh, which of the following does NOT have the right of shuf'a?
A) Co-owner
B) Lessee
C) Khalit
D) Adjoining neighbor

Key Takeaway
Shuf'a is a compulsory right of ownership based on an implied sale, granted to co-owners, easement holders, and neighbors in Hanafi law.

Expropriation (Naz' al-Milkiyyah) and Compulsory Contracts in Hanafi Fiqh
Explains expropriation for public interest and classification of compulsory contracts

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between explicit and implied compulsory contracts and explain how expropriation for public benefit is justified in Hanafi jurisprudence.

Core Idea
Expropriation for mosques, roads, and schools is a compulsory contract implied by law—ownership transfers based on fair compensation.

This passage extends the concept of compulsory ownership to public interest takings. The Hanafi school permits expropriation of property for mosque expansion, road widening, and other public needs, with compensation. Jurists treat these as implied sale contracts, where the state acts as a compulsory buyer.

Guided Path
1
Identify the forms of compulsory acquisition
List shuf'a and expropriation as the two forms.
2
Understand the examples of expropriation
Recognize the scenarios: mosque expansion, road widening, public projects like schools and hospitals.
3
Analyze the contract classification
Note that contracts can be consensual or compulsory, and compulsory contracts can be explicit (court-ordered sale) or implied (necessity-based).
4
Apply the concept to modern takings
Modern expropriation laws mirror these classical rulings, treating the acquisition as an implied sale for fair value.

حتى لقد نص الفقهاء على أنه يجوز أن يُؤخَذَ لتوسيع الطريق جانب من المسجد عند الحاجة ( ر ) رد المحتار ٣٨٣/٣ - ٣٨٤) .
وقانون الاستملاك للنفع العام لدينا يسوع نزع الملكية الجبري من كل عقار تقرّر السلطة الإدارية العليا وجود النفع العام في استملاكه لمصلحة من المصالح العامة، كمدرسة أو مستشفى أو حديقة، وذلك بقيمته التي تقدرها له لجنة خبراء . فجميع هذه الأحوال تستند الملكية فيها فقهياً إلى سبب العقد :

هذا ما يقتضيه التخريج الفقهي لحالات التملك الجبري.

(1) يقول الفقهاء أن أخذ العقار بالشفعة قبل تسليمه إلى المشتري هو كشراء من البائع وأخذه بعد التسليم كشراء من المشتري ( ر ) الدرر شرح الغرر كتاب الشفعة ٢/
٣٤٠
.(۲۱۱)
Chapter (23): Definition of Ownership and Explanation of Its Causes. In this case, ownership is based on an explicit sale contract executed by the judicial authority as a compulsory proxy for the owner. The second: the case of compulsory acquisition, called in legal terminology: expropriation (naz' al-milkiyyah al-jabri). This compulsory ownership in Islamic and legal rulings has two forms, according to the person who has the interest in it: a) The first form is shuf'a (pre-emption), a right granted by Sharia to a person to compulsorily own real estate against its purchaser for the price and costs borne by the purchaser, and the holder of this right is called 'shafi'' (pre-emptor). The right of shuf'a is established by Sharia for the co-owner, the participant in easements (khalit), and the adjoining neighbor. b) The second form is acquisition for public interests: Islamic law has permitted the compulsory acquisition of land adjacent to a mosque from its owners if they refuse to sell it and the mosque becomes too crowded for its people and needs it; similarly, they permitted such for widening a road if people's need requires widening it, for the value that the acquired property is worth. Indeed, the jurists have stated that it is permissible to take part of the mosque for road widening when necessary (see Radd al-Muhtar 3/383-384). Our law of acquisition for public benefit permits compulsory expropriation of any real estate determined by the highest administrative authority to have public benefit in its acquisition for a public interest, such as a school, hospital, or park, for its value estimated by a committee of experts. In all these cases, ownership is legally based on the cause of the contract: compulsory. For the ownership of the pre-emptor of the property adjudicated to him in the case of shuf'a, and the ownership of the acquiring entity for its account in the case of expropriation, they are considered based on a purchase contract whose existence is assumed by way of necessity and implication (bi tariq al-iqtida' wa al-istilzam). Thus, it is presumed that sale and purchase occur between the two parties through the binding authority's will as a result of the judge's ruling granting ownership to the pre-emptor, or of the acquisition process carried out by the entity entitled to it, according to the prescribed methods and procedures. This is what the juristic derivation (takhrij fiqhi) of the cases of compulsory ownership requires. It becomes clear based on the foregoing that the contract in causing ownership is sometimes consensual and sometimes compulsory, and the compulsory contract is sometimes explicit and sometimes implied by necessity (muqaddaran iqtida'). (1) The jurists say that taking the property by shuf'a before delivery to the purchaser is like a purchase from the seller, and taking it after delivery is like a purchase from the purchaser (see al-Durar Sharh al-Ghurar, Kitab al-Shuf'a, 2/340).
Source pages p. 318

Commentary
This passage is a legal philosophy discussion on the basis of ownership in cases where property is acquired without the voluntary consent of the original owner. The author, Mustafa Ahmad al-Zarqa', explains that Islamic jurisprudence recognizes two forms of compulsory acquisition: shuf'a (pre-emption) and expropriation for public interest. In shuf'a, a partner, participant in easements, or adjoining neighbor has the right to take over a property that has been sold to a third party by paying the sale price. In expropriation, the state may take property for public needs like mosque expansion or road widening, paying fair compensation. The key juristic point is that in both cases, ownership is held to be based on a contract—either explicit (as when a court executes a sale on behalf of an unwilling seller) or implied by necessity (muqaddar iqtida'). The Hanafi jurists consider the pre-emptor's acquisition before delivery as a purchase from the original seller, and after delivery as a purchase from the buyer. This analysis shows that the cause of ownership (sabab al-milkiyyah) can be a consensual or compulsory contract, and compulsory contracts can be explicit or implicit. The author cites Radd al-Muhtar and al-Durar as references, indicating the Hanafi authority of these positions.

Key Terms
العقد الجبري (al-‘aqd al-jabrī): A contract that is imposed by legal authority against the will of one party, such as compulsory sale by court or expropriation.
التملك الجبري (al-tamalluk al-jabrī): Acquiring ownership of property without the voluntary consent of the previous owner, justified by law or necessity.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following is NOT a valid example of expropriation in Hanafi fiqh?
A) Taking land for mosque expansion
B) Taking land for a private park
C) Taking land for road widening
D) Taking part of a mosque for road widening

Key Takeaway
Expropriation for public benefit is a compulsory sale implied by necessity, recognized by Hanafi jurists.

Forced Contracts as Causes of Ownership in Hanafi Fiqh
Explaining compulsory acquisition and forced contracts

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the Hanafi concept of a forced contract (al-ʿaqd al-jabrī) and apply it to cases of compulsory acquisition and preemption, distinguishing explicit from implied forms.

Core Idea
A forced contract, though lacking mutual consent, is a valid cause of ownership when legally presumed as a sale.

In this lesson, we explore how Hanafi fiqh handles ownership transfers that occur without the owner's voluntary consent. The passage introduces two key categories: consensual contracts and forced contracts. You will learn how compulsory acquisition for public projects and preemption (shufʿa) are legally constructed as sales, ensuring both public benefit and fair compensation. The distinction between explicit and implied forced contracts is central to understanding these rulings.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two main categories of contracts causing ownership
Recognize from the passage that contracts causing ownership are either consensual (ridāʾī) or compulsory (jabrī). This is the foundational classification.
Consensual contracts are based on mutual agreement, while compulsory contracts are imposed by law.
2
Understand the legal treatment of compulsory acquisition
The passage states that compulsory acquisition (istimlāk jabrī) is legally treated as a sale (bayʿ). This ensures the owner receives compensation and the transaction is valid under fiqh.
The state's authority to take property for public need is recognized, but the transaction is still considered a contract.
3
Distinguish explicit and implied forced contracts
Explicit forced contracts result from a direct court order or administrative decision. Implied forced contracts (muqaddar iqtiḍāʾ) are presumed by legal necessity, such as in preemption.
Preemption is treated as a purchase from the seller before delivery and from the buyer after delivery, by legal presumption.
4
Apply the concept to real cases
Consider the examples: road widening (taking property at value), expropriation for schools/hospitals, and preemption. In each, ownership is based on a forced contract.
The jurists even permit taking part of a mosque for road widening when needed, citing Radd al-Muhtar.

Chapter Five: The Theory of Ownership in Jurisprudence. For the purpose of widening the road if people's need requires its expansion, at the value equal to the expropriated property. The jurists have even stated that it is permissible to take part of a mosque to widen a road when needed (see Radd al-Muhtar 3/383-384). And the law of expropriation for public benefit allows compulsory acquisition of any property for which the highest administrative authority has determined a public benefit in its acquisition for a public interest, such as a school, hospital, or park, at its value assessed by a committee of experts. So the ownership of the preemptor of the property adjudicated to him in the case of preemption, and the ownership of the acquiring entity on its behalf in the case of expropriation, are considered based on a contract of purchase whose existence is presumed by way of implication and necessity. Commentary
This passage discusses the legal concept of compulsory acquisition (الاستملاك الجبري) as a cause of ownership in Hanafi fiqh. It opens by stating that when public need requires widening a road, the government may take private property at fair value. Jurists even permit taking part of a mosque for this purpose when necessary, citing Radd al-Muhtar. The law allows compulsory acquisition for public utilities like schools, hospitals, and parks, with compensation assessed by experts. The key legal point is that ownership in these cases is based on a contract—but a compulsory one. The author distinguishes between consensual contracts (عقد رضائي) and compulsory contracts (عقد جبري), and further divides compulsory contracts into explicit (صريح) and implied by necessity (مقدر اقتضاء). The preemption (شفعة) example illustrates the implied contract: when a property is taken by preemption, it is treated as a purchase from the seller before delivery and from the buyer after delivery, by legal presumption. The passage concludes that all compulsory acquisitions are legally constructed as sales, ensuring the owner receives compensation and the transaction is valid under fiqh.

Key Terms
العقد الجبري (al-ʿaqd al-jabrī): A contract whose conclusion is compelled by law or authority, not by mutual consent, but treated as a valid contract for legal purposes.
الاستملاك الجبري (al-istimlāk al-jabrī): Compulsory acquisition of private property by the state for public benefit, with compensation, considered as a forced sale.
التخريج الفقهي (al-takhrīj al-fiqhī): Juristic reasoning that deduces legal rulings from established principles and analogies.

Substitution (al-Khalafiyyah) and Inheritance as an Automatic Cause of Ownership
Defining substitution and explaining inheritance as a natural, automatic cause of ownership.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define al-khalafiyyah, distinguish its two types, and explain why inheritance is automatic and non-rejectable in Hanafi fiqh.

Core Idea
Inheritance is a natural substitution where the heir automatically replaces the deceased in ownership, without need for acceptance.

In this lesson, we explore the foundational concept of substitution (al-khalafiyyah) in Islamic legal theory. The author defines it as the replacement of a new person or thing in place of an old one in rights. We focus on the first type: inheritance, which is a cause of ownership established by Shariah. Understanding its automatic nature is crucial for distinguishing it from other transfers like bequests.

Guided Path
1
Identify the term and its definition
Locate the term 'الخَلَفيَّة' in the passage and note its linguistic meaning: replacement of a new person or thing for an old one in rights.
The term is derived from 'خلف' meaning successor.
2
Distinguish the two types of substitution
Recognize that substitution is divided into person-for-person (inheritance) and thing-for-thing (indemnity/compensation).
Inheritance is the focus; indemnity is mentioned for completeness.
3
Analyze inheritance as substitution
Understand how the heir replaces the deceased in ownership of the estate and in financial responsibilities limited to the estate.
The heir is not personally liable beyond the estate.
4
Understand the limit of liability
Note that inheritance is a cause of ownership, not a penalty; therefore the heir is not forced to pay debts exceeding the estate.
This is based on wisdom and fairness.
5
Grasp the automatic nature of inheritance
Inheritance operates without the heir's acceptance or rejection; it is part of Shariah's public order, unlike a bequest which requires acceptance.
This distinguishes inheritance from voluntary transfers.

الإرث خَلَفيَّة يحل بها الوارث محل المتوفى في ملكية أمواله المخلفة التي تسمى (تركة» وفي المسؤوليات المالية بتلك التركة.
فإذا لم يكن للمتوفى تركة أو كانت تركته أقل مما عليه من ديون، لا يكون الوارث مسؤولاً بأمواله فيما زاد من الدين عن التركة، لأنَّ الإرث شُرعَ سبباً للملك لا للتغريم، إذ لا يعقل أن يغرم إنسان تبعات تصرف صادر من غيره ولم يكن له به دخل، ولم يتكفل به.
والإرث سبيل تواردت عليه الشرائع الإلهية والوضعية، وفيه من الحكمة والنفع ما فيه؛ لأنه يحول نتائج جهود السلف إلى الخلف من رأس مال، وقوة إنتاجية ووسائل حيوية، فيجعل حبل الإنتاج والفعالية الاقتصادية متصلاً بنسبة من القوة مستمرة أو متزايدة؛ فلا يحتاج الخلف إلى إنشاء وسائل الحياة والإنتاج من جديد مبتدئاً من نواتها الصغرى، فيقضي معظم حياته في تكوين العدة الكافية .
وكما يتلقى الوارث هذه الخميرة الإرثية عن سلفه يحيلها هو بدوره إلى خلفه، فتكون في الأغلب قوة مالية ثابتة دائمة وينشط كل إنسان عادة إلى تنميتها وزيادتها حرصاً على أعقابه وترفيهاً لهم.
هذا كان الإرث سبباً طبيعياً للتملك، ثابتاً بحكم الشريعة دون وعن حاجة إلى سابق اتفاق أو وصية ولا يتوقف على قبول الوارث بعد وفاة المورث ؛ ولا يرتد برده ورفضه كما ترد الوصية برد الموصى له؛ لأنه
معدود من نظم الشريعة أي من النظام العام فيها .
23/8 - (Third) - Substitution (al-Khalafiyyah):
Chapter (23): Definition of Ownership and Explanation of Its Causes
Substitution (with two fatha and a shaddah on the ya) is: the replacement of a new person or thing in the place of an old, extinct one in rights. It is of two types:
- Substitution of a person for a person: inheritance.
- Substitution of a thing for a thing: indemnity or compensation.
a - Inheritance:
Inheritance is a substitution by which the heir takes the place of the deceased in the ownership of his left assets called 'estate' and in the financial responsibilities of that estate.
If the deceased has no estate, or his estate is less than his debts, the heir is not liable with his own assets for the excess of debt over the estate, because inheritance was legislated as a cause of ownership, not of fining. It is inconceivable that a person should bear the consequences of a transaction made by another, in which he had no involvement and for which he did not guarantee.
Inheritance is a path that divine and positive laws have concurred upon, and it has wisdom and benefit: it converts the results of the predecessors' efforts into the successors' capital, productive capacity, and vital means, making the rope of production and economic activity connected with a continuous or increasing strength; so the successor does not need to create the means of life and production anew starting from their smallest seeds, spending most of his life in forming sufficient equipment.
Just as the heir receives this inherited 'yeast' from his predecessor, he in turn passes it to his successor, so it is usually a stable, permanent financial strength, and every person is inclined to develop and increase it out of care for his descendants and to provide comfort for them.
Thus, inheritance is a natural cause of ownership, established by the ruling of Shariah without need for prior agreement or will, and it does not depend on the heir's acceptance after the death of the testator; nor is it rejected by the heir's refusal and rejection, as a bequest is rejected by the legatee's refusal, because it is counted among the systems of Shariah, i.e., part of its public order.
Source pages p. 320

Commentary
This passage introduces the concept of substitution (خلفية) as a cause of ownership. The author defines it as the replacement of a new person or thing in place of an old, extinct one in legal rights. There are two types: substitution of a person (inheritance) and substitution of a thing (indemnity or compensation). The focus then turns to inheritance, which is a substitution where the heir steps into the deceased's place regarding ownership of the estate and its liabilities. An important rule: the heir is not personally liable for debts exceeding the estate, because inheritance is meant for ownership, not penalty. The passage highlights the wisdom of inheritance: it ensures economic continuity, allowing successors to build on the capital and productive means of predecessors without starting from scratch. Inheritance is automatic; it does not require the heir's acceptance nor can it be rejected like a bequest, because it is part of the Shariah's public order (system). This distinguishes inheritance from a will, which requires the legatee's acceptance.

Key Terms
الخَلَفيَّة (al-khalafiyyah): The legal concept of a new person or thing taking the place of an old one in rights.
الإرث (al-irth): Inheritance: a substitution where the heir replaces the deceased in ownership and financial responsibility of the estate.
التضمين (al-taḍmīn): Indemnity: substitution of a thing (compensation) for a lost or damaged item.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why can an heir not be held personally liable for debts exceeding the estate?
A) Because the heir never consented.
B) Because inheritance is a cause of ownership, not a penalty.
C) Because the deceased's debts are void after death.
D) Because the state pays the debts.

Key Takeaway
Inheritance is an automatic substitution causing ownership; it is part of Shariah's public order and cannot be refused.

Definition of Khalfiyya and Its Types: Inheritance and Compensation
Defining khalfiyya as a cause of ownership and its two types.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define khalfiyya as a cause of ownership in Hanafi law and distinguish between its two primary types: inheritance (al-irth) and compensation (al-tadmeen/ta'weed).

Core Idea
Khalfiyya: substitution of a new person or thing for the old in rights, yielding inheritance and compensation.

This passage from Al-Madkhal al-Fiqhi al-Amm introduces a foundational concept in Hanafi legal philosophy: khalfiyya (substitution). Understanding this concept is key to grasping how ownership transfers in Islam—whether through the compulsory system of inheritance or through liability for harm. By parsing the definition and its two branches, you will see the underlying logic that links seemingly disparate areas of fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Understand the linguistic and technical definition
Identify the definition of khalfiyya: a new person or thing taking the place of an old one that has ceased to exist in rights. This is a broad category for causes of ownership.
The term comes from 'khalafa' meaning to succeed or substitute.
2
Identify the two types
Recognize that khalfiyya divides into: (1) substitution of a person for a person — inheritance, and (2) substitution of a thing for a thing — compensation (indemnity or damages).
These correspond to inherited ownership and liability for harm.
3
Analyze the characteristics of inheritance
Note that inheritance is a compulsory, automatic transfer of ownership without need for acceptance or possibility of renunciation. It is part of Sharia's public order (al-nizam al-'amm).
This distinguishes it from bequests, which require acceptance and are limited to one-third.
4
Examine the condition regarding debts
Understand that debts of the deceased must be paid from the estate before inheritance takes effect. Heirs are not personally liable beyond the estate; inheritance is a cause for ownership, not a penalty.
If debts exceed the estate, heirs cannot be forced to pay from their own wealth unless they voluntarily choose to.
5
Introduce compensation (tadmeen/ta'weed)
Recognize that compensation arises when a thing (e.g., value) replaces another thing due to destruction, usurpation, or harm. This is a different form of khalfiyya, focusing on restitution.
Details of compensation rules are covered in chapters on torts (ghasb, jinayat).

الفصل (۲۳) تعريف الملك وبيان أسبابه
الخلفية (بفتحتين مع تشديد الياء هي: حلول شخص أو شيء جديد محل قديم زائل في الحقوق، وهي نوعان:

- وخلفية شيء عن شيء، وهي التضمين أو التعويض.
أ - الإرث:
الإرث خَلَفيَّة يحل بها الوارث محل المتوفى في ملكية أمواله المخلفة التي تسمى «تركة» وفي المسؤوليات المالية بتلك التركة.
فإذا لم يكن للمتوفى تركة أو كانت تركته أقل مما عليه من ديون، لا يكون الوارث مسؤولاً بأمواله فيما زاد من الدين عن التركة، لأنَّ الإرث شُرعَ سبباً للملك لا للتغريم، إذ لا يعقل أن يغرم إنسان تبعات تصرف صادر من غيره ولم يكن له به دخل، ولم يتكفل به.

هذا كان الإرث سبباً طبيعياً للتملك، ثابتاً بحكم الشريعة دون وعن حاجة إلى سابق اتفاق أو وصية ولا يتوقف على قبول الوارث بعد وفاة المورث؛ ولا يرتد برده ورفضه كما ترد الوصية برد الموصى له؛ لأنه معدود من نظم الشريعة أي من النظام العام فيها.
وهذا معنى قولهم: إن الإرث جبري فلا يسقط بإسقاط الوارث، كما لا يؤثر فيه قصد المورث إلى الحرمان.
فلو أعلن إنسان وسجّل حرمان أحد ورثته أو تفضيل بعضهم على بعض في النصيب الإرثي، لم يكن لحرمانه أو تفضيله من أثر. وإنما يستطيع المورث أن يتصرف بأمواله كلها بالهبة أو سواها من التصرفات المنجَزَة حال حياته، وأن يوصي بثلث ماله فما دونه بعد موته لمن ليس بوارث، إذ لا وصية لوارث كيلا تُتَّخذ الوصية ذريعة إلى التفضيل في الإرث (۱).

Chapter (23): Definition of ownership and explanation of its causes. Khalfiyya (with two fatha and shaddah on ya) is: a new person or thing taking the place of an old one that has ceased to exist in rights. It is of two types: (1) Substitution of a person for a person, which is inheritance. (2) Substitution of a thing for a thing, which is indemnity or compensation. A - Inheritance: Inheritance is a khalfiyya by which the heir takes the place of the deceased in ownership of his left properties called 'estate' and in the financial liabilities of that estate. If the deceased has no estate or his estate is less than his debts, the heir is not liable with his own assets for the excess of debt over the estate, because inheritance was legislated as a cause for ownership, not for penalty, since it is unreasonable that a person should bear the consequences of an action emanating from another with which he had no involvement and for which he did not guarantee. Inheritance is a path that divine and positive laws have concurred upon, and in it is wisdom and benefit; because it transforms the results of the predecessors' efforts into capital, productive capacity, and vital means for the successors, making the thread of production and economic activity connected with a continuous or increasing proportion of strength; so the successor does not need to create the means of life and production anew starting from their smallest nucleus, thus spending most of his life forming sufficient equipment. And just as the heir receives this inherited yeast from his predecessor, he in turn passes it to his successor, so it often becomes a permanent fixed financial strength, and every person usually strives to develop and increase it out of care for his offspring and to provide comfort for them. This is why inheritance is a natural cause of ownership, established by the ruling of Sharia without need for prior agreement or will, and it does not depend on the acceptance of the heir after the death of the testator; nor is it revoked by his rejection and refusal, just as a bequest is revoked by the rejection of the legatee; because it is counted among the systems of Sharia, i.e., from its public order. This is the meaning of their statement: 'Inheritance is compulsory, so it is not dropped by the heir's renunciation, nor does the testator's intention to disinherit affect it.' So if a person announces and records the disinheritance of one of his heirs or the preference of some over others in the inherited share, his disinheritance or preference has no effect. Rather, the testator can dispose of all his wealth by gift or other immediate dispositions during his lifetime, and he can bequeath one-third of his wealth or less after his death to someone who is not an heir, as there is no bequest to an heir lest the bequest be used as a means to favor in inheritance (1). It becomes clear from the above that the condition for ownership by inheritance is that the deceased not have a debt that encompasses the estate, for if there is, its repayment from the estate takes precedence over the inheritance of the heirs, so that even if these heirs sell something from the estate, the sale is not effective because they sold what they did not own, unless the heirs wish to pay all the debt from their own funds. Then the estate is delivered to them if the creditors are paid their debts in full, so they have no remaining connection to the estate. If the amount of debt is more than the estate and the heirs wish to pay the creditors from the debt only the equivalent value of the estate and take ownership of it, they do not have that right unless the creditors agree. (R) al-Durr al-Mukhtar and Radd al-Muhtar in the Book of Qada 4/340. B - Indemnity or compensation: If someone destroys something belonging to another, or usurps it and it perishes or is lost, and likewise if he causes harm to another by a crime or direct causation, then in these and similar cases indemnity is obligatory upon him for what... (1) Previously explained the method of 'blocking the means' and its examples in Islamic legislation when discussing istislah; see there p. 7/5. Also previously mentioned the prophetic hadith 'No bequest to an heir' and its takhrij at the end of paragraph 2/10 B.
Source pages p. 320

Commentary
This passage is a foundational legal definition from Dr. Mustafa al-Zarqa's 'Al-Madkhal al-Fiqhi al-Amm'. It introduces the concept of 'khalfiyya' (substitution) as a cause of ownership. The author first defines khalfiyya linguistically and technically: it is the replacement of a person or thing by another in rights. This is a broad category that covers two main types: inheritance (substitution of a person for a person) and compensation (substitution of a thing for a thing). The discussion focuses primarily on inheritance, explaining its nature as a compulsory transfer of ownership without the need for acceptance or the possibility of rejection. The author emphasizes the wisdom behind inheritance: it preserves capital and productive forces across generations, preventing each generation from starting from scratch. He also clarifies a key rule: heirs are not personally liable for debts beyond the estate; inheritance is a cause for ownership, not for penalty. However, debts must be paid before distribution. Neither the testator's attempt to disinherit nor the heir's renunciation has legal effect because inheritance is part of the public order of Sharia. The author also mentions that bequests are limited to one-third and cannot be made to an heir. The condition for inheritance is that the estate is not entirely consumed by debts; if debts exist, they take priority. Heirs may only retain the estate if they pay off all debts personally. The passage ends with a brief introduction to the second type of khalfiyya—compensation (tadmeen or ta'weed)—which arises from torts like destruction or usurpation, but the detailed discussion is cut off here. This lesson provides a clear, systematic overview of how Hanafi fiqh categorizes these causes of ownership.

Key Terms
الخَلَفيَّة (al-khalfiyya): The substitution of a new person or thing for an old one that has ceased to exist in rights.
الإرث (al-irth): A khalfiyya where the heir replaces the deceased in ownership of the estate and its financial liabilities.
التضمين أو التعويض (al-tadmeen aw al-ta'weed): A khalfiyya where a thing replaces another thing as compensation for loss or damage.
التركة (al-tarika): The estate left by the deceased, which is subject to inheritance after payment of debts.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Because inheritance is a debt that must be paid.
B) Because inheritance is considered part of the public order (al-nizam al-'amm) of Sharia.
C) Because the heir must accept it as a trust from the deceased.
D) Because the heir is legally bound by the will of the testator.

Key Takeaway
Inheritance is a compulsory khalfiyya that transfers ownership automatically and cannot be renounced; compensation is a khalfiyya replacing a thing with its value in torts.

Legal Philosophy and Theory

Ownership through Substitution: Definition and Scope
Defining ownership through substitution with examples

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the cause of ownership through substitution (al-khalafiyya) and distinguish it from other causes such as contract or acquisition.

Core Idea
Ownership through substitution arises automatically from liability for harm, not from contract or acquisition.

In Hanafi legal theory, ownership can arise through four causes: acquisition (ihraz), contract, inheritance, and substitution. This passage introduces the third cause — substitution — which is unique because ownership here is not voluntarily acquired but is imposed by law to compensate for loss. Understanding this principle helps muftis analyze liability cases involving damage to property, benefit, or bodily harm.

Guided Path
1
Identify the triggering event
Legal substitution begins when a person causes harm (itlaf or ta'diyya) to another's property, benefit, or limb. The harm may be direct or indirect.
Distinguish harm that is legally compensable (e.g., property destruction, injury) from harm that is not (e.g., mere inconvenience).
2
Recognize the legal consequence
Liability arises on the harmer to provide compensation—diya for life or limb, arsh for wounds, or monetary equivalent for property.
Diya and arsh are specific terms: diya for full life or limb, arsh for lesser injuries.
3
Understand the ownership shift
The victim becomes the owner of the compensation as a substitute for the right that was lost. This ownership is not based on agreement (contract) or taking possession (acquisition) but on the legal obligation to substitute.
The compensation is called 'iwad (substitute) or khalaf (replacement).
4
Differentiate from other causes
Contrast substitution with contract: in contract, ownership arises from mutual consent and exchange (e.g., sale). In substitution, ownership is involuntary on the payer but legally imposed.
Also differentiate from inheritance (succession to the deceased's estate) and acquisition (taking possession of ownerless property).
5
Apply to new scenarios
Test the concept: if a person damages a borrowed item, does the owner's right to compensation arise by substitution? Yes, because the harm caused by the borrower triggers liability.
This helps classify real-life cases under the correct legal cause for rulings.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

He destroys it, and compensation for the harm that he directly caused or was the cause of. Then the compensated person owns that compensation by way of ownership based on the cause of substitution, because this compensation is a substitute for what was damaged of property, benefit, or limb. This includes blood money and the 'arsh of injuries; all that is owned due to substitution.

Commentary
This passage explains the third cause of ownership: substitution (al-khalafiyya). When a person harms another's property, benefit, or limb liability arises, and the compensation (diya or arsh) becomes owned by the victim through substitution. This is a distinct cause because ownership arises neither by contract nor by acquisition (ihraz) but as a legal substitute for the original right.

Key Terms
الخلفية (al-khalafiyya): A cause of ownership where one owns something as a substitute for a right that was lost due to harm caused by another.
الأرش (al-arsh): Financial compensation for injuries short of the soul or limb (as opposed to diya for life or full limb).

The Fourth Cause of Ownership: Tawallud min al-Mamluk
Critiquing the Majalla's enumeration and adding a fourth cause.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the four causes of ownership in Hanafi fiqh and explain why tawallud is an independent cause.

Core Idea
Progeny of owned property is owned by a separate cause: tawallud, not derivatively from acquisition, contract, or substitution.

The Ottoman Majalla limited the causes of ownership to three: acquisition (ihraz), contract (aqd), and substitution (khilafiyyah) — the latter interpreted narrowly as inheritance. However, Hanafi legal scholars recognized that certain forms of ownership, such as the offspring of livestock or crops from land, do not fit neatly into these categories. In this passage, the author argues for a fourth cause: tawallud min al-mamluk (progeny from owned property). This lesson unpacks that argument and its implications for property law.

Guided Path
1
Examine the Majalla's classification
Identify the three causes listed in Article 1248 (ihraz, aqd, khilafiyyah) and note that tawallud is omitted. Understand that khilafiyyah was restricted to inheritance.
2
Understand the ghasb example
Analyze the case of a usurper planting land: the crop belongs to the usurper (as growth of his seed), not the landowner. This shows that the fruit of one's property (seed) is owned by the seed's owner, not the landowner. The landowner only gets compensation for damage.
3
Trace the reasoning for tawallud as independent
Follow the argument: the offspring (e.g., of a usurped animal) cannot be owned via acquisition (it is not taken), contract, or substitution (no transfer). Hence it must be via a separate cause: tawallud from the owned original.
4
Apply to a concrete scenario
Consider a usurped sheep giving birth. The lamb is owned by the original owner, not the usurper, because it is tawallud min al-mamluk. The usurper is not liable as a usurper for the lamb, only for the sheep.
5
Note the modern legal adaptation
The Syrian Civil Code (Art. 885) modified the ghasb case, giving the landowner options. This shows the ongoing relevance of these causation principles.

Source Text

Chapter Five: The Theory of Property in Jurisprudence. The Book of Usurpation in jurisprudential works, because the confirmation of ownership of the branch to the owner of the original does not necessitate that the usurper of the original be considered a usurper of the branch that he did not take from the owner, rather it came into his possession by occurrence. As for if the usurper plants the land, then the crop belongs to him, not to the landowner, because it is the growth of the seed, which is his property, but he guarantees to the landowner the diminution of the land by planting if it is diminished thereby (1). Likewise, the Majalla intended by al-khilafiyyah: inheritance only, so it interpreted it as substitution of one person for another, following in this statement what is in some fiqh books (2), without considering what the branches of rulings indicate regarding the legal causes of ownership. And we have seen from tracing the branches of forms of ownership that it is necessary to add a fourth cause, which is tawallud from the owned, as we did, because the thing that generates from the owned does not have the ownership of the original owner based on acquisition, since it may be in someone else's hand like the offspring of a usurped animal, nor is it based on a contract or substitution, as is apparent, so it is necessary that tawallud be an independent cause. (1) This legal ruling has now been amended in our view by Article 885 of the new Syrian Civil Code, for this article ruled when someone other than the owner plants the land that the landowner has a choice between two matters: either to pay the planter the value of the seed and take ownership of his crop by annexation to the land, or to leave the crop to the planter and take from him the rent equivalent for the land for a full year. This is if the planting season has passed; otherwise, the landowner, if he wishes, may compel the planter to remove his seed from the land without any compensation for the labor of plowing and planting. (2) Ibn Nujaym mentioned that in [the chapter on] hunting and slaughtering in his book al-Ashbah wa al-Naza'ir, and the Majalla followed him in it (see: Mir'at al-Majalla, Article 1248).
Source pages p. 323

Commentary
This passage is a legal philosophical analysis of the causes of ownership in Hanafi fiqh. The author begins by discussing the example of usurpation (ghasb): if someone usurps land and plants it, the crop belongs to the usurper because it is the growth of his own seed, not the landowner's. However, the usurper is liable for any damage to the land. The key principle is that the offspring of an owned thing is owned by the owner of the original, but the usurper is not considered a usurper of the offspring simply because it came into his possession. This leads to the critique of the Ottoman Majalla, which in Article 1248 enumerated only three causes of ownership: acquisition (ihraz), contract (aqd), and substitution (khilafiyyah) – interpreting the latter only as inheritance (person substituting person). The author argues that a fourth independent cause is necessary: tawallud min al-mamluk (progeny or produce from owned property). He explains that the ownership of such progeny cannot be based on acquisition (since it may be in another's hand, e.g., offspring of a usurped animal), nor on contract or substitution (as is evident), so it must be a separate cause. The footnote provides a modern Syrian legal modification: in the case of planting land without permission, the landowner now has options, including compensating the planter for the seed or taking rent.

Key Terms
التولد من المملوك (al-tawallud min al-mamluk): Ownership of the progeny or produce of an owned thing, such as offspring of livestock or crops from land.
الخَلَفيَّة (al-khilafiyyah): Substitution: person substituting person (inheritance) or thing substituting thing (compensation).
الغَضب (al-ghasb): Unlawful taking of property without permission; usurpation.
سبب الملك (sabab al-milk): A legal cause that gives rise to ownership.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the author, why is tawallud min al-mamluk an independent cause of ownership?

Compensation as a Cause of Ownership: Subsumed Under Substitution or Contract
Argue that compensation ownership needs no fifth cause

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the expanded concept of khilafiyyah to classify compensation ownership under the four causes of ownership and distinguish it from pre-emption ownership.

Core Idea
Ownership of compensation arises instantly from the harmful act, not from payment or judgment.

This passage addresses a subtle point in Hanafi legal philosophy: whether compensation for damages (ta'wid) requires adding a fifth cause of ownership beyond the established four (inheritance, contract, etc.). The author argues that by expanding the concept of khilafiyyah (substitution) to include substitution of thing for thing, compensation fits neatly into the existing framework. He also refutes the idea that compensation is merely a form of contract, highlighting a key distinction between compensation and pre-emption (shuf'a). Understanding this distinction is crucial for fatwa in cases of property damage and liability.

Guided Path
1
Identify the four causes of ownership
Recall the standard Hanafi four causes: inheritance (irth), contract ('aqd), substitution (khilafiyyah), and testament (wasiyyah). The passage focuses on how compensation relates to khilafiyyah and contract.
The author expands khilafiyyah to include substitution of person for person (inheritance) and substitution of thing for thing (compensation).
2
Analyze the narrow vs. expanded khilafiyyah
The Majalla narrowly defined khilafiyyah as inheritance only. The author expands it to include substitution of thing for thing, which covers compensation. Recognize this as a jurisprudential choice to avoid adding a fifth cause.
This expansion maintains consistency of the four-cause framework.
3
Refute the contract analogy for compensation
Some may think compensation arises via a consensual or compulsory contract (like pre-emption). The author refutes this by showing that compensation debt exists from the moment of the harmful act, not from payment or judgment. Distinguish this from pre-emption where ownership is created by consent or judgment.
Key difference: compensation debt is immediate; pre-emption is a right to acquire, not ownership.
4
Apply the distinction to a scenario
Consider a case where someone destroys another's property. The victim owns the compensation debt immediately. Any later payment or court judgment only manifests that pre-existing ownership, not creates it. Contrast with pre-emption where the pre-emptor does not own the property until acquisition.
This affects whether the victim can take the tortfeasor's property without court order (Hanafi view permits self-help).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أما ملك العوض في حالات التعويض أو التضمين فيبقى أيضاً خارجاً الاستناد إلى أحد الأسباب الأربعة إذا فُسِّرت الخلفية بالإرث كما فعلت عن المجلة ومصادرها الفقهية (۱).
لكننا استغنينا عن جعل التعويض سبباً خامساً بالتوسع في معنى الخَلَفيَّة فجعلناها نوعين: خَلَفيَّةُ شخص عن شخص، وخَلَفيَّةُ شيء عن شيء كما رأيت. وبذلك يصبح مُطّرداً تخريج جميع صور الملكية على هذه الأسباب الأربعة.
أن ملك العوض في حالة التعويض يمكن رده إلى سبب العقد، باعتبار أن (۱) قد يتوهم دفع العوّض إن كان باختيار الجاني فهو عقد ،رضائي وإن كان بالحكم عليه فهو عقد إجباري كما في حالة الأخذ بالشفعة.
سبب الملك هو
ولكن هذا التوهم مدفوع بان ما قارنه ثبوت الملك، وأن الفعل الضار منشية لالتزام الفاعل بالتعويض منذ صدور الفعل عنه، فحق الشخص المتضرر في العوض عن ضرره ثابت بصفة دين له في ذمة الجاني قبل القضاء له به أو دفع الجاني بدلاً عنه برضاه. فمن أتلف لآخر مالاً أصبح مديناً لصاحبه بمثله أو بقيمته بدليل أن المتضرر يحل له شرعاً إذا وقع في يده مال للجاني أن يستوفي منه قدر حقه هذا ويتصرف به كما تقدم
ر ف ٤/٢)
فهذا الدين في ذمة الجاني هو أحد أنواع الملكية كما تقدّم بيانه، فتحتاج هذه الملكية إلى سبب مقارن تستند إليه منذ حصولها. وليس دفع الجاني للعوض برضاه إلا تفريغاً لذمته من دين مملوك قبل الدفع. وليس القضاء به عند التخاصم إلا مظهراً ومثبتاً لحق المالك هنا لا منشئاً له. وذلك بخلاف حال الشفعة فإن المقرر شرعاً أنّ الشفيع لا يملك العقار الذي له فيه شفعة إلا إذا أعطاه إياه المشتري برضاه أو قضي عليه بالشفعة؛ أما قبل ذلك فالعقار باق على ملك المشتري، وإنما للشفيع حق أرجحية شرعية عليه في شراء العقار المبيع. فالقضاء بالشفعة منشيء لملك الشفيع لا مظهر وإن كان حقه في أن يتملك» ناشئاً ببيعه منذ انعقاده. العقار المبيع فلذلك أمكن أن نعتبر ملك الشفيع في حال الشفعة مستنداً إلى سببية عقد ضمني رضائي أو قضائي، ولا يمكن مثل هذا الاعتبار في الملك بالتعويض
As for the ownership of compensation in cases of compensation or guarantee, it remains outside the reference to one of the four causes if khilafiyyah is interpreted as inheritance as done by the Majalla and its jurisprudential sources (1). But we dispensed with making compensation a fifth cause by expanding the meaning of khilafiyyah, so we made it two types: substitution of person for person, and substitution of thing for thing, as you have seen. And thereby it becomes consistent to derive all forms of ownership upon these four causes. That the ownership of compensation in a case of compensation can be reduced to the cause of contract, considering that (1) one might imagine that the payment of compensation if it is by the choice of the tortfeasor is a consensual contract, and if by judgment against him it is a compulsory contract, as in the case of pre-emption (shuf'a). [But] this imagination is refuted by what [you will see]: that [the cause] accompanies the establishment of ownership, and that the harmful act is the cause of the obligation of the actor to compensate from the moment the act occurs from him. So the right of the injured person to compensation for his harm is established as a debt in the liability of the tortfeasor before a judgment in his favor or the tortfeasor's voluntary payment. So whoever destroys the property of another becomes indebted to its owner for its like or its value, evidenced by the fact that the injured person is legally permitted, if property of the tortfeasor comes into his hand, to collect his right from it and to dispose of it, as previously mentioned (ref. 4/2). So this debt in the liability of the tortfeasor is one of the types of ownership, as its explanation preceded, and this ownership requires a contemporaneous cause upon which it is based from its occurrence. And the tortfeasor's voluntary payment of compensation is only a discharge of his liability from a debt that was owned before the payment. And the judgment upon litigation is only a manifestation and affirmation of the right of the owner here, not a creator of it. This is contrary to the case of pre-emption, for it is legally established that the pre-emptor does not own the property in which he has pre-emption unless the buyer gives it to him voluntarily or it is adjudged upon him; before that, the property remains in the ownership of the buyer, and the pre-emptor only has a legal priority right in purchasing the sold property. So the judgment of pre-emption creates the ownership of the pre-emptor, not manifests it, even though his right to own arises from the sale from its conclusion. Therefore, we can consider the ownership of the pre-emptor in the case of pre-emption as based on a tacit consensual or judicial contract, and such consideration is not possible for ownership by compensation.
Source pages p. 324

Commentary
This passage addresses the potential need for a fifth cause of ownership: compensation (ta'wid) in cases of tort or guarantee. The author notes that if khilafiyyah (substitution) is narrowly defined as inheritance only (as in the Majalla), then compensation for damages would fall outside the four causes. However, the author avoids adding a fifth cause by expanding khilafiyyah to include substitution of thing for thing (e.g., compensation in kind). Thus compensation can be subsumed under this expanded substitution or under a contract (consensual or compulsory). The author then refutes the idea that compensation is based on a contract. He argues that the tortfeasor's obligation arises from the moment of the harmful act, so the right to compensation is already a debt in the tortfeasor's liability. The ownership of this debt exists from the start, not created by payment or judgment. This distinguishes compensation from pre-emption (shuf'a), where the pre-emptor does not own the property until acquisition by consent or judgment. In pre-emption, the judgment creates ownership; in compensation, it only manifests pre-existing ownership. Therefore, compensation does not require a separate cause; it can be explained via the existing four causes, especially expanded khilafiyyah.

Key Terms
التعويض (al-ta'wid): Compensation paid for damage or destruction of property.
الشفعة (al-shuf'a): The right of pre-emption; a legal priority to buy sold property.
خَلَفيَّة الشيء عن الشيء (khilafiyyat al-shay' 'an al-shay'): Substitution of one thing for another, e.g., paying compensation in kind.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does the author consider that compensation does not require a fifth independent cause of ownership?
A) Because compensation is always based on a contract between the parties.
B) Because the right to compensation arises as a debt immediately from the harmful act, so it can be subsumed under expanded khilafiyyah.
C) Because the Majalla already included it under the three causes.
D) Because compensation is not a form of ownership.

Key Takeaway
Compensation ownership is not contract-based; it arises from the harmful act and is subsumed under an expanded concept of khilafiyyah.

Expanding Khilāfah to Absorb Compensation as a Cause of Ownership
Legal-philosophical refinement of ownership causes

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain how the Hanafi principle of khilāfah (substitution) is expanded to include compensation, thereby avoiding the need for a fifth cause of ownership.

Core Idea
Compensation is not a fifth cause; expanding khilāfah to cover thing-substitution absorbs it.

The Hanafi school recognizes four causes of ownership: contract, seizure of mubah, inheritance, and production. Compensation (ʿiwaḍ) might seem to require a fifth cause, but this passage shows how the concept of substitution (khilāfah) can be broadened to include both substitution of a person (inheritance) and substitution of a thing (compensation). Understanding this expansion is key to seeing the coherence of the Hanafi system of ownership causes.

Guided Path
1
Identify the four established causes of ownership
Recall the four causes: contract, seizure of mubah, inheritance, and production from owned property. This passage discusses whether compensation fits as a fifth.
The author argues against a fifth cause.
2
Understand the traditional definition of khilāfah
In the Majalla, khilāfah was confined to inheritance — substitution of a person. The author proposes expanding it to substitution of a thing.
This is the key move to avoid a fifth cause.
3
See how compensation fits as thing-substitution
When property is destroyed, the victim's right to compensation is a debt that arises instantly. This debt is a form of ownership (milk al-dayn). The compensation payment is merely discharging that debt, not creating ownership.
Thus compensation is already covered under the cause of tort/debt, which is a form of contract or inheritance? Actually it stems from the harmful act, not a separate cause.
4
Apply the expanded khilāfah to a scenario
Example: A person destroys another's car. Immediately, the victim owns a debt (the car's value) in the wrongdoer's liability. This ownership arises from the tort, which is not a separate cause but a type of obligation (iltizām) analogous to contract.
The author subsumes tort under contract as 'compulsory contract' or debt, maintaining only four causes.

Source Text

الفصل (۲۳) تعريف الملك وبيان أسبابه
أما ملك العوض في حالات التعويض أو التضمين فيبقى أيضاً خارجاً الاستناد إلى أحد الأسباب الأربعة إذا فُسِّرت الخلفية بالإرث كما فعلت عن المجلة ومصادرها الفقهية (۱).
لكننا استغنينا عن جعل التعويض سبباً خامساً بالتوسع في معنى الخَلَفيَّة فجعلناها نوعين: خَلَفيَّةُ شخص عن شخص، وخَلَفيَّةُ شيء عن شيء كما رأيت. وبذلك يصبح مُطّرداً تخريج جميع صور الملكية على هذه الأسباب الأربعة.

فهذا الدين في ذمة الجاني هو أحد أنواع الملكية كما تقدّم بيانه، فتحتاج هذه الملكية إلى سبب مقارن تستند إليه منذ حصولها. وليس دفع الجاني للعوض برضاه إلا تفريغاً لذمته من دين مملوك قبل الدفع. وليس القضاء به عند التخاصم إلا مظهراً ومثبتاً لحق المالك هنا لا منشئاً له. وذلك بخلاف حال الشفعة فإن المقرر شرعاً أنّ الشفيع لا يملك العقار الذي له فيه شفعة إلا إذا أعطاه إياه المشتري برضاه أو قضي عليه بالشفعة؛ أما قبل ذلك فالعقار باق على ملك المشتري، وإنما للشفيع حق أرجحية شرعية عليه في شراء العقار المبيع. فالقضاء بالشفعة منشيء لملك الشفيع لا مظهر وإن كان حقه في أن يتملك» ناشئاً ببيعه منذ انعقاده. العقار المبيع فلذلك أمكن أن نعتبر ملك الشفيع في حال الشفعة مستنداً إلى سببية عقد ضمني رضائي أو قضائي، ولا يمكن مثل هذا الاعتبار في الملك بالتعويض
Chapter (23): Definition of Ownership and Explanation of Its Causes. As for ownership of compensation in cases of compensation or indemnification, it also remains outside the reliance on one of the four causes if substitution is interpreted as inheritance as done in the Majalla and its jurisprudential sources (1). But we dispensed with making compensation a fifth cause by expanding the meaning of substitution, making it two types: substitution of a person for a person and substitution of a thing for a thing, as you saw. Thus, the derivation of all forms of ownership from these four causes becomes consistent. That ownership of compensation in the case of compensation can be traced back to the cause of contract, considering that (1) one might imagine that payment of compensation, if done voluntarily by the wrongdoer, is a consensual contract, and if by judicial ruling, it is a compulsory contract, as in the case of pre-emption. But this delusion is refuted because what accompanies it is the establishment of ownership, and that the harmful act generates the obligation of the wrongdoer to compensate from the moment the act occurs. So the right of the injured person to compensation for his harm is established as a debt in the liability of the wrongdoer before judgment is given for it or the wrongdoer pays in its place voluntarily. So whoever destroys another's property becomes indebted to its owner for its equivalent or value, as evidence that the injured person is legally permitted, if he obtains property of the wrongdoer, to collect from it the amount of his right and dispose of it as previously stated (ref. 4/2). So this debt in the wrongdoer's liability is one of the types of ownership as previously explained, and this ownership requires a concurrent cause to rely on since its occurrence. The wrongdoer's payment of compensation voluntarily is only a discharge of his liability from a debt owned before payment. And the judicial ruling in case of dispute is only a manifestation and confirmation of the owner's right here, not a creator of it. This is unlike the case of pre-emption, for it is established legally that the pre-emptor does not own the property in which he has pre-emption unless the buyer gives it to him voluntarily or judgment is given against him for pre-emption; before that, the property remains in the ownership of the buyer, and the pre-emptor only has a legal priority right to purchase the sold property. So the judgment of pre-emption creates ownership for the pre-emptor, not merely manifests it, even though his right to own the sold property arises from the sale since its conclusion. Therefore, we can consider the pre-emptor's ownership in the case of pre-emption based on a causative consensual or judicial implied contract, and such consideration is not possible in ownership by compensation.
Source pages p. 324

Commentary
This passage is a legal-philosophical analysis of the causes of ownership, specifically addressing whether compensation (عوض) constitutes a fifth cause of ownership. The author argues that compensation does not need to be a separate cause because the concept of substitution (خلفية) can be expanded to include both substitution of a person (as in inheritance) and substitution of a thing. The key point is that when someone causes harm, a debt arises immediately in the wrongdoer's liability, and this debt is a form of ownership for the victim. The subsequent payment or judgment merely discharges or confirms this pre-existing right. This is contrasted with pre-emption (شفعة), where the pre-emptor does not own the property until the payment or judgment, so the judgment actually creates ownership. Therefore, compensation is not an independent cause but falls under contract (express or implied) or tort, already covered by the original four causes (contract, seizure of mubah, inheritance, and production from owned property).

Key Terms
خلافة (khilāfah): Substitution; here it means the replacement of one person or thing by another in the context of causes of ownership, expanded to include both substitution of a person (e.g., inheritance) and substitution of a thing (e.g., compensation).
عوض (ʿiwaḍ): Compensation; the payment made as a substitute for harm or loss, which the author argues is not a separate cause of ownership but a consequence of a pre-existing debt.
سبب الملك (sabab al-milk): The legal cause that gives rise to ownership; the four recognized causes are contract, seizure of mubah, inheritance, and production from owned property.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) Only when the wrongdoer voluntarily pays.
B) Only when the court issues a judgment.
C) Immediately at the moment the harmful act occurs.
D) When the victim seizes property of the wrongdoer.

Key Takeaway
Compensation is not a fifth cause of ownership; expanding khilāfah to include thing-substitution maintains the Hanafi four-cause framework.

Compensation vs. Pre-emption: Why Timing of Ownership Differs
Critical distinction between two cases

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the legal effect of a judgment in compensation (declaratory) from that in pre-emption (creative) and apply this distinction to determine when ownership arises.

Core Idea
In pre-emption, ownership arises at judgment; in compensation, ownership arises at the harmful act.

One might mistakenly analogize compensation to pre-emption because both can involve compulsory payment. However, the passage clarifies a fundamental difference: in pre-emption, the judgment actually creates ownership, whereas in compensation, it merely confirms a pre-existing right. Understanding this distinction is essential for correctly classifying ownership causes and for muftis issuing fatwas on related cases.

Guided Path
1
Recall the facts of compensation (from previous lesson)
When property is destroyed, a debt arises immediately. Payment/judgment are confirmatory. Ownership (as debt) exists from the harmful act.
This sets the baseline.
2
Recall the facts of pre-emption (shufʿa)
The pre-emptor has only a priority right to purchase. Ownership of the property remains with the buyer until the pre-emptor pays or obtains a judgment. The judgment creates ownership.
The right is not ownership yet.
3
Identify the legal consequence of timing
In compensation, because ownership exists from the harmful act, the victim may even take property of the wrongdoer to satisfy the debt without a judgment. In pre-emption, the pre-emptor cannot take the property without payment/judgment.
The passage cites the victim's right to seize: دليل أن المتضرر يحل له شرعاً إذا وقع في يده مال للجاني أن يستوفي منه قدر حقه
4
Explain why the analogy fails
Both involve compulsory payment, but the underlying right differs: one is a debt (ownership already), the other is a mere priority.
The author's use of 'منشيء' vs 'مظهر' is key.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
