ولكن هذا التوهم مدفوع بان ما قارنه ثبوت الملك، وأن الفعل الضار منشية لالتزام الفاعل بالتعويض منذ صدور الفعل عنه، فحق الشخص المتضرر في العوض عن ضرره ثابت بصفة دين له في ذمة الجاني قبل القضاء له به أو دفع الجاني بدلاً عنه برضاه. فمن أتلف لآخر مالاً أصبح مديناً لصاحبه بمثله أو بقيمته بدليل أن المتضرر يحل له شرعاً إذا وقع في يده مال للجاني أن يستوفي منه قدر حقه هذا ويتصرف به كما تقدم (ر ف ٤/٢)
فهذا الدين في ذمة الجاني هو أحد أنواع الملكية كما تقدّم بيانه، فتحتاج هذه الملكية إلى سبب مقارن تستند إليه منذ حصولها. وليس دفع الجاني للعوض برضاه إلا تفريغاً لذمته من دين مملوك قبل الدفع. وليس القضاء به عند التخاصم إلا مظهراً ومثبتاً لحق المالك هنا لا منشئاً له. وذلك بخلاف حال الشفعة فإن المقرر شرعاً أنّ الشفيع لا يملك العقار الذي له فيه شفعة إلا إذا أعطاه إياه المشتري برضاه أو قضي عليه بالشفعة؛ أما قبل ذلك فالعقار باق على ملك المشتري، وإنما للشفيع حق أرجحية شرعية عليه في شراء العقار المبيع. فالقضاء بالشفعة منشيء لملك الشفيع لا مظهر وإن كان حقه في أن يتملك» ناشئاً ببيعه منذ انعقاده. العقار المبيع فلذلك أمكن أن نعتبر ملك الشفيع في حال الشفعة مستنداً إلى سببية عقد ضمني رضائي أو قضائي، ولا يمكن مثل هذا الاعتبار في الملك بالتعويض
Chapter (23): Definition of Ownership and Explanation of Its Causes. As for ownership of compensation in cases of compensation or indemnification, it also remains outside the reliance on one of the four causes if substitution is interpreted as inheritance as done in the Majalla and its jurisprudential sources (1). But we dispensed with making compensation a fifth cause by expanding the meaning of substitution, making it two types: substitution of a person for a person and substitution of a thing for a thing, as you saw. Thus, the derivation of all forms of ownership from these four causes becomes consistent. That ownership of compensation in the case of compensation can be traced back to the cause of contract, considering that (1) one might imagine that payment of compensation, if done voluntarily by the wrongdoer, is a consensual contract, and if by judicial ruling, it is a compulsory contract, as in the case of pre-emption. But this delusion is refuted because what accompanies it is the establishment of ownership, and that the harmful act generates the obligation of the wrongdoer to compensate from the moment the act occurs. So the right of the injured person to compensation for his harm is established as a debt in the liability of the wrongdoer before judgment is given for it or the wrongdoer pays in its place voluntarily. So whoever destroys another's property becomes indebted to its owner for its equivalent or value, as evidence that the injured person is legally permitted, if he obtains property of the wrongdoer, to collect from it the amount of his right and dispose of it as previously stated (ref. 4/2). So this debt in the wrongdoer's liability is one of the types of ownership as previously explained, and this ownership requires a concurrent cause to rely on since its occurrence. The wrongdoer's payment of compensation voluntarily is only a discharge of his liability from a debt owned before payment. And the judicial ruling in case of dispute is only a manifestation and confirmation of the owner's right here, not a creator of it. This is unlike the case of pre-emption, for it is established legally that the pre-emptor does not own the property in which he has pre-emption unless the buyer gives it to him voluntarily or judgment is given against him for pre-emption; before that, the property remains in the ownership of the buyer, and the pre-emptor only has a legal priority right to purchase the sold property. So the judgment of pre-emption creates ownership for the pre-emptor, not merely manifests it, even though his right to own the sold property arises from the sale since its conclusion. Therefore, we can consider the pre-emptor's ownership in the case of pre-emption based on a causative consensual or judicial implied contract, and such consideration is not possible in ownership by compensation.
Source pages p. 324

Commentary
This passage is a legal-philosophical analysis of the causes of ownership, specifically addressing whether compensation (عوض) constitutes a fifth cause of ownership. The author argues that compensation does not need to be a separate cause because the concept of substitution (خلفية) can be expanded to include both substitution of a person (as in inheritance) and substitution of a thing. The key point is that when someone causes harm, a debt arises immediately in the wrongdoer's liability, and this debt is a form of ownership for the victim. The subsequent payment or judgment merely discharges or confirms this pre-existing right. This is contrasted with pre-emption (شفعة), where the pre-emptor does not own the property until the payment or judgment, so the judgment actually creates ownership. Therefore, compensation is not an independent cause but falls under contract (express or implied) or tort, already covered by the original four causes (contract, seizure of mubah, inheritance, and production from owned property).

Key Terms
مظهر (muzhir): Declaratory; a judgment that confirms pre-existing ownership without creating it.
منشيء (munshiʾ): Constitutive; a judgment that itself creates ownership (as in pre-emption).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the legal nature of the court's judgment in a compensation case?
A) It creates ownership (munshiʾ).
B) It confirms pre-existing ownership (muzhir).
C) It has no effect on ownership; ownership is irrelevant.
D) It both creates and confirms ownership.

Key Takeaway
In compensation, the judgment is declaratory (muzhir) of pre-existing ownership; in pre-emption, it is constitutive (munshiʾ), because the underlying right differs.

Legal Philosophy and Theory

Causes of Ownership: Voluntary vs Compulsory, Creative vs Transferring
Classifying causes of ownership by choice and effect

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify any given cause of ownership as voluntary or compulsory, and as creative or transferring, based on the Hanafi classification as presented by al-Zarqa.

Core Idea
Ownership arises from four causes: two voluntary (acquisition of permissible things, contracts) and two compulsory (inheritance, natural increase).

This passage from al-Zarqa's Al-Madkhal al-Fiqhi al-‘Amm provides a foundational classification of how ownership is acquired in Hanafi law. Understanding these categories is essential for the mufti when analyzing the validity of ownership claims, as the nature of the cause determines whether ownership is original or transferred. We will dissect the twofold division: first by the presence of choice, and second by the effect on pre-existing ownership.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two bases of division
The passage divides causes of ownership according to two distinct considerations: (1) whether the owner has choice in bringing about the cause, and (2) whether the cause creates new ownership or transfers existing ownership.
These are independent cross-cutting classifications.
2
List the four causes and assign to voluntary/compulsory
Recall the four causes: acquisition of permissible things (ihraz al-mubahat), contracts ('uqud), inheritance (irth), and natural increase from owned property (tawallud min al-mamluk). Assign each as voluntary (ihraz and contracts) or compulsory (inheritance and natural increase).
Natural increase includes offspring of livestock, fruits of trees, etc.
3
Assign each cause to creative or transferring
Now apply the second division: creative causes (munshi'a) bring ownership into existence for the first time – these are ihraz and tawallud. Transferring causes (naqila) move pre-existing ownership – these are inheritance (which transfers from the deceased) and contracts.
Contracts are only transferring for ownership, as the seller must own the object.
4
Recognize the special role of contracts
The passage notes a subtlety: while contracts only transfer ownership, they can create obligations (such as the seller's duty to deliver). This is important for fatwa because a contract that attempts to create ownership where none exists would be void.
This distinction prevents confusion between property law and contract law.

Source Text

الفصل الرابع والعشرون
تقسيم الملك وتقسيم أسبابه
المبحث الأول : تقسيم أسباب الملك
١/٢٤ - يتضح بعدما أسلفنا بيانه عن أسباب الملكية الأربعة أنَّ هذه الأسباب تقسم باعتبارين:
۱ - باعتبار وجود الاختيار وعدمه فيها.
۲ - باعتبار أثرها النوعي.
أولاً من حيث الاختيار وعدمه :
فمن حيث الاختيار تنقسم أسباب الملكية إلى نوعين اختيارية وجبرية .
أ - فالأسباب الاختيارية هي ما كان الإنسان مختاراً في إيجادها وهي سببان إحراز المباحات، والعقود.
ب - والأسباب الجبرية ما ليس له فيها اختيار؛ وهي سببان أيضاً: الإرث، والتولد من المملوك
ثانياً : من حيث الأثر النوعي:
وأما من حيث الأثر النوعي فتنقسم أسباب الملكية أيضاً إلى نوعين :
أسباب مُنْشِئة للملك، وأسباب ناقلة له :
أ - فالأسبابُ المُنْشِئَة : التي توجد ملكاً على شيء غير مملوك قبلها؛ وهي إحراز المباحات، والتولد من المملوك.
ب - والأسباب الناقلة : هي التي تتعلق بملك موجود قبلها، فتنقله من حوزة إلى حوزة وهي : الخلفية، والعقود (۱)
المبحث الثاني : تقسيم الملك
٢/٢٤ - وأما الملك نفسه فينقسم أيضاً باعتبارين :
۱ - باعتبار محله أي الشيء الذي يرد عليه الملك ويتعلق به .
۲ - باعتبار صورته أي كيفية تعلقه بالمملوك.
٣/٢٤ - المطلب الأول: من حيث المحل
فمن حيث المحل ينقسم الملك إلى ثلاثة أنواع : ملك العين، وملك المنفعة، وملك الدين.
أولاً : فأما ملك العين ويسمى أيضاً ملك الرقبة، فهو أن تكون ذات الشيء ومادته مملوكة، وذلك كملك الأموال المنقولة من متاع
(1) يعرفون العقد في الاصطلاح القانوني بأنه : اتفاق إرادتين على إنشاء حق أو على نقله أو على إنهائه كما تقدم (ر: ف ٤/٢٣). لكن يجب أن يلحظ في هذا المقام أن العقد لا ينحصر أثره في حق الملكية بل هو سبب : للالتزامات وعلى هذا أ - فبالنسبة إلى سببية العقد في الملكية هو سبب ناقل أبداً لا منشىء لأن العاقد أو المعقود باسمه، كالبائع أو الموكل مثلاً، يجب أن يكون قبل العقد مالكاً للشيء الذي يريد تمليكه، فإن فاقد الشيء لا يعطيه.
ب - أما بالنسبة إلى سببية العقد في الالتزامات فقد يكون ناقلاً، وقد يكون منشئاً : - فالبيع كما ينقل ملكية المبيع إلى المشتري، ينشىء به البائع على نفسه التزاماً بتسليم المبيع، وضمان العيب إلخ.. والكفالة تنشىء التزاماً جديداً على الكفيل بتأدية الدين المكفول عن غيره، إلى جانب التزام المدين الذي هو باق على حاله . والحوالة تنقل التزام تأدية الدين إلى صاحبه عن عهدة المدين المحيل إلى عهدة المحال عليه فيصبح هذا الأخير هو وحده المكلف بهذه التأدية تجاه الدائن.
Chapter Twenty-Four: Division of Ownership and Division of Its Causes. First Section: Division of the Causes of Ownership. 1/24 - It is clear from what we have previously explained about the four causes of ownership that these causes are divided according to two considerations: 1 - According to the presence or absence of choice in them. 2 - According to their qualitative effect. First: Regarding choice and its absence: From the perspective of choice, the causes of ownership are divided into two types: voluntary and compulsory. A - Voluntary causes are those in which a person has a choice in bringing them about, and they are two: acquisition of permissible (unowned) things, and contracts. B - Compulsory causes are those in which he has no choice; they are also two: inheritance, and offspring from owned property. Second: Regarding qualitative effect: As for the qualitative effect, the causes of ownership are also divided into two types: causes that create ownership, and causes that transfer it. A - The creative causes are those that create ownership of something not owned before; they are acquisition of permissible things and offspring from owned property. B - The transfer causes are those that pertain to pre-existing ownership, transferring it from one domain to another; they are succession (khalafiyya) and contracts (1). Second Section: Division of Ownership. 2/24 - As for ownership itself, it is also divided according to two considerations: 1 - According to its subject, i.e., the thing to which ownership is directed and to which it attaches. 2 - According to its form, i.e., the manner of its attachment to the owned thing. 3/24 - First Sub-section: Regarding the subject. From the perspective of subject, ownership is divided into three types: ownership of a 'ayn (specific object), ownership of a usufruct (manfa'a), and ownership of a debt (dayn). First: As for ownership of a 'ayn, also called ownership of the corpus (raqaba), it is that the essence and substance of the thing is owned, such as ownership of movable property... (1) They define a contract in legal terminology as: an agreement of two wills to create a right, transfer it, or terminate it, as previously mentioned (see § 4/23). However, it must be noted here that the effect of a contract is not limited to ownership rights; rather, it is also a cause for obligations. On this basis: A - Regarding the causality of contracts for ownership, it is always a transferring cause, never a creative one, because the contractor or the one in whose name the contract is made, such as a seller or an agent, must be the owner of the thing he wants to transfer ownership of before the contract, for one who does not possess something cannot give it. B - As for the causality of contracts for obligations, it may be transferring or creative: - Sale, as it transfers ownership of the sold item to the buyer, creates an obligation on the seller to deliver the sold item, guarantee against defects, etc. - Guarantee (kafala) creates a new obligation on the guarantor to pay the guaranteed debt on behalf of another, alongside the debtor's existing obligation. - Transfer of debt (hawala) transfers the obligation to pay the debt from the account of the transferor debtor to the transferee, so that the latter becomes solely responsible for payment to the creditor.
Source pages p. 325

Commentary
This passage is a systematic classification of ownership and its causes from a modern Hanafi legal theory textbook (Al-Madkhal al-Fiqhi al-‘Amm by Mustafa Ahmad al-Zarqa). The author divides the causes of ownership (أسباب الملكية) into two main categories: voluntary (اختيارية) and compulsory (جبرية). The voluntary causes are two: acquisition of permissible unowned things (إحراز المباحات) and contracts (العقود). The compulsory causes are also two: inheritance (الإرث) and natural increase from owned property (التولد من المملوك), such as offspring of livestock or fruit from trees. Then, from the perspective of their effect, causes are divided into creative (منشئة) and transferring (ناقلة). Creative causes bring ownership into existence for the first time; they include acquisition of permissible things and natural increase. Transferring causes move pre-existing ownership from one person to another; they include succession (الخلفية) such as inheritance and testament, and contracts. The author notes an important point: contracts are never creative of ownership; they only transfer it because the transferor must already own the object. However, contracts can create obligations – an important distinction. The second part divides ownership itself by its subject (محل) into three: ownership of a 'ayn (عين/رقبة) i.e., the physical object; ownership of usufruct (منفعة); and ownership of a debt (دين). This classification is foundational in Hanafi fiqh and shapes rules about sale, lease, inheritance, and debts.

Key Terms
أسباب الملك (asbab al-milk): Legal causes through which ownership is acquired or transferred.
إحراز المباحات (ihraz al-mubahat): Taking possession of permissible things that are not owned by anyone.
تولد من المملوك (tawallud min al-mamluk): Natural increase from owned property, such as offspring of animals or fruits of trees.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following is both a voluntary and creative cause of ownership?
A) Inheritance
B) Contracts
C) Acquisition of permissible things
D) Natural increase

Key Takeaway
Ownership is acquired through only four causes: two voluntary (ihraz, contracts) and two compulsory (inheritance, natural increase), with the latter being creative and the former transferring except for ihraz which is creative.

Types of Ownership by Subject: Ayn, Manfa‘a, and Dayn
Classifying ownership by its subject into three types

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between ownership of a 'ayn (corpus), ownership of a usufruct (manfa'a), and ownership of a debt (dayn), and apply this classification to categorize property in Hanafi fiqh.

Core Idea
Ownership is divided into three types by its subject: ownership of the physical object ('ayn), ownership of the right to use it (manfa'a), and ownership of a debt (dayn).

After classifying the causes of ownership, al-Zarqa turns to the classification of ownership itself based on its subject (mahall). This tripartite division is crucial in Hanafi fiqh for distinguishing between sales of objects, leases, and debts. Understanding it allows the mufti to correctly characterize transactions and their legal effects.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three types of ownership by subject
The passage states that ownership is divided into: ownership of a 'ayn (also called milk al-raqaba), ownership of a manfa'a (usufruct), and ownership of a dayn (debt). Each attaches to a different kind of thing.
These are not mutually exclusive; one can have both 'ayn and manfa'a simultaneously.
2
Define milk al-‘ayn
Milk al-'ayn (or milk al-raqaba) is ownership of the physical substance or corpus of an object. It gives the owner full control, including the right to use, sell, and destroy.
Example: owning a house outright.
3
Define milk al-manfa‘a
Milk al-manfa'a is ownership of the right to use or derive benefit from an object without owning its substance. This is typically established through contracts like lease (ijara).
The usufruct is a separate property right; it can be sold or inherited separately in some cases.
4
Define milk al-dayn
Milk al-dayn is ownership of a debt, i.e., a claim against another person for a specific payment or performance. This is a credit right, not a physical thing.
Example: owning a promissory note.
5
Apply the distinction in transactions
Understanding these types helps the mufti determine whether a transaction is valid. For example, selling a debt (dayn) to a third party is subject to specific rules (e.g., Hawala or sale of debt). Leasing transfers manfa'a but not 'ayn.
This classification is foundational for contract law in Hanafi fiqh.

Source Text

(1) They define a contract in legal terminology as: an agreement of two wills to create a right, transfer it, or terminate it, as previously mentioned (see § 4/23). However, it must be noted here that the effect of a contract is not limited to ownership rights; rather, it is also a cause for obligations. On this basis: A - Regarding the causality of contracts for ownership, it is always a transferring cause, never a creative one, because the contractor or the one in whose name the contract is made, such as a seller or an agent, must be the owner of the thing he wants to transfer ownership of before the contract, for one who does not possess something cannot give it. B - As for the causality of contracts for obligations, it may be transferring or creative: - Sale, as it transfers ownership of the sold item to the buyer, creates an obligation on the seller to deliver the sold item, guarantee against defects, etc. - Guarantee (kafala) creates a new obligation on the guarantor to pay the guaranteed debt on behalf of another, alongside the debtor's existing obligation. - Transfer of debt (hawala) transfers the obligation to pay the debt from the account of the transferor debtor to the transferee, so that the latter becomes solely responsible for payment to the creditor.
Source pages p. 325

Commentary
This passage is a systematic classification of ownership and its causes from a modern Hanafi legal theory textbook (Al-Madkhal al-Fiqhi al-‘Amm by Mustafa Ahmad al-Zarqa). The author divides the causes of ownership (أسباب الملكية) into two main categories: voluntary (اختيارية) and compulsory (جبرية). The voluntary causes are two: acquisition of permissible unowned things (إحراز المباحات) and contracts (العقود). The compulsory causes are also two: inheritance (الإرث) and natural increase from owned property (التولد من المملوك), such as offspring of livestock or fruit from trees. Then, from the perspective of their effect, causes are divided into creative (منشئة) and transferring (ناقلة). Creative causes bring ownership into existence for the first time; they include acquisition of permissible things and natural increase. Transferring causes move pre-existing ownership from one person to another; they include succession (الخلفية) such as inheritance and testament, and contracts. The author notes an important point: contracts are never creative of ownership; they only transfer it because the transferor must already own the object. However, contracts can create obligations – an important distinction. The second part divides ownership itself by its subject (محل) into three: ownership of a 'ayn (عين/رقبة) i.e., the physical object; ownership of usufruct (منفعة); and ownership of a debt (دين). This classification is foundational in Hanafi fiqh and shapes rules about sale, lease, inheritance, and debts.

Key Terms
ملك المنفعة (milk al-manfa'a): Ownership of the usufruct, i.e., the right to use and derive benefit from an object without owning its substance.
ملك الدين (milk al-dayn): Ownership of a debt, a claim against another person for a specific payment or performance.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which type of ownership is transferred in a lease (ijara) contract?
A) Milk al-‘ayn
B) Milk al-manfa‘a
C) Milk al-dayn
D) Both A and B

Key Takeaway
Ownership is classified into three types by subject: milk al-'ayn (corpus), milk al-manfa'a (usufruct), and milk al-dayn (debt), each with distinct legal implications in transactions.

The Contract as a Transferring Cause: Hanafi Ownership Principles
Definition and distinction of transferring causes and contracts' dual role

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between contracts as transferring causes for ownership and contracts as creating or transferring causes for obligations, and apply the Hanafi principle that a contract never creates ownership.

Core Idea
In Hanafi law, a contract never creates ownership — it only transfers pre-existing property from one owner to another.

This passage from Mustafa Ahmad al-Zarqa's al-Madkhal al-Fiqhi al-Amm defines a key structural principle of Hanafi property law: all causes of ownership are either creating or transferring. The author focuses on contracts as the paradigmatic transferring cause, arguing that a contract cannot create ownership because the transferor must already be the owner. However, contracts can both create and transfer obligations. Grasping this distinction is essential for building a coherent understanding of Islamic contract theory.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two categories of ownership causes
The passage divides causes into creating and transferring. Focus on the transferring cause, which is the subject of this lesson.
The creating causes (al-asbab al-munshi'a) are mentioned but not elaborated here.
2
Isolate the definition of a transferring cause
Extract the exact Arabic phrase 'السبب الناقل' and its definition: 'تتعلق بملك موجود قبلها، فتنقله من حوزة إلى حوزة'.
Note the condition: the ownership must pre-exist.
3
Examine the contract's role in ownership
Understand that in Hanafi law, a contract can only transfer ownership; it cannot create it. The contractor must be the owner before the contract.
This is derived from the maxim: 'فإن فاقد الشيء لا يعطيه'.
4
Contrast ownership vs obligations
Observe that while contracts only transfer ownership, they can both create and transfer obligations. Examples: sale creates an obligation to deliver; kafala creates a new obligation; hawala transfers an obligation.
This dual nature is crucial for understanding contractual effects.
5
Apply to examples
Work through the given examples of sale, kafala, and hawala to see the distinction in practice. For each, identify whether ownership or obligation is transferred or created.
Practice with modern parallels like pre-construction sales.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ب - والأسباب الناقلة : هي التي تتعلق بملك موجود قبلها، فتنقله من حوزة إلى حوزة وهي : الخلفية، والعقود (١) (١) يعرفون العقد في الاصطلاح القانوني بأنه : اتفاق إرادتين على إنشاء حق أو على نقله أو على إنهائه كما تقدم (ر: ف ٤/٢٣). أن وظاهر هذا التعريف يقتضي أيضاً أن العقد يعتبر سبباً صالحاً لإنشاء الملك تارة، ولنقله تارة أخرى. لكن يجب أن يلحظ في هذا المقام أن العقد لا ينحصر أثره في حق الملكية بل هو سبب : للالتزامات. وعلى هذا أ - فبالنسبة إلى سببية العقد في الملكية هو سبب ناقل أبداً لا منشىء لأن العاقد أو المعقود باسمه، كالبائع أو الموكل مثلاً، يجب أن يكون قبل العقد مالكاً للشيء الذي يريد تمليكه، فإن فاقد الشيء لا يعطيه. ب - أما بالنسبة إلى سببية العقد في الالتزامات فقد يكون ناقلاً، وقد يكون منشئاً : - فالبيع كما ينقل ملكية المبيع إلى المشتري، ينشىء به البائع على نفسه التزاماً بتسليم المبيع، ويضمان العيب إلخ.. والكفالة تنشىء التزاماً جديداً على الكفيل بتأدية الدين المكفول عن غيره، إلى جانب التزام المدين الذي هو باق على حاله . والحوالة تنقل التزام تأدية الدين إلى صاحبه عن عهدة المدين المحيل إلى عهدة المحال عليه فيصبح هذا الأخير هو وحده المكلف بهذه التأدية تجاه الدائن.
B - The transferring causes: These are the causes that relate to pre-existing ownership and transfer it from one possession to another. They are: inheritance and contracts. (1) They define contract in legal terminology as: an agreement of two wills to create, transfer, or terminate a right, as previously mentioned (see section 4/23). The apparent meaning of this definition also implies that the contract is considered a valid cause for creating ownership sometimes and for transferring it other times. But it must be observed in this context that the effect of a contract is not limited to the right of ownership; rather, it is also a cause for obligations. Accordingly: A) With respect to the causality of the contract in ownership, it is always a transferring cause, not a creating one, because the contractor or the one in whose name the contract is made, such as the seller or the agent, must be the owner of the thing he wishes to transfer before the contract, for he who does not possess a thing cannot give it. B) As for the causality of the contract in obligations, it may be transferring or creating: Sale, while transferring the ownership of the sold item to the buyer, creates an obligation on the seller to deliver the sold item, and guarantee of defects, etc. Guarantee (kafala) creates a new obligation on the guarantor to pay the guaranteed debt on behalf of another, alongside the debtor's obligation which remains unchanged. Assignment (hawala) transfers the obligation to pay the debt from the account of the assignor debtor to the account of the assignee, so that the latter becomes solely responsible for this payment to the creditor.
Source pages p. 326

Commentary
This passage defines transferring causes of ownership as those that operate on already-existing ownership, moving it from one person to another. Two examples are given: inheritance (al-khilafa) and contracts (al-uqud). A lengthy footnote clarifies that a contract, in legal terminology, is an agreement between two parties to create, transfer, or terminate a right. Importantly, the author notes that a contract can never create ownership ex nihilo in the Hanafi view; it always transfers existing ownership. This is because the person disposing (e.g., seller) must be the owner beforehand. However, contracts can both transfer and create obligations: for instance, a sale transfers the ownership of the item and also creates new obligations on the seller to deliver and guarantee. Other examples include kafala (suretyship) which creates a new obligation, and hawala (assignment) which transfers an existing debt obligation. This is a fundamental distinction in Islamic contract law.

Key Terms
السبب الناقل (al-sabab al-naqil): Cause that transfers pre-existing ownership from one possession to another
العقد (al-'aqd): Agreement of two wills to create, transfer, or terminate a right

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the role of a contract in ownership?
A) It can both create and transfer ownership.
B) It is always a transferring cause, never a creating cause.
C) It only creates obligations, not ownership.
D) It only transfers ownership if there is a prior contract.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, a contract is always a transferring cause for ownership, never a creating cause; only obligations can be created or transferred by contract.

Division of Ownership into Types
Classifying ownership by subject matter and completeness

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify any given property right into one of the three types of ownership (milk al-'ayn, milk al-manfa'a, milk al-dayn) and distinguish perfect from imperfect ownership.

Core Idea
Ownership is divided by subject into substance, usufruct, and debt; and by completeness into perfect and imperfect.

This passage from Mustafa Ahmad al-Zarqa's introduction to fiqh presents the foundational classification of ownership in Islamic law. Understanding these categories is essential for analyzing contracts, property rights, and liabilities. The classification proceeds in two dimensions: by what is owned (the subject matter) and by how completely it is owned (the form).

Guided Path
1
Identify the two considerations
Note that ownership is divided according to its subject matter (mahall) and its form (sura). This will frame the rest of the classification.
2
List the three types by subject matter
Ownership can be of the substance ('ayn/corpus), of the usufruct (manfa'a), or of a debt (dayn). Write down the Arabic terms and their definitions.
مِلْك العين, مِلْك المنفعة, مِلْك الدين
3
Provide examples for each type
For each type, recall the examples given: movable/immovable property for 'ayn; lease, loan for manfa'a; sale price, loan repayment, tort compensation for dayn.
Also note the subordination relationship between 'ayn and manfa'a.
4
Distinguish perfect vs. imperfect ownership
Perfect ownership (ملكية تامة) combines both corpus and usufruct. Imperfect ownership (ملكية ناقصة) includes only one, typically usufruct without corpus (e.g., tenant).
The second form of imperfect ownership (corpus without usufruct) is implied but not detailed here.
5
Apply to the special case of deposit
Analyze a deposit (wadi'a): it is milk al-'ayn, not milk al-dayn, because the depositary must return the exact item. If he misuses it, it becomes a debt.
This illustrates the boundary between the two categories.

المبحث الثاني : تقسيم الملك ٢/٢٤ - وأما الملك نفسه فينقسم أيضاً باعتبارين : ۱ - باعتبار محله أي الشيء الذي يرد عليه الملك ويتعلق به . ۲ - باعتبار صورته أي كيفية تعلقه بالمملوك. ٣/٢٤ - المطلب الأول: من حيث المحل . فمن حيث المحل ينقسم الملك إلى ثلاثة أنواع : ملك العين، وملك المنفعة، وملك الدين. أولاً : فأما ملك العين ويسمى أيضاً ملك الرقبة، فهو أن تكون ذات الشيء ومادته مملوكة، وذلك كملك الأموال المنقولة من متاع وحيوان، وكملك العقار، أي الأموال غير المنقولة، من أراض، ودور وحوانيت . ثانياً : وأما ملك المنفعة فهو أن يملك الإنسان حق الاستفادة فقط من الشيء مع المحافظة على عينه كقراءة الكتب، واستعمال الآلات والأدوات وسكنى الدور والحوانيت إلخ .. بطريق الإجارة أو الإعارة، أو بغيرهما من الأسباب المشروعة لتمليك المنافع . هذا، وإن بين ملك العين وملك المنفعة تلازماً وتبعية في أحوال دون أخرى، كما سنراه قريباً في خصائص الملكية (ف ٢/٢٥). ثالثاً - وأما ملك الدين فكمبلغ لأحد في ذمة آخر بسبب مبيع على مشتريه وبدل قرض على مقترضه وقيمة مال متلف على من أتلفه، ونحو ذلك . ولا يسمى ديناً إلا إذا كان المبلغ التزاماً في الذمة . فأما الوديعة من النقود المحفوظة لصاحبها لدى وديع فهي من قبيل ملك العين لأنها أمانة متعينة يجب على الوديع حفظها ثم ردها بذاتها عند الطلب، وليس له أن يأخذها ويرد مثلها . فلو تصرف الوديع بها أصبح غاصباً، وعندئذ يضمنها، فيصير مبلغها دَيْناً في ذمته كسائر الديون يوفى بأداء مثله ٤/٢٤ - الملكية التامة، والملكية الناقصة . هذا، وبالنسبة إلى العين والمنفعة تنقسم الملكية أيضاً انقساماً فرعياً إلى نوعين : ۱ - ملكية تامة، وهي أن يتملك الشخص رقبة الشيء ومنفعته معاً. ٢ - وملكية ناقصة، ولها شكلان الشكل الأول - أن يملك الشخص منافع الشيء دون رقبته .
Second Section: Division of Ownership 2/24 - As for ownership itself, it is also divided according to two considerations: 1) According to its subject matter, i.e., the thing to which ownership pertains and attaches. 2) According to its form, i.e., how it attaches to the owned thing. 3/24 - First Subsection: In terms of subject matter. In terms of subject matter, ownership is divided into three types: ownership of substance, ownership of usufruct, and ownership of debt. First: Ownership of substance, also called ownership of the corpus, is that the essence and material of the thing are owned, such as the ownership of movable property like goods and animals, and the ownership of real estate, i.e., immovable property, such as land, houses, and shops. Second: Ownership of usufruct is that a person owns only the right to benefit from the thing while preserving its substance, like reading books, using tools and instruments, and dwelling in houses and shops, etc., through lease, loan, or other legitimate means of transferring usufructs. Indeed, there is interdependence and subordination between ownership of substance and ownership of usufruct in some cases but not others, as we will see shortly in the characteristics of ownership (section 2/25). Third: Ownership of debt is like an amount owed by one person to another in his liability, such as the price of a sale owed by the buyer, the repayment of a loan owed by the borrower, the value of a damaged property owed by the one who destroyed it, and the like. It is not called a debt unless the amount is an obligation in the liability. As for the deposit of money kept for its owner with a depositary, it is a type of ownership of substance because it is a specific trust (amanah) that the depositary must preserve and then return its exact entity upon demand; he may not take it and return its equivalent. If the depositary disposes of it, he becomes a usurper, and then he becomes liable for it, so its amount becomes a debt in his liability like other debts, paid by delivering its equivalent. 4/24 - Perfect ownership and imperfect ownership. Moreover, with respect to substance and usufruct, ownership is also subdivided into two types: 1) Perfect ownership, which is that a person owns both the corpus of the thing and its usufruct together. 2) Imperfect ownership, which has two forms: The first form is that a person owns the usufruct of the thing without its corpus.
Source pages p. 326

Commentary
This passage presents a systematic classification of ownership in Islamic jurisprudence. Ownership is divided according to two aspects: subject matter (mahall) and form (sura). Regarding subject matter, there are three types: (1) Ownership of substance ('ayn) or corpus (raqaba) – owning the very essence of a thing, whether movable (goods, animals) or immovable (land, houses). (2) Ownership of usufruct (manfa'a) – owning the right to benefit from a thing while keeping the substance intact, obtained through lease (ijara), loan ('ariya), or other legitimate means. The author notes that there is a relationship of interdependence and subordination between the two types, details to follow in later sections. (3) Ownership of debt (dayn) – a claim on another's liability, such as a sale price, loan repayment, or compensation for damage. A debt is a liability in the dhimmah. A deposit (wadi'a) is not a debt; it is ownership of substance because the depositary must return the exact item; if he uses it, he becomes liable and the amount becomes a debt. Finally, ownership can be perfect (tamma) when one owns both the corpus and the usufruct, or imperfect (naqisa) when one owns only the usufruct (e.g., a tenant) or possibly only the corpus (but the text only mentions usufruct without corpus as the first form; the second form is not fully stated, but by implication could be owning corpus without usufruct, such as a mortgaged property). This classification is fundamental in Hanafi law for understanding transactions, rights, and obligations.

Key Terms
ملك العين (milk al-'ayn): Ownership of the substance or corpus of a thing
ملك المنفعة (milk al-manfa'a): Ownership of the right to benefit from a thing without owning its substance
ملك الدين (milk al-dayn): Ownership of a debt or claim in another's liability
ملكية تامة (milkīya tāmma): Perfect ownership combining both substance and usufruct
ملكية ناقصة (milkīya nāqiṣa): Imperfect ownership where one does not have both substance and usufruct

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Perfect ownership of the house
B) Ownership of the substance (milk al-'ayn)
C) Ownership of the usufruct (milk al-manfa'a)
D) Ownership of a debt (milk al-dayn)

Key Takeaway
Ownership in Islamic law is classified by subject into substance, usufruct, and debt, and by completeness into perfect and imperfect; this classification is essential for understanding property rights and transactions.

The Concept of Incomplete Ownership: Owning Manfa'a without Raqaba
Describes the first form of incomplete ownership and its four acquisition methods.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the four classical methods of acquiring ownership of benefits (manfa'a) without substance (raqaba) in Hanafi fiqh and explain how incomplete ownership differs from complete ownership.

Core Idea
Incomplete ownership splits rights: you can own the benefit without owning the substance.

In this passage, Mustafa Ahmad al-Zarqa introduces the most common form of incomplete ownership: the ownership of benefits alone. This concept is fundamental in Hanafi property law, as it allows for leasing, lending, endowments, and bequests of usufruct. Let's explore the four methods by which this type of ownership is acquired.

Guided Path
1
Identify the concept
Define incomplete ownership (al-milkiyya al-naqisa) as the separation of benefit (manfa'a) from substance (raqaba). Recognize that this is a legitimate Shari'a classification.
2
List the four methods
From the passage, extract the four methods: ijarah (lease), i'arah (loan), waqf (endowment), and wasiyya bi al-manfa'a (bequest of usufruct). Note that each is a distinct legal cause for acquiring manfa'a.
These methods cover contracts and unilateral acts.
3
Understand the acquisition mechanism
For each method, understand how the benefit is transferred: ijarah via contract with rent, i'arah via gratuitous permission, waqf via dedication, wasiyya via bequest.
In waqf, the beneficiary gets the usufruct, but the waqf itself is not owned by anyone.
4
Distinguish from complete ownership
Complete ownership (milkiyya tamma) includes both raqaba and manfa'a. In incomplete ownership, the owner of the benefit cannot sell or dispose of the substance; that remains with the owner of the raqaba.
Example: a tenant in ijarah cannot sell the house.
5
Connect to modern law
The footnote mentions the modern civil code usufruct. Recognize that it is a parallel concept, but note differences in duration and legal construction.
Modern usufruct is often for life; ijarah can be for a fixed term.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الباب الخامس : نظرية الملكية في الفقه
وهذا الشكل أكثر الحالات الواقعية للملكية الناقصة.
·
وتستفاد ملكية المنافع وحدها بإحدى طرق أربعة، وهي الإجارة (۱) ،
والإعارة، والوقف والوصية لأحد بالمنفعة كسكني دار أو غلة كرم
ونحو ذلك (٢).
ويُلحظ هنا أنه يدخل في الإجارة حقوق القرار التي تنشأ على عقارات الأوقاف بعقد خاص، كعقد التحكير في الأراضي الموقوفة، وكعقد الإجارتين في المباني الوقفية المتوهنة عندما لا يكون في الوقف إمكان مالي لتعميرها . فعقد الحكر والإجارتين يقوم كل منهما على أساس إجارة طويلة تمنح المستأجر حق الانتفاع الدائم بالعقار الموقوف لقاء أجرة معجلة تقارب قيمة العقار، وأجرة مؤجلة سنوية ضئيلة رمزية لحفظ علاقة الوقف، كما سنرى في الجزء الثالث من هذه السلسلة الفقهية .
(٢) يلحظ هنا أيضاً أن القانون المدني الجديد، وقبله قانون الملكية العقارية ذو الرقم ٣٣٣٩ / لدينا قد سوغا إنشاء حق انتفاع على العقارات بعقد خاص بين المالك والمنتفع مدى حياة هذا الأخير، وقررا فيه بين الطرفين حقوقاً وواجبات مفصلة .
Chapter Five: Theory of Ownership in Fiqh. This form is the most common actual case of incomplete ownership. The ownership of benefits alone is acquired by one of four methods: ijarah (lease), i'arah (loan), waqf (endowment), and bequest to someone of the benefit, such as dwelling in a house or produce of a vineyard, etc. It is noted here that ijarah includes the rights of permanence that arise on waqf properties through a special contract, such as the contract of hikr on endowed lands, and the contract of ijaratayn on dilapidated endowment buildings when the waqf lacks financial capacity for reconstruction. The hikr and ijaratayn contracts are each based on a long-term lease granting the tenant permanent usufruct of the endowed property in exchange for an immediate rent approximating the property's value and a small annual deferred rent symbolically maintaining the waqf relationship, as will be seen in Part Three of this fiqh series. It is also noted here that the new Civil Code, and before it the Real Property Law No. 3339 in our country, have permitted the creation of a usufruct right on real estate by a special contract between the owner and the beneficiary for the latter's lifetime, and have detailed rights and obligations between the two parties.
Source pages p. 328

Commentary
This passage introduces the first form of incomplete ownership (al-milkiyya al-naqisa), where a person owns the usufruct (manfa'a) of an asset without owning its substance (raqaba). The author states that this is the most common real-world scenario of incomplete ownership. He lists four classical methods for acquiring the ownership of benefits alone: (1) ijarah (leasing), (2) i'arah (gratuitous loan for use), (3) waqf (endowment, where the beneficiary gets the usufruct), and (4) wasiyya bi al-manfa'a (bequest of the usufruct, e.g., the right to live in a house or receive the produce of a vineyard). The author then expands on ijarah, noting that it includes certain contracts on waqf properties like hikr (ground rent) and ijaratayn (double lease) which are long-term leases that effectively grant permanent usufruct in exchange for an upfront payment approximating the property's value and a nominal annual rent. These contracts are legal stratagems (hiyal) to circumvent the prohibition of selling waqf property while allowing its development. Finally, the footnote draws a parallel with modern positive law, mentioning that the new Civil Code and the Real Property Law allow creating a usufruct right by contract for the beneficiary's lifetime.

Key Terms
الملكية الناقصة (al-milkiyya al-naqisa): Ownership that is incomplete, where the owner does not have full authority over both substance and benefit; here specifically ownership of benefits without substance.
المنفعة (al-manfa'a): The usufruct or benefit derived from an asset, such as dwelling in a house or consuming the produce of a vine.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following is NOT a method of acquiring ownership of benefits mentioned in the passage?
A) Ijarah
B) I'arah
C) Hibah
D) Wasiyya

Key Takeaway
The ability to own benefits without substance is a cornerstone of Hanafi property law, enabling flexible transactions like leasing and endowments.

Special Waqf Contracts: Hikr and Ijaratayn as Forms of Incomplete Ownership
Describes the first form of incomplete ownership and its four acquisition methods.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain how hikr and ijaratayn contracts function as long-term leases on waqf properties to effectively grant permanent usufruct, and identify their modern legal parallels.

Core Idea
Hikr and ijaratayn allow waqf properties to be developed permanently without selling the endowment.

The passage expands on ijarah by introducing two special contracts used on waqf properties: hikr (ground lease) and ijaratayn (double lease). These contracts were developed as legal stratagems to circumvent the prohibition of selling waqf assets while enabling their productive use. Let's examine their structure and rationale.

Guided Path
1
Context: the waqf problem
Waqf properties often become dilapidated with no funds for repair. Selling waqf is prohibited. These contracts solve this by attracting private capital.
Waqf is perpetual; its substance cannot be sold or inherited.
2
Identify the contracts
Hikr is used on endowed lands; ijaratayn on endowed buildings. Both are subcategories of ijarah.
Hikr literally means 'appropriation' of the land's use.
3
Mechanism: payment structure
The tenant pays a large upfront rent (mu'ajjala) approximating the property's value, plus a small annual deferred rent (mu'ajjala) symbolically preserving the waqf relationship.
The upfront rent provides capital for development; the annual rent acknowledges the waqf's ownership.
4
Legal effect: permanent usufruct
The tenant gets the right to use and benefit from the property permanently (or for a very long term). The waqf retains substance ownership (raqaba). This is a form of incomplete ownership.
The tenant cannot sell the land/building because they don't own the substance.
5
Modern parallel
The footnote mentions the civil code usufruct right. In modern law, similar long-term leases exist (e.g., ground leases, construction leases). The mufti must align them with Shari'a categories.
Usufruct is often for life; hikr/ijaratayn may be perpetual.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الباب الخامس : نظرية الملكية في الفقه
وهذا الشكل أكثر الحالات الواقعية للملكية الناقصة.
·
وتستفاد ملكية المنافع وحدها بإحدى طرق أربعة، وهي الإجارة (۱) ،
والإعارة، والوقف والوصية لأحد بالمنفعة كسكني دار أو غلة كرم
ونحو ذلك (٢).
ويُلحظ هنا أنه يدخل في الإجارة حقوق القرار التي تنشأ على عقارات الأوقاف بعقد خاص، كعقد التحكير في الأراضي الموقوفة، وكعقد الإجارتين في المباني الوقفية المتوهنة عندما لا يكون في الوقف إمكان مالي لتعميرها . فعقد الحكر والإجارتين يقوم كل منهما على أساس إجارة طويلة تمنح المستأجر حق الانتفاع الدائم بالعقار الموقوف لقاء أجرة معجلة تقارب قيمة العقار، وأجرة مؤجلة سنوية ضئيلة رمزية لحفظ علاقة الوقف، كما سنرى في الجزء الثالث من هذه السلسلة الفقهية .
(٢) يلحظ هنا أيضاً أن القانون المدني الجديد، وقبله قانون الملكية العقارية ذو الرقم ٣٣٣٩ / لدينا قد سوغا إنشاء حق انتفاع على العقارات بعقد خاص بين المالك والمنتفع مدى حياة هذا الأخير، وقررا فيه بين الطرفين حقوقاً وواجبات مفصلة .
Chapter Five: Theory of Ownership in Fiqh. This form is the most common actual case of incomplete ownership. The ownership of benefits alone is acquired by one of four methods: ijarah (lease), i'arah (loan), waqf (endowment), and bequest to someone of the benefit, such as dwelling in a house or produce of a vineyard, etc. It is noted here that ijarah includes the rights of permanence that arise on waqf properties through a special contract, such as the contract of hikr on endowed lands, and the contract of ijaratayn on dilapidated endowment buildings when the waqf lacks financial capacity for reconstruction. The hikr and ijaratayn contracts are each based on a long-term lease granting the tenant permanent usufruct of the endowed property in exchange for an immediate rent approximating the property's value and a small annual deferred rent symbolically maintaining the waqf relationship, as will be seen in Part Three of this fiqh series. It is also noted here that the new Civil Code, and before it the Real Property Law No. 3339 in our country, have permitted the creation of a usufruct right on real estate by a special contract between the owner and the beneficiary for the latter's lifetime, and have detailed rights and obligations between the two parties.
Source pages p. 328

Commentary
This passage introduces the first form of incomplete ownership (al-milkiyya al-naqisa), where a person owns the usufruct (manfa'a) of an asset without owning its substance (raqaba). The author states that this is the most common real-world scenario of incomplete ownership. He lists four classical methods for acquiring the ownership of benefits alone: (1) ijarah (leasing), (2) i'arah (gratuitous loan for use), (3) waqf (endowment, where the beneficiary gets the usufruct), and (4) wasiyya bi al-manfa'a (bequest of the usufruct, e.g., the right to live in a house or receive the produce of a vineyard). The author then expands on ijarah, noting that it includes certain contracts on waqf properties like hikr (ground rent) and ijaratayn (double lease) which are long-term leases that effectively grant permanent usufruct in exchange for an upfront payment approximating the property's value and a nominal annual rent. These contracts are legal stratagems (hiyal) to circumvent the prohibition of selling waqf property while allowing its development. Finally, the footnote draws a parallel with modern positive law, mentioning that the new Civil Code and the Real Property Law allow creating a usufruct right by contract for the beneficiary's lifetime.

Key Terms
الحكر (al-hikr): A ground lease contract on waqf land granting the tenant permanent usufruct in exchange for an upfront rent approximating the land's value and a nominal annual rent.
الإجارتين (al-ijaratayn): A double lease contract on waqf buildings where the tenant pays an immediate lump sum (approximating the building's value) and a small annual rent, receiving permanent usufruct.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following best describes the purpose of hikr and ijaratayn contracts?
A) To sell waqf property for quick funding
B) To generate permanent usufruct on waqf property without selling the substance
C) To lend money to the waqf administrator
D) To transfer ownership of the waqf to the tenant after a period

Key Takeaway
Hikr and ijaratayn are legitimate Hanafi devices to use waqf property productively without violating the perpetual nature of waqf.

The Hanafi Distinction Between Milk and Mal
Explains the Hanafi conceptual distinction between ownership and wealth.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi definition of mal, apply the distinction between milk and mal to classify benefits and debts, and explain the practical consequence for contracts like ijarah.

Core Idea
Benefits and debts are owned but not wealth — a key Hanafi distinction shaping contract law.

This passage explains why Hanafi jurists treat benefits and debts as possessions (amlak) but not as wealth (amwal). Understanding this distinction is essential for grasping why certain transfers (like ijarah) are structured differently in Hanafi fiqh. We will explore the definition of mal, the broader concept of milk, and the practical implications for incomplete ownership.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two forms of incomplete ownership via bequest
Read the examples: (1) bequest of benefit to one person, heirs inherit substance; (2) bequest of benefit to one and substance to another. Both create temporary separation of substance and benefit.
The separation ends when the beneficiary's right expires.
2
Extract the Hanafi definition of mal
The passage states: 'mal is what nature inclines to and can be stored for the time of need.' This definition excludes benefits (cannot be stored) and debts (not physically tangible).
Contrast with the common understanding of wealth.
3
Understand why benefits and debts are not mal but are milk
Hanafis classify benefits and debts as owned things (mamlukat) but not as mal. They are legally protected and compensable, but they fail the storability test. Thus, milk is broader than mal.
The term milk here is used as mamluk (owned thing), not ownership itself.
4
Connect to the inter-madhhab dispute and practical consequence
Shafi'is consider benefits as mal because they have value and are compensable. Hanafis respond that compensation is due to their being owned assets, not because they are mal. Practical result: Hanafis forbid independent sale of benefits; they must be transferred via ijarah.
This affects fatwa in modern transactions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصل (٢٤) تقسيم الملك وتقسيم أسبابه
يملكون بالإرث رقبة ذلك الشيء فقط أما منفعته فيملكها الموصى له، ويتأخر إرثها، فيستوفيها الموصى له على حساب المورث لا على حساب الورثة، وتعتبر العين كأنَّها محبوسة على ملك المورث في حق المنفعة الموصى بها.
ثانياً: إذا أوصى مالك العين لشخص بمنفعتها مدة معينة أو مدة حياته، وأوصى لآخر برقبتها، فإنَّ الموصى له بالعين يكون مالكاً لرقبتها وحدها مدة انتفاع الموصى له بالمنفعة حتى تنقضي بانتهاء المدة المحددة له أو بموته.
·
وفي كلتا هاتين الصورتين يكون انفكاك ملك الرقبة عن ملك المنفعة موقوتاً بمدة استحقاق المنتفع للمنفعة فمتى انتهت عادت المنفعة إلى تبعيتها للرقبة، فيصبح مالك الرقبة مالكاً للمنفعة على حكم الأصل (۱). (ر: البدائع ٣٥٢/٧ - ٣٥٤ ، والهداية والدر المختار أول باب الوصية بالخدمة والسكنى والثمرة.
٢٤/ ٥ ـ وفقهاء الحنفية قد اعتبروا المنافع والديون أملاكاً ولم يعتبروها أموالاً. وبذلك فرَّقوا بين مفهوم «الملك» ومفهوم «المال» كما تقدم (ر) ف ۳/۲۳، والمجلة ١٢٥ و ١٢٦ ، ورد المحتار أوائل البيوع، وفتح القدير أول الشركة).
فالملك بمعنى المملوك (۲) هو أعم مفهوماً من المال، لأن المال عند الفقهاء الحنفية ما يميل إليه الطبع ويمكن ادخاره لوقت الحاجة، والمنافع لا يمكن ادخارها والديون ليست مالاً حقيقياً. (۱) الأوضاع القانونية أوجدت انفكاك الانتفاع عن ملك الرقبة بصورة دائمة بالنسبة لأملاك الدولة، كما في حق التصرف بالأراضي الأميرية، فإن رقبتها باقية لبيت المال، وإنما للناس فيها حق التصرف والانتفاع الدائم بكل الوجوه ؛ كما أقرت هذا الانفكاك بصورة موقوتة بمدى حياة المنتفع بطريق التعاقد بعقد خاص ينشيء حقاً عينياً يسمى : حق الانتفاع . (۲) يلحظ هنا أنَّ الفقهاء عندما يقولون بأن المنافع والديون هي من قبيل الملك لا المال إنما يريدون بالملك معنى اسم المفعول، أي المملوك الذي يتعلق به الملك؛ لأن الملك بالمعنى المصدري أي الملكية إنما هي علاقة اختصاصية للإنسان بالأشياء من أموال ومنافع كما تقدم فالمنافع ونحوها هي محل للملكية، أي أنها مملوكات وليست هي الملكية نفسها.
Chapter (24): Division of Ownership and Division of Its Causes. They inherit only the substance of that thing, while its benefit is owned by the legatee, and its inheritance is delayed; the legatee receives it at the expense of the testator, not at the expense of the heirs, and the object is considered as if it is retained on the testator's ownership with respect to the bequeathed benefit. Second: If the owner of the object bequeaths its benefit to someone for a specified period or for his life, and bequeaths its substance to another, then the legatee of the substance owns only its substance during the period of the legatee's enjoyment of the benefit, until it expires by the end of the specified period or his death. In both these scenarios, the separation of ownership of substance from ownership of benefit is temporary for the duration of the beneficiary's entitlement to the benefit; when it ends, the benefit returns to its subordination to the substance, and the owner of the substance becomes the owner of the benefit according to the original rule. (See: Bada'i' 7/352-354, al-Hidayah and al-Durr al-Mukhtar, beginning of the chapter on bequest of service, dwelling, and fruit.) Hanafi jurists have considered benefits and debts as possessions (amlak) and did not consider them wealth (amwal). Thus they distinguished between the concept of milk and the concept of mal, as previously mentioned (see para 3/23, and Majalla articles 125 & 126, Radd al-Muhtar beginning of Buyu', Fath al-Qadir beginning of Sharika). So milk in the sense of 'mamluk' (owned thing) is broader in concept than mal, because according to Hanafi jurists, mal is what nature inclines to and can be stored for the time of need, and benefits cannot be stored and debts are not real mal. Legal provisions have created a permanent separation of usufruct from ownership of substance for state properties, such as the right of disposition over amiri lands, whose substance remains in the treasury, and people have the right of permanent disposition and enjoyment in all ways; and they have also established this separation temporarily for the lifetime of the beneficiary by way of a special contract creating a real right called 'usufruct right'. It is noted here that when the jurists say that benefits and debts are of the category of milk not mal, they mean milk in the sense of the passive participle, i.e., the owned thing to which ownership attaches; because ownership (al-milkiyya) is a special relationship of a person to things, both wealth and benefits, as previously mentioned; so benefits and the like are loci of ownership, i.e., they are owned things, not ownership itself.
Source pages p. 329

Commentary
This passage covers the second form of incomplete ownership: owning the substance (raqaba) without the benefits. The author describes two scenarios arising from bequest (wasiyya). First: a testator bequeaths the benefit of an asset to someone; the heirs inherit only the substance, while the beneficiary gets the benefit for the specified period. Second: the testator bequeaths the benefit to one person and the substance to another; the substance owner must wait until the benefit period ends before he can enjoy it. In both cases, the separation is temporary; once the beneficiary's right ends, the benefit rejoins the substance. The author then explains the Hanafi distinction between milk (ownership/owned thing) and mal (wealth). Hanafis classify benefits and debts as milk (owned things) but not as mal, because mal must be storable and physically tangible. This has practical implications: benefits cannot be sold as a commodity separate from the substance except in specific contracts like ijarah. The footnote adds that modern positive law has created permanent separation for state lands (like amiri lands) and temporary separation by contract (usufruct right). The final note clarifies that when jurists say benefits are 'milk' they mean the passive sense (mamluk, i.e., owned object), not the active sense (milkiyya, i.e., ownership itself).

Key Terms
الرقبة (al-raqaba): The substance or corpus of an owned object, as opposed to its usufruct.
الملك (al-milk): Here used in the sense of the owned thing (mamluk), broader than mal, encompassing benefits and debts.
المال (al-mal): Wealth; according to Hanafis, it is limited to tangible, storable objects that nature inclines to for need.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Hanafi jurists, why are benefits (manafi') not considered mal?
A) They have no legal value.
B) They cannot be stored for future need.
C) They are not owned by anyone.
D) They are intangible and thus invalid.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, mal is defined as storable and tangible; benefits and debts are owned but not mal, affecting how they can be transferred.

Distinct vs Common Property in Hanafi Law
Defines and contrasts distinct and common property with examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between distinct property (al-milk al-mumayyaz) and common property (al-milk al-sha'i'), provide examples, and explain the legal concept of the common share (al-hissa al-sha'i'a).

Core Idea
Distinct property is fully owned individually; common property is owned proportionally by every co-owner in every atom.

Property (milk) in Hanafi fiqh is broadly defined to include physical assets, usufructs, and debts. However, the way ownership attaches to a thing determines the legal consequences. This lesson focuses on the two fundamental forms of property: distinct (mumayyaz) and common (sha'i'), also called joint or co-ownership. Understanding this distinction is crucial for muftis dealing with sales, inheritance, and disputes over shared assets.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types of property
Read the definitions of distinct and common property. Note that distinct property attaches to a specific bounded thing, while common property attaches to an undetermined part of a whole.
The key is whether the owner's right is localized (distinct) or permeates the whole (common).
2
Analyze the common share
Focus on the definition of the common share (hissa sha'i'a): it is a share that runs through every part of the commonly owned asset. Understand that no part is exclusively owned by any co-owner.
This concept is foundational for rules of partition and disposal.
3
Apply examples from the text
Given examples: a whole house vs half a house. Understand why each is distinct or common.
Test yourself with new examples: e.g., owning a specific apartment vs owning 30% of a building.
4
Understand removal of common ownership
When the common property is divided, each co-owner's share becomes distinct. This is the legal basis for partition (qisma).
Partition does not create new ownership; it transforms common into distinct.

على أن الاجتهادين المالكي والشافعي يعتبران المنافع أموالاً كالأعيان بلا فرق. وهذا هو الأوجه والموافق للأنظار القانونية العامة الحديثة. وسنرى في الجزء الثالث من هذه السلسلة الفقهية مزيد بسط وإيضاح للتفريق بين المال والملك في النظر الفقهي وثمرة هذا التفريق (ر: ج ف/ ٦٩ - ٧٣) .

إزالة الشيوع :
(1) يفرق الفقهاء بين القيمة والثمن : فالقيمة هي : العوض الحقيقي الذي يساويه الشيء بين الناس . أما الثمن فهو : البَدَل الذي يتفق عليه المتبايعان للمبيع سواء أكان أقل من قيمته الحقيقية أو أكثر، كما سنرى في عقد البيع من الجزء الرابع الخاص بالعقود المسماة .
Chapter Five: The Theory of Property in Fiqh. Hanafi jurists restrict [property] to material valuables among people, but owned property (al-mamluk) is everything that ownership (al-milkiyya) i.e., exclusive right (al-ikhtisas) attaches to, so it includes physical assets, usufructs, and debts, because usufructs and debts are subject to exclusive right just as physical assets are. However, the Maliki and Shafi'i schools consider usufructs as property like physical assets without distinction. This is the more appropriate [view] and consistent with modern general legal perspectives. We will see in the third part of this fiqh series further elaboration and clarification of the distinction between wealth (al-mal) and property (al-milk) in juristic theory and the fruit of this distinction (cf. vol. 6, pp. 69-73). The Second Subsection: In terms of form. In terms of form, property is also divided into two types: distinct property (milk mumayyaz) and common property (milk sha'i'). First: Distinct property is what attaches to a specific thing with boundaries separating it from others. For example, a person owns a head of sheep, a book, an entire house, a specific floor of a multi-story house, and the like. Second: Common or shared property (al-milk al-sha'i' aw al-musha') is property that attaches to a relative unspecified part of the whole thing, regardless of whether that part is large or small. For example, a person owns half a house, a quarter of a horse, a hundredth or more of land, and the like. This is what they call the 'common share in a jointly owned thing.' According to the theory of common ownership, every part or atom of the jointly owned property is not exclusive to any of the share holders; rather, the ownership of all of them attaches to it. From this, they define the common share as: 'the share that permeates every part of the jointly owned property' (al-Majalla, Article 139). And the jurists describe it as 'a part embedded in the whole.' It is clear that once the jointly owned property is divided among the partners, the common ownership is removed from the property, and each person's ownership of their share becomes distinct. Common Ownership in Debts: Common ownership is not restricted to the ownership of physical assets; rather, debts may also be jointly and commonly owned, in which case the debt is called a joint debt. It arises for multiple persons in the liability of another due to a single cause. For example, if two persons sell to a third person a jointly owned thing with a single contract, or if this [third] person destroys a jointly owned thing of theirs, then the price in the case of sale, and likewise the compensation in the case of destruction, becomes a joint debt for them in the liability of the other. That is, their rights in his liability are intermingled notionally, like the common ownership of share holders in a jointly owned physical asset. If the cause of the debt is not single—as if each partner sold his share from the jointly owned property with an independent contract, or the destroyer destroyed a distinct non-jointly owned asset for each of them—then the price or compensation in his liability is not a joint debt; rather, the debt of each of them is independent from the other. We will soon see in the sixth characteristic of the properties of ownership the practical result of considering a debt as joint or independent (cf. para 10/25). Removal of Common Ownership: (1) The jurists distinguish between compensation (al-qima) and price (al-thaman): Compensation is the real consideration that the thing equals among people. As for price, it is the substitute that the two contracting parties agree upon for the sold item, whether it is less than its real compensation or more, as we will see in the contract of sale in Part Four on named contracts.
Source pages p. 330

Commentary
This passage is a legal-theoretical exposition on the classification of property ownership (al-milk) in Hanafi fiqh. The author begins by distinguishing between wealth (al-mal) and owned property (al-mamluk), noting that Hanafi jurists have a narrower definition of wealth (restricted to material valuables), but owned property includes also usufructs (manafi) and debts. The author favors the Maliki/Shafi'i view that usufructs are directly property, aligning it with modern legal theory. Then the passage divides property (milk) into two types based on form: distinct (mumayyaz) and common (sha'i'). Distinct property attaches to a specific, separately defined object; common property attaches to an undifferentiated share in a whole. The concept of the common share (hissa sha'i'a) is defined as a share that permeates every part of the jointly owned asset, such that no part is exclusively owned by any one co-owner. The passage then extends the concept of common ownership to debts: when multiple creditors have a right in a debtor's liability due to a single cause (e.g., a joint sale), the debt is common (joint) just like common ownership of physical assets. If the cause is separate, each debt is independent. Finally, a short note defines compensation (qima) vs price (thaman) for later use.

Key Terms
الملك المتميز (al-milk al-mumayyaz): Property that attaches to a specific thing with boundaries separating it from others; individual ownership.
الملك الشائع (al-milk al-sha'i'): Property that attaches to a relative unspecified part of a whole; common/co-ownership.
الحصة الشائعة (al-hissa al-sha'i'a): The common share that permeates every part of the jointly owned property.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

B) It is property where each co-owner's ownership attaches to every part of the asset.
C) It is property that cannot be sold without partition.
D) It is property that always arises from inheritance.

Key Takeaway
Distinct property is individually owned in a specific object; common property is owned proportionally in every part of the undivided whole.

Legal Philosophy and Theory
intermediate
6 min
Common Ownership in Debts (Joint Debt)
Explains the extension of common ownership to debts: joint debt definition, examples, and distinction from separate debts.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define joint debt (al-dayn al-mushtarak) in Hanafi fiqh, identify when a debt becomes joint, and explain the practical difference between a joint debt and separate debts.

Core Idea
A debt is joint when multiple creditors claim from a single debtor due to a single cause; their rights intermingle like common property.

The Hanafi concept of common ownership extends beyond physical assets to debts. When multiple people have a right against a single debtor arising from a single transaction or event, the debt is considered joint (mushtarak). This has important implications for how the debt is claimed, repaid, and discharged. This lesson builds on the classification of property to cover this special case.

Guided Path
1
Understand the condition for joint debt
Joint debt requires a single cause (sabab wahid). Example: two partners sell a jointly owned item with one contract; or one person destroys a jointly owned asset of multiple owners.
If each partner sells his own share separately, the debts are independent, not joint.
2
Analyze the effect of joint debt
In joint debt, the creditors' rights are intermingled (mukhtalita) in the debtor's liability, analogous to common ownership in a physical asset. The debtor's payment must be applied proportionally to all creditors.
The distinction matters for claiming in court, bankruptcy, and discharge.
3
Distinguish from separate debts
If the cause is separate, each creditor has an independent debt. The debtor can pay each separately, and one creditor's release does not affect the others.
This is important for drafting contracts and assessing liability.
4
Recognize the practical outcome
The passage indicates that the practical result (natija amaliyya) will be discussed later (section 10/25). At minimum, joint debts cannot be claimed individually in full; they are collective.
Think about how this affects legal proceedings.

Chapter Five: The Theory of Property in Fiqh. Hanafi jurists restrict [property] to material valuables among people, but owned property (al-mamluk) is everything that ownership (al-milkiyya) i.e., exclusive right (al-ikhtisas) attaches to, so it includes physical assets, usufructs, and debts, because usufructs and debts are subject to exclusive right just as physical assets are. However, the Maliki and Shafi'i schools consider usufructs as property like physical assets without distinction. This is the more appropriate [view] and consistent with modern general legal perspectives. We will see in the third part of this fiqh series further elaboration and clarification of the distinction between wealth (al-mal) and property (al-milk) in juristic theory and the fruit of this distinction (cf. vol. 6, pp. 69-73). The Second Subsection: In terms of form. In terms of form, property is also divided into two types: distinct property (milk mumayyaz) and common property (milk sha'i'). First: Distinct property is what attaches to a specific thing with boundaries separating it from others. For example, a person owns a head of sheep, a book, an entire house, a specific floor of a multi-story house, and the like. Second: Common or shared property (al-milk al-sha'i' aw al-musha') is property that attaches to a relative unspecified part of the whole thing, regardless of whether that part is large or small. For example, a person owns half a house, a quarter of a horse, a hundredth or more of land, and the like. This is what they call the 'common share in a jointly owned thing.' According to the theory of common ownership, every part or atom of the jointly owned property is not exclusive to any of the share holders; rather, the ownership of all of them attaches to it. From this, they define the common share as: 'the share that permeates every part of the jointly owned property' (al-Majalla, Article 139). And the jurists describe it as 'a part embedded in the whole.' It is clear that once the jointly owned property is divided among the partners, the common ownership is removed from the property, and each person's ownership of their share becomes distinct. Common Ownership in Debts: Common ownership is not restricted to the ownership of physical assets; rather, debts may also be jointly and commonly owned, in which case the debt is called a joint debt. It arises for multiple persons in the liability of another due to a single cause. For example, if two persons sell to a third person a jointly owned thing with a single contract, or if this [third] person destroys a jointly owned thing of theirs, then the price in the case of sale, and likewise the compensation in the case of destruction, becomes a joint debt for them in the liability of the other. That is, their rights in his liability are intermingled notionally, like the common ownership of share holders in a jointly owned physical asset. If the cause of the debt is not single—as if each partner sold his share from the jointly owned property with an independent contract, or the destroyer destroyed a distinct non-jointly owned asset for each of them—then the price or compensation in his liability is not a joint debt; rather, the debt of each of them is independent from the other. We will soon see in the sixth characteristic of the properties of ownership the practical result of considering a debt as joint or independent (cf. para 10/25). Removal of Common Ownership: (1) The jurists distinguish between compensation (al-qima) and price (al-thaman): Compensation is the real consideration that the thing equals among people. As for price, it is the substitute that the two contracting parties agree upon for the sold item, whether it is less than its real compensation or more, as we will see in the contract of sale in Part Four on named contracts.
Source pages p. 330

Commentary
This passage is a legal-theoretical exposition on the classification of property ownership (al-milk) in Hanafi fiqh. The author begins by distinguishing between wealth (al-mal) and owned property (al-mamluk), noting that Hanafi jurists have a narrower definition of wealth (restricted to material valuables), but owned property includes also usufructs (manafi) and debts. The author favors the Maliki/Shafi'i view that usufructs are directly property, aligning it with modern legal theory. If the cause is separate, each debt is independent. Finally, a short note defines compensation (qima) vs price (thaman) for later use.

Key Terms
الدين المشترك (al-dayn al-mushtarak): A debt owed to multiple creditors arising from a single cause, with their rights intermingled in the debtor's liability.
السبب الواحد (al-sabab al-wahid): The single cause that gives rise to a joint debt; e.g., a single contract of sale for a common asset, or a single act of destruction of common property.
القيمة (al-qima): Compensation; the real market value of a thing.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
A joint debt in Hanafi law requires a single cause and gives creditors intermingled rights, preventing individual claims for the full amount.

Legal Philosophy and Theory
beginner
8 min
Co-ownership in Property and Debts, and Its Removal
Conceptual explanation of shuyu' and its removal.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between co-ownership in tangible assets and debts, identify the conditions for joint debt, and apply the Hanafi legal principles for removing co-ownership.

Core Idea
Co-ownership is a legal hindrance removed by division; joint debts require a single cause.

This passage from al-Zarqa's introduction to fiqh explains the concept of co-ownership (shuyu') in both tangible property and debts, and the legal mechanisms to terminate it. Understanding the distinction between ownership partnership (shirkat al-milk) and contractual partnership (shirkat al-'aqd) is crucial for muftis when advising on inherited assets or joint obligations.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types of co-ownership
Recognize that shuyu' can occur in tangible assets (ayn) and in debts (dayn). Tangible co-ownership arises from shared ownership of a physical asset; joint debt arises from a single cause (e.g., one contract) creating a liability in a third party.
If debts arise from multiple independent causes, they are separate, not joint.
2
Determine the cause of joint debt
A debt is joint only if it originates from one cause—such as two persons selling jointly owned property in one contract. If each sells separately, each debt is independent.
This matters for division: joint debt can only be divided after receipt.
3
Distinguish between shirkat al-milk and shirkat al-'aqd
Shirkat al-milk is co-ownership without agreement to invest jointly; it is a hindrance to disposition. Shirkat al-'aqd is a contractual partnership for profit, which is beneficial.
This distinction affects how one disposes of the property.
4
Apply the removal mechanisms: division and pre-emption
Division (qisma) is the primary method; it can be compelled by court. Pre-emption (shuf'a) indirectly reduces co-ownership by allowing partners to buy out shares sold to outsiders.
Joint debts cannot be divided while still in the liability.
