٣٥٤
Chapter (24): Division of Ownership and Division of its Causes.
From the parts of common property (Majalla article 139) and jurists describe it as: a part embedded in the whole.
It is evident that when common property is divided among partners, co-ownership ceases, and each one's ownership of his share becomes distinct.
7/24 - Co-ownership in Debts:
Co-ownership is not specific to ownership of tangible assets; rather, debts may also have shared, common ownership, and then the debt is called a joint debt. It arises for multiple persons in the liability of another due to a single cause; such as when two sell to another by one contract something jointly owned between them, or this person destroys for them something jointly owned. In the case of sale, the price, and likewise the compensation (1) in case of destruction, becomes a joint debt in the liability of the other, meaning their rights in his liability are conceptually intermingled like the co-ownership of owners of shares in a common tangible asset.
If the cause of the debt were not single—as if each partner sold his share from the common property by an independent contract, or the destroyer destroyed for each of them distinct non-common property—then the price or compensation in his liability would not be a joint debt, but rather the debt of each would be independent from the other.
We will soon see in the sixth characteristic of ownership the practical consequence of considering a debt joint or independent (see section 10/25).
8/24 - Removal of Co-ownership:
(1) Jurists distinguish between compensation (qima) and price (thaman):
Compensation is the real equivalent that the thing is worth among people. As for price, it is the consideration agreed upon by the two contracting parties for the sold item, whether it is less than its real value or more, as we will see in the contract of sale from the fourth part on nominate contracts.
353
It became clear from the above that common ownership always exists in a shared thing. This partnership, if it is only in the tangible asset itself without agreement on its investment by joint work, is called "partnership of ownership" (shirkat al-milk).
Its counterpart is "partnership of contract" (shirkat al-'aqd),
which is that two or more persons contract on investment of capital or work and sharing profit, as in commercial or industrial companies. Partnership of contract is beneficial because it strengthens and expands economic activity and cooperates by capital and work to create the necessary power for many production projects that an individual is often unable to do.
As for partnership of ownership, it is a hindrance to disposition, because the partner cannot physically dispose of his common share in the shared property in a way that harms his partner, such as consuming the asset or changing its form and the like.
Hence, in partnership of ownership, the path of removing co-ownership from common tangible assets is legislated (1)
(2)
And division (qisma) is that primary path, legally and by law, to remove co-ownership from common property and to allocate each partner a distinct portion. If one of the partners refuses division, the court divides it compulsorily upon request of one.
(1) As for joint debts, removal of co-ownership cannot occur while they are in the liability; rather, it is possible in what is received from them, so it is divided after receipt among the joint creditors, and each one's ownership becomes independent and distinct in what he receives after division, as is apparent. See the sixth characteristic of ownership (section 20/25).
(2) It is worth mentioning that pre-emption (shuf'a) in common real estate between two or more persons, when one partner sells his share to a non-partner, is among the measures leading to removal of co-ownership or to its reduction by decreasing the number of common shares and consolidating some ownerships.
Thus pre-emption contributes to the purpose of removing co-ownership indirectly, although it has other objectives in the view of legislation that necessitate the application of pre-emption in cases other than co-ownership of property.
354
Source pages p. 331

Commentary
This passage from al-Zarqa's work on general fiqh introduction explains the concept of co-ownership (shuyu') in property and debts, and the legal means to terminate it. The author first defines common property as a part embedded in the whole, and notes that division dissolves co-ownership. He then addresses co-ownership in debts (7/24): debts can be jointly owned if they arise from a single cause, such as two partners selling a jointly owned item in one contract. If each sells independently, the debts are separate. A footnote distinguishes between real value (qima) and price (thaman). Next, section 8/24 discusses removal of co-ownership. The author distinguishes between two types of partnership: shirkat al-milk (partnership in ownership of a tangible asset without agreement on joint investment) and shirkat al-'aqd (contractual partnership for profit). Shirkat al-milk hinders disposition, so Islamic law provides ways to remove co-ownership, primarily through division (qisma). If a partner refuses, the court can compel division. A footnote notes that joint debts cannot be divided while still in the liability; only after receipt can they be divided. Another footnote mentions that pre-emption (shuf'a) also helps reduce co-ownership indirectly.

Key Terms
شيوع (shuyu'): The state of being common or shared among multiple persons; co-ownership.
شركة ملك (shirkat al-milk): Partnership solely in ownership of a tangible asset, without mutual agreement on joint investment or work.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, when does a debt become a joint debt (dayn mushtarak)?
A) Whenever two or more persons have claims against the same debtor.
B) Only when the debt arises from a single cause, such as a single sale contract or destruction of jointly owned property.
C) When the debt is divided into shares through division.
D) Only when the debt is for the price of a jointly owned asset.

Key Takeaway
Co-ownership in debts requires a single cause; removal of co-ownership is primarily through division, which can be compelled by court, but joint debts may only be divided after receipt.

Legal Philosophy and Theory
intermediate
8 min
Distinguishing Shirkat al-Milk from Shirkat al-Aqd
Taxonomy of partnerships

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish shirkat al-milk from shirkat al-aqd and identify the legal consequences of each type in Hanafi fiqh.

Core Idea
Shirkat al-milk restricts disposal; shirkat al-aqd enables economic cooperation and profit-sharing.

Co-ownership is common in daily life, but not all partnerships are the same. In Hanafi law, a key distinction exists between mere ownership of a shared asset (shirkat al-milk) and contractual partnership for profit (shirkat al-aqd). This lesson unpacks that distinction and its practical implications.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types
Locate in the text the definitions of shirkat al-milk and shirkat al-aqd. Note the trigger: shirkat al-milk is joint ownership without joint work agreement; shirkat al-aqd requires a contract for investment and profit-sharing.
Shirkat al-milk arises from inheritance, purchase, etc.; shirkat al-aqd is voluntary.
2
Define shirkat al-milk
Extract its essential elements: joint ownership in a specific asset (ayn mal) and the absence of an agreement for joint exploitation. Understand its restrictive nature: a partner cannot physically dispose in a way that harms the other.
The restriction is a necessary consequence to protect the other owner.
3
Define shirkat al-aqd
Note its elements: two or more persons contract to invest capital or labor and share profits. Recognize its benefits: strengthening economic activity and enabling projects beyond individual capacity.
This includes partnerships like mudaraba and 'inan.
4
Compare legal effects
List the differences: shirkat al-milk is a hindrance (qayd mu'riq), while shirkat al-aqd is beneficial (nafi'a). In shirkat al-milk, partners cannot force each other to work together; in shirkat al-aqd, cooperation is central.
The legal category changes the rights and obligations.
5
Apply classification
Given a scenario (e.g., two siblings inherit a car), classify as shirkat al-milk unless they agree to a contract for joint use for profit. Determine the ruling on disposal.
A mufti uses this classification to advise on property disputes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الباب الخامس : نظرية الملكية في الفقه
تبين مما تقدّم أنَّ الملكية الشائعة إنما تكون دائماً في شيء مشترك فهذه الشركة إذا كانت في عين المال فقط دون الاتفاق على استثماره بعمل مشترك تسمى : شركة ملك»
وتقابلها «شركة العقد»
وهي أن يتعاقد شخصان فأكثر على استثمار المال أو العمل، واقتسام الربح كما في الشركات التجارية أو الصناعية. وشركة العقد نافعة لأنَّ فيها تقوية وتوسيعاً للفعالية الاقتصادية وتعاوناً بالمال والعمل على إيجاد القوة اللازمة في كثير من المشروعات الإنتاجية التي يعجز عنها الفرد غالباً.
أما شركة الملك فهي قيد معرقل للتصرف، لأن الشريك لا يستطيع أن يتصرف في حصته الشائعة من المال المشترك تصرُّفاً فعلياً يضر بشريكه كاستهلاك العين أو تغيير شكلها ونحو ذلك
ومن ثم شرع في شركة الملك سبيل إزالة الشيوع من الأعيان المالية المشتركة (١)
(۲)
وإنَّ القسمة هي ذلك الطريق الأساسي شرعاً وقانوناً لإزالة الشيوع المال المشترك، واختصاص كل شريك بقسم متميز فإذا أبى أحد الشركاء القسمةَ تُقسم المحكمة جبراً عليه بطلب أحد
الشركاء إذا كان المال قابلاً للقسمة، لا فرق في ذلك بين المنقول والعقار وفقاً للمواد // ۱۱۲۳ / و / ١١٣٠ - ١١٥٢/ من المجلة
وأما إذا كان المال المشترك غير قابل للقسمة فإنَّ فقهاءنا لم يوجبوا فيه البيع الجبري لإزالة الشيوع عند اختلاف الشركاء بل تستمر بينهم شركة الملك ويتهايؤون أي يتناوبون في استعمال المال المشترك. وهذا حكم ما يملك، وأن المهايأة هي شرعاً على يجبر القياس، لأنَّ الإنسان لا بيع وقد بينت المجلة كيفية المهايأة الشرعية وأنواعها من زمانية ومكانية ومن رضائية وقضائية أي اختيارية وجبرية وفصلت أحكامها في المواد ١١٧٤ - ١١٩١ / منها (١) .
٩/٢٤ - غير أن قانون إزالة الشيوع لدينا الصادر في العهد العثماني قانون تقسيم الأموال غير المنقولة قد أوجب إزالة الشيوع في العقار المشترك بطريق القضاء بناء على طلب أحد الشركاء جبراً على سائرهم، سواء أكان قابلاً للقسمة أو غير قابل.
فإن كان قابلاً للقسمة يقسم بينهم قسماً، وفقاً للحكم الفقهي المبين
في المواد / ١١٤٧ - ١١٥٢ / من المجلة .
وإن كان غير قابل للقسمة يُباع بالمزاد العلني ويقسم ثمنه بين الشركاء .
وقد بقي المال المنقول المشترك بمقتضى هذا القانون، على أصل الحكم الشرعي، لا إجبار فيه على إزالة الشيوع عند عدم قابلية القسمة. وإزالة الشيوع من العقار بمقتضى قانون تقسيم الأموال غير المنقولة
(۱) سيأتي قريباً في الفقرة التالية ۱۰/۲٤ أن المهايأة قد أصبحت ملغاة قانوناً في العقارات الزراعية .
Chapter Five: Theory of Ownership in Fiqh. It is clear from the foregoing that common ownership always exists in a shared thing. This partnership, if it is only in the specific property without agreement to invest it with joint work, is called 'shirkat al-milk' (ownership partnership). Its counterpart is 'shirkat al-aqd' (contractual partnership), which is that two or more persons contract to invest capital or labor and share profit, as in commercial or industrial companies. Contractual partnership is beneficial because it strengthens and expands economic activity and cooperates with capital and labor to create the necessary strength in many productive projects that an individual is often incapable of. As for ownership partnership, it is a hindering restriction on disposal, because the partner cannot actually dispose of his undivided share in the common property in a way that harms his partner, such as consuming the property or changing its form, etc. Hence, in ownership partnership, a means of removing the commonality from shared material properties was legislated. Partition (qisma) is that primary path, legally and by law, to remove commonality from shared property, and for each partner to be allocated a distinct portion. If one of the partners refuses partition, the court partitions it compulsorily upon the request of one of the partners if the property is divisible, with no difference between movable and immovable according to Articles // 1123 / and / 1130-1152/ of the Majalla. As for undivided property that is indivisible, our jurists did not impose compulsory sale to remove commonality when partners disagree; rather, the ownership partnership continues and they practice muhaya'ah, i.e., they take turns using the shared property. This is the ruling of what is owned, and muhaya'ah is legally obligatory by analogy because a person cannot [sell?]. The Majalla explained the method of legal muhaya'ah and its types: temporal, spatial, consensual, and judicial (i.e., optional and compulsory), and detailed its rulings in Articles 1174-1191 of it (1). 9/24 - However, the Law of Removal of Commonality issued in the Ottoman era, the Law of Partition of Immovable Property, made removal of commonality in jointly owned immovable property compulsory by court order upon the request of one partner, against the others, whether it is divisible or not. If it is divisible, it is partitioned among them according to the fiqh ruling explained in Articles 1147-1152 of the Majalla. If it is indivisible, it is sold by public auction and its price is divided among the partners. Under this law, shared movable property remained on the original shari ruling: there is no compulsion to remove commonality when it is indivisible. Removal of commonality from immovable property is under the Law of Partition of Immovable Property. (1) It will come soon in the following paragraph 10/24 that muhaya'ah has been abolished by law in agricultural immovables.
Source pages p. 332

Commentary
This passage presents the Hanafi theory of ownership and partnership, distinguishing between two types of co-ownership: shirkat al-milk (ownership partnership) which arises from joint ownership of a specific asset without any agreement to work together, and shirkat al-aqd (contractual partnership) which is formed by contract to invest capital or labor for profit. The author notes that shirkat al-milk restricts each partner's ability to dispose of the property because any physical action that harms the other partner is prohibited. To resolve this gridlock, Islamic law introduced the mechanism of qisma (partition). Partition is the primary legal method to terminate co-ownership, giving each partner exclusive ownership of a distinct portion. If the property is divisible (qabil li al-qisma), a partner can compel partition through the court. This applies to both movable and immovable property according to the Majalla. However, if the property is indivisible, classical Hanafi jurists did not impose compulsory sale; instead, they prescribed muhaya'ah (alternation of use), where partners take turns using the property. This is based on qiyas because a person cannot be forced to sell his property. The Majalla details the types of muhaya'ah: temporal, spatial, consensual, and judicial. The passage then notes a modern legislative change: the Ottoman Law of Partition of Immovable Property made removal of commonality compulsory for immovable property, regardless of divisibility. For divisible immovables, partition is carried out; for indivisible immovables, the property is sold by auction and the proceeds divided. Movable property remains under the original Hanafi rule: no compulsion to partition if indivisible. The passage highlights the interaction between classical fiqh and Ottoman codification.

Key Terms
شركة ملك (shirkat al-milk): Partnership in ownership of a specific asset without agreement on joint exploitation
شركة عقد (shirkat al-aqd): Partnership by contract to invest capital or labor and share profits

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following is the distinguishing feature of shirkat al-milk according to the passage?
A) It is formed by a written contract
B) It involves joint work for profit
C) It exists in the specific asset without agreement on joint exploitation
D) It is only valid for immovable property

Key Takeaway
Hanafi law distinguishes between passive co-ownership (shirkat al-milk) and active partnership (shirkat al-aqd), with different legal effects on disposal and profit-sharing.

Legal Philosophy and Theory
intermediate
10 min
Partition and Muhaya'ah: Remedies for Co-ownership
Legal remedies for co-ownership

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rules for partition (qisma) and muhaya'ah to resolve co-ownership disputes.

Core Idea
Partition is compulsory for divisible property; muhaya'ah (alternation) is the remedy for indivisible property.

Co-ownership can lead to deadlock. Hanafi fiqh provides two remedies: partition (qisma) to give each owner a separate share, and muhaya'ah (alternation of use) when the property cannot be physically divided. This lesson explores the conditions and procedures for each, as well as the impact of Ottoman codification.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the problem
Understand that shirkat al-milk restricts disposal. The solution is removal of commonality (izalat al-shuyu'). Identify the two remedies: qisma and muhaya'ah.
The text says: 'شرع في شركة الملك سبيل إزالة الشيوع'.
2
Explain partition (qisma)
Define qisma as the primary method: each partner gets a distinct portion. Note that it is compulsory upon request if the property is divisible (qabil li al-qisma). No distinction between movable and immovable according to the Majalla.
Articles 1123, 1130-1152 of the Majalla.
3
Explain muhaya'ah
For indivisible property, classical Hanafi jurists prescribed muhaya'ah (alternation of use) instead of forced sale. This can be temporal or spatial, consensual or judicial. The text mentions it is obligatory by analogy (qiyas) because a person cannot be forced to sell.
The passage says: 'المهايأة هي شرعاً على يجبر القياس، لأنَّ الإنسان لا بيع' (though the Arabic is slightly corrupted, the meaning is clear: muhaya'ah is imposed because forced sale is not allowed).
4
Note the Ottoman legal change
The Ottoman Law of Partition of Immovable Property made removal of commonality compulsory for immovable property regardless of divisibility. If divisible, partition; if indivisible, sale by auction. Movable property retained the classical rule.
This is an example of tatbiq (application) changing the ruling for immovable property.
5
Apply to a scenario
Given a case of co-ownership of a house (divisible) or a car (indivisible), determine whether partition or muhaya'ah applies, considering the property type and the applicable law (classical fiqh vs Ottoman statute).
For fatwa today, a mufti considers the local civil code but may refer to Hanafi fiqh as the basis.

Source Text

Legal Philosophy and Theory

Ottoman Codification and Its Impact on Hanafi Property Law
Historical legal change in co-ownership rules

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to analyze how the Ottoman Law on Partition of Immovable Property modified the classical Hanafi rulings on removal of commonality.

Core Idea
Ottoman law changed the rule for immovables: compulsory sale now possible, but movables remain under classical Hanafi fiqh.

Classical Hanafi fiqh prioritized preserving co-ownership by muhaya'ah for indivisible property. However, the Ottoman codification introduced a significant change: for immovable property, a single partner could force a sale. This lesson explores that development and its implications for a mufti today.

Guided Path
1
Recall the classical rule
Review that classical Hanafi fiqh prohibits forced sale of indivisible property; muhaya'ah is prescribed.
The basis: 'الإنسان لا بيع' (a person cannot be forced to sell).
2
Identify the Ottoman law
The Law on Partition of Immovable Property (Qanun Taqsim al-Amwal Ghayr al-Manqula) made removal of commonality compulsory for immovables, whether divisible or not.
Passage: 'أوجب إزالة الشيوع في العقار المشترك بطريق القضاء... سواء أكان قابلاً للقسمة أو غير قابل'.
3
Understand the dual system
For divisible immovables, partition according to fiqh; for indivisible immovables, sale by auction. For movables, the classical rule remains unchanged.
Passage: 'بقي المال المنقول المشترك... على أصل الحكم الشرعي'.
4
Analyze the rationale
The law aimed to facilitate economic transactions and remove obstacles from land ownership, reflecting a policy of public benefit (maslaha) overriding the classical reluctance to force sale.
This is an example of tatbiq (application) adapting fiqh to practical needs.
5
Consider the mufti's role
A mufti must be aware of both the classical ruling and the positive law that may supersede it in certain jurisdictions. The fatwa may differ depending on whether the question involves movable or immovable property.
The mufti should also note that muhaya'ah has been abolished for agricultural immovables (passage footnote).

(۱) سيأتي قريباً في الفقرة التالية ۱۰/۲٤ أن المهايأة قد أصبحت ملغاة قانوناً في العقارات الزراعية .
Chapter Five: Theory of Ownership in Fiqh. It is clear from the foregoing that common ownership always exists in a shared thing. This partnership, if it is only in the specific property without agreement to invest it with joint work, is called 'shirkat al-milk' (ownership partnership). Its counterpart is 'shirkat al-aqd' (contractual partnership), which is that two or more persons contract to invest capital or labor and share profit, as in commercial or industrial companies. Contractual partnership is beneficial because it strengthens and expands economic activity and cooperates with capital and labor to create the necessary strength in many productive projects that an individual is often incapable of. As for ownership partnership, it is a hindering restriction on disposal, because the partner cannot actually dispose of his undivided share in the common property in a way that harms his partner, such as consuming the property or changing its form, etc. Hence, in ownership partnership, a means of removing the commonality from shared material properties was legislated. Partition (qisma) is that primary path, legally and by law, to remove commonality from shared property, and for each partner to be allocated a distinct portion. If one of the partners refuses partition, the court partitions it compulsorily upon the request of one of the partners if the property is divisible, with no difference between movable and immovable according to Articles // 1123 / and / 1130-1152/ of the Majalla. As for undivided property that is indivisible, our jurists did not impose compulsory sale to remove commonality when partners disagree; rather, the ownership partnership continues and they practice muhaya'ah, i.e., they take turns using the shared property. This is the ruling of what is owned, and muhaya'ah is legally obligatory by analogy because a person cannot [sell?]. The Majalla explained the method of legal muhaya'ah and its types: temporal, spatial, consensual, and judicial (i.e., optional and compulsory), and detailed its rulings in Articles 1174-1191 of it (1). 9/24 - However, the Law of Removal of Commonality issued in the Ottoman era, the Law of Partition of Immovable Property, made removal of commonality in jointly owned immovable property compulsory by court order upon the request of one partner, against the others, whether it is divisible or not. If it is divisible, it is partitioned among them according to the fiqh ruling explained in Articles 1147-1152 of the Majalla. If it is indivisible, it is sold by public auction and its price is divided among the partners. Under this law, shared movable property remained on the original shari ruling: there is no compulsion to remove commonality when it is indivisible. Removal of commonality from immovable property is under the Law of Partition of Immovable Property. (1) It will come soon in the following paragraph 10/24 that muhaya'ah has been abolished by law in agricultural immovables.
Source pages p. 332

Commentary
This passage presents the Hanafi theory of ownership and partnership, distinguishing between two types of co-ownership: shirkat al-milk (ownership partnership) which arises from joint ownership of a specific asset without any agreement to work together, and shirkat al-aqd (contractual partnership) which is formed by contract to invest capital or labor for profit. The author notes that shirkat al-milk restricts each partner's ability to dispose of the property because any physical action that harms the other partner is prohibited. To resolve this gridlock, Islamic law introduced the mechanism of qisma (partition). Partition is the primary legal method to terminate co-ownership, giving each partner exclusive ownership of a distinct portion. If the property is divisible (qabil li al-qisma), a partner can compel partition through the court. This applies to both movable and immovable property according to the Majalla. However, if the property is indivisible, classical Hanafi jurists did not impose compulsory sale; instead, they prescribed muhaya'ah (alternation of use), where partners take turns using the property. This is based on qiyas because a person cannot be forced to sell his property. The Majalla details the types of muhaya'ah: temporal, spatial, consensual, and judicial. The passage then notes a modern legislative change: the Ottoman Law of Partition of Immovable Property made removal of commonality compulsory for immovable property, regardless of divisibility. For divisible immovables, partition is carried out; for indivisible immovables, the property is sold by auction and the proceeds divided. Movable property remains under the original Hanafi rule: no compulsion to partition if indivisible. The passage highlights the interaction between classical fiqh and Ottoman codification.

Key Terms
قانون تقسيم الأموال غير المنقولة (qanun taqsim al-amwal ghayr al-manqula): The Ottoman Law on Partition of Immovable Property, which made removal of commonality compulsory for immovables.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what was the effect of the Ottoman Law on Partition of Immovable Property on movable property?
A) It also made removal of commonality compulsory for movables
B) It left movables under the original shari ruling
C) It abolished muhaya'ah for movables
D) It required movables to be sold if indivisible

Key Takeaway
The Ottoman Law on Partition of Immovable Property modified the classical Hanafi rule for immovables (now compulsory sale possible) but preserved the classical rule for movables.

Legal Philosophy and Theory
intermediate
10 min
Hanafi Ruling on Indivisible Joint Property and Legal Reform via Istihsan
Legal philosophy and historical development of joint property rules

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on indivisible joint property, explain the concept of muhayaah, and evaluate how positive law can override strict qiyas through istihsan for public benefit.

Core Idea
For indivisible joint property, fiqh permits alternating use (muhayaah); positive law can impose forced sale via istihsan.

This passage from al-Madkhal al-Fiqhi al-‘Amm traces the Hanafi position on joint property when it cannot be divided physically. While the school did not mandate forced sale, later Ottoman and Syrian laws introduced compulsory judicial dissolution for public interest. You will see how istihsan (juristic preference) bridges the gap between classical fiqh and modern legislation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the fiqh ruling for divisible vs. indivisible joint property
First, note that if property is divisible, partners can demand a physical division (qisma). If not, Hanafi jurists do not impose a forced sale; instead, co-ownership continues.
This is the default fiqhi position.
2
Understand muhayaah as a division of benefits
Muhayaah is the alternating use of the undivided property, considered a division of benefits (qismat al-manafi) rather than a division of the property itself. It can be temporal or spatial, voluntary or compulsory.
The Mejelle codified its types in Articles 1174-1191.
3
Recognize the shift when positive law imposes forced sale
The Ottoman Law on Division of Immovable Property made judicial removal of joint ownership obligatory even for indivisible property, overriding the fiqh rule. Later, in 1947, this was extended to movable property as well.
This shows the interaction between fiqh and qanun.
4
Analyze the role of istihsan in overriding qiyas
The author notes that forced sale is against strict qiyas (analogy), but istihsan (juristic preference) accepts it because of manifest public interest (maslaha). Istihsan is a recognized source in Hanafi usul.
This illustrates how public policy can modify rulings.
5
Note the current legal outcome and scope of muhayaah
After these laws, compulsory muhayaah became largely unnecessary for immovable property, but may still apply if a partner requests it and no one demands forced sale. For agricultural lands, it was abolished earlier.
Understand the residual role of the fiqh rule.

Chapter (24): Division of Ownership and Division of Its Causes. Partners, if the property is divisible, there is no difference in that between movable and immovable property according to Articles // 1123 / and / 1130 - 1152/ of the Mejelle. And if the joint property is indivisible, then our jurists did not require a forced sale to remove joint ownership when the partners differ, but rather their co-ownership continues and they mutually alternate (yatahaayaoon), i.e., they take turns using the joint property. This is the ruling of what they own, and that muhayaah (alternating use) is legally a division of benefits (qismat al-manafi), because a person is not [forced to] sell. And the Mejelle explained the method of legal muhayaah and its types: temporal and spatial, and consensual and judicial (i.e., voluntary and compulsory), and detailed its rulings in Articles 1174 - 1191 thereof (1). 9/24 - However, the law for removing joint ownership here, issued in the Ottoman era, the Law on Division of Immovable Property, made removal of joint ownership in joint immovable property obligatory by judicial means upon the request of one partner, forcibly upon the others, whether it was divisible or not. If it is divisible, it is divided among them in kind, according to the juristic ruling stated in Articles 1147 - 1152 of the Mejelle. And if it is indivisible, it is sold by public auction and its price is divided among the partners. And movable joint property remained, under this law, on the original legal ruling: no compulsion to remove joint ownership when it is indivisible. And removal of joint ownership of immovable property under the Law on Division of Immovable Property (1) will soon come in the following paragraph 10/24 that muhayaah has become legally abolished for agricultural lands. 355 [PAGE_334] 1: Fifth Chapter: Theory of Ownership in Fiqh. The aforementioned [removal] is within the jurisdiction of our magistrates' courts (1). Finally, in 1366 AH = 1947 CE, Article 3 of our Justices of the Peace Law was amended by Law No. 353 of June 3, 1947, and this amended article generalized the ruling of compulsory judicial removal of joint ownership to movable property as well, in the manner legally followed for joint immovable property: by dividing it if divisible, and selling it if not. And in this is an apparent benefit (maslaha) in removing the problems of co-ownership whenever they arise by natural causes such as inheritance and others. And such a benefit is not rejected by juristic istihsan (juristic preference), even if qiyas (analogy) would reject it. 24/10 - And it must be noted in this regard that after the laws of compulsory judicial removal of joint ownership for immovable property, there remains no need for compulsory muhayaah by way of alternating use of the benefits of the joint thing, unless one partner claims muhayaah and the other partner does not request removal of joint ownership. This is because every joint ownership has become capable of being judicially dissolved in any case: if not by division then by sale and division of the price. (2) (1) Magistrates' courts (al-mahakim al-sulhiyya) in the Syrian judicial system handle small and simple cases; their procedures, time limits, and methods of appeal are abbreviated for speed in resolution, and they are called in Egypt and some other Arab countries: al-mahakim al-juziyya (partial courts) as opposed to al-mahakim al-kulliyya (general courts). (2) We draw attention here that compulsory muhayaah has become abolished for agricultural lands by virtue of Article 1 of Legislative Decree No. 171 issued by the French High Commissioner during the French mandate on March 10, 1926, concerning the dissolution of commonly held lands for the improvement of agriculture. And the text of Article 1 of the said decree is as follows: 'The periodic division among persons who will occupy agricultural lands, orchards, vineyards, gardens, etc., owned in common (musha'), contrary to the instructions mentioned in the title deeds or...'
Source pages p. 333

Commentary
Passage type: legal philosophy and historical development. This passage from Dr. Mustafa Ahmad al-Zarqa's al-Madkhal al-Fiqhi al-'Amm discusses the Hanafi ruling on joint property (shirkat al-milk) when it is indivisible. The author first states the Hanafi position: if the property is divisible, partners can demand a division in kind (qisma), covering both movable and immovable as per the Ottoman Mejelle. If the property is indivisible, the Hanafi school does not impose a forced sale to break the joint ownership. Instead, the partners continue as co-owners and may resort to muhayaah, which is a legal arrangement for alternating use of the property. Muhayaah is defined as a division of benefits (qismat al-manafi) rather than a division of the property itself. The Mejelle codified this with detailed rules. The passage then notes that the Ottoman Law on Division of Immovable Property (a positive law) overrode this principle for immovable property, allowing a partner to force a judicial sale even if the property is not physically divisible. Movable property initially remained under the original Hanafi rule. The text traces further legal amendments: in 1947, the Syrian law extended compulsory removal of joint ownership to movable property as well. The author comments that this is a clear benefit (maslaha) that is accepted by istihsan even if qiyas would reject it. This illustrates a fundamental principle in Hanafi usul al-ifta: istihsan can override qiyas when there is a genuine public interest. Finally, the author notes that with these laws, compulsory muhayaah becomes unnecessary for immovable property, but may still arise if a partner requests muhayaah instead of forced sale. Footnotes explain that for agricultural lands, compulsory muhayaah was abolished earlier by the French mandate.

Key Terms
الملك المشترك (al-mulk al-mushtarak): Property owned by two or more persons in undivided shares (shuyu').
إزالة الشيوع (izalat al-shuyu'): The legal process of terminating joint ownership, either by physical division or by forced sale.
الاستحسان (al-istihsan): Juristic preference that deviates from strict analogy (qiyas) in favor of a ruling that better serves public interest or legal harmony.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the basis for the Syrian law that allows forced sale of indivisible joint property?
A) Qiyas (analogy) from divisible property.
B) Istihsan (juristic preference) due to manifest benefit.
C) Ijma' (consensus) of all schools.
D) A direct Quranic verse.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi fiqh ruling on indivisible joint property (muhayaah) can be overridden by positive law based on istihsan for public benefit, showing the dynamic interaction between fiqh and modern legislation.

Legal Philosophy and Theory

First Characteristic: Ownership of Corpus Entails Usufruct
Explains a fundamental characteristic of ownership and its exceptions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi principle regarding ownership of corpus and usufruct, identify the exceptions, and explain the practical consequence of the theoretical disagreement with the Maliki school.

Core Idea
Owning the corpus of a thing gives you its usufruct by default, but owning the usufruct does not give you the corpus.

This lesson introduces the first fundamental characteristic of ownership in Hanafi fiqh: the inseparable link between owning the substance (ayn) of a thing and its benefit (manfa'ah). This principle underlies countless transactions, from sale to lease to bequest. Understanding this maxim equips you to distinguish between different types of ownership and to apply the correct rulings in cases of bequest and temporary grants.

Guided Path
1
Identify the principle and its reversal
State the rule clearly: ownership of the corpus (milk al-ayn) entails ownership of the usufruct (milk al-manfa'ah) immediately or eventually, but owning the usufruct does not entail owning the corpus. This is the 'no vice versa' part.
The principle is: 'ملك العين يستلزم ملك المنفعة ولا عكس'.
2
Understand the rationale
Ownership of things is not an end in itself; the goal is to derive benefit. The Lawgiver established corpus ownership as a support for lawful enjoyment and disposal. Permanent separation would be absurd.
The passage calls permanent separation 'عبث' (absurdity).
3
Recognize the exceptions
The only exceptions are two forms of bequest of usufruct (الوصية بالمنفعة): (1) bequeathing the usufruct for a limited period or life, (2) bequeathing the corpus to one and usufruct to another for a period. In both, separation is temporary.
The exceptions are not permanent; usufruct eventually reverts to corpus owner.
4
Analyze the theoretical disagreement
Some Maliki jurists argue that ownership only falls on usufructs, not substances, because humans cannot truly dispose of essences. The majority (including Hanafis) hold that ownership applies to both. The author notes no practical consequence.
This disagreement does not affect fatwa rulings.
5
Apply the principle to common transactions
In a sale, the buyer owns both corpus and usufruct. In a lease, the lessee owns only usufruct temporarily, not the corpus. A gift of corpus includes usufruct. This principle governs many contracts.
The principle is foundational for distinguishing sale from lease.

Chapter Twenty-Five: Characteristics of Ownership. 1/25 - Ownership in its various types and forms has characteristics by which some types of ownership differ from others. These characteristics are legal capacities and considerations by virtue of which some types of ownership accept what others do not of dispositions and rulings, according to the nature of each type and the objective of the legislative view in it. The jurists did not collect these characteristics of ownership in an independent study, but they appear to the researching jurist from the folds of the juristic rulings that jurisprudence has established in various investigations and occasions connected to ownership and its causes. Below we will present these characteristics indicated by the texts and branches of juristic rulings: 2/25 - The First Characteristic: Ownership of the corpus (ayn) necessarily entails ownership of the usufruct (manfa'ah), but not vice versa. Ownership of the corpus entails ownership of the usufruct, i.e., it follows it immediately or eventually. And not vice versa, meaning that ownership of the usufruct does not entail ownership of the corpus; a person may own the usufruct of a thing without its corpus, as in leasing and lending. That is because ownership of things is not intended for itself; rather the goal is their usufruct; for ownership of the corpus is nothing without the expected usufruct. The Lawgiver established the idea of ownership of the corpus to be a support for complete free enjoyment and lawful disposal, even by consumption. So if we imagine the separation of ownership of the corpus of a thing from the right of usufruct permanently, that is absurdity which legislation is not meant to approve. It only approves it exceptionally in temporary, transient situations that end with the usufructs following the corpus, so that the ownership of the corpus returns to its reasonable fruit. On this basis, there was no cause of ownership in Islamic jurisprudence that necessitates owning the corpus alone without the usufruct except in the two forms of bequest of usufruct mentioned earlier (4/24), namely: - If the owner bequeaths the usufruct of something from his property, such as the residence of his house or garden, to a person for a specified period or for his lifetime. - If he bequeaths the corpus of property to one person and its usufruct to another for a specified period. Then the heirs in the first form, and the legatee of the corpus in the second form, own the corpus only without the usufruct until the right of the legatee of the usufruct ends, then the right of usufruct returns to the owner of the corpus, whether heir or legatee of the corpus. If they are not alive, then to their heirs as previously in the division of ownership. The legal view on the validity of this bequest is that the separation of ownership of the corpus... (Footnote 1: * Until some Maliki jurists go in the philosophy of ownership to a view in which they think that ownership cannot fall on the essences of things and the substance of their matter because disposal of their essence is not in human power; rather it falls on the usufructs of things in a manner that varies in breadth and narrowness, absoluteness and restriction, temporariness and perpetuity. According to these limits, contracts differ in their names and results: sale gives ownership of usufructs absolutely and perpetually, lease gives it restricted and temporary, etc. But the majority of jurists, including the Hanafis, are contrary to this view; they see that ownership falls on the essences and substances of things as it falls on their usufructs. We do not see any practical consequence of this theoretical disagreement (see: al-Milkiyyah wa Nazariyyat al-'Aqd by Professor Abu Zahra, p. 17). (Footnote 2: See what was previously indicated regarding the situation of state lands in our law (4/24 footnote).)
Source pages p. 336

Commentary
This passage begins a discussion on the characteristics of ownership in Islamic jurisprudence. The author notes that these characteristics are not collected in one independent study but are extracted from various rulings. The first characteristic is a foundational principle: ownership of the corpus ('ayn) of a thing inherently includes ownership of its usufruct (manfa'ah), but the reverse is not true. One can own the benefit without the corpus, such as a lessee (musta'jir) or borrower (musta'ir). The reasoning is that ownership of objects is not an end in itself; the purpose is to derive benefits. The corpus is merely a vehicle for lawful enjoyment and disposal. Therefore, permanent separation of corpus from usufruct is considered absurd ( 'abath) and not sanctioned by the Shari'ah except as temporary exceptions. The only exceptions in Islamic law are two cases of bequest (wasiyyah) of usufruct: (1) bequeathing the use of a house or garden for a limited period or for life, where the heirs own the corpus but cannot use it until the period ends; (2) bequeathing the corpus to one person and the usufruct to another for a period, where the legatee of the corpus owns only the corpus until the usufruct period expires. In both cases, the separation is temporary, and ultimately the usufruct reunites with the corpus. The footnote discusses a theoretical disagreement with a minority Maliki view that ownership actually falls on usufructs, not substances. The majority, including Hanafis, hold that ownership applies to both substances and usufructs. However, the author considers this difference practically fruitless (la thamara lahu). This passage is important for Hanafi fiqh because it establishes a key principle that governs many transactions, such as sale, lease, gift, and bequest.

Key Terms
ملك العين (milk al-ayn): Ownership of the substance or corpus of a thing, as opposed to its usufruct.
ملك المنفعة (milk al-manfa'ah): Ownership of the benefit or usufruct of a thing, which can be separate from ownership of the corpus in contracts like lease.
الوصية بالمنفعة (al-wasiyyah bi al-manfa'ah): A bequest of the usufruct of an asset to a person for a limited period or for life, while the corpus remains owned by the heirs or another legatee.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the first characteristic of ownership in Hanafi fiqh, which of the following is TRUE?
A) Ownership of usufruct always entails ownership of corpus.
B) Ownership of corpus entails ownership of usufruct but not vice versa.
C) Ownership of corpus and usufruct are always independent and separable permanently.
D) Ownership of corpus is only possible if one also owns the usufruct indefinitely.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, ownership of the corpus of a thing necessarily includes ownership of its usufruct, but not vice versa; permanent separation is void, only temporary exceptions are allowed.

First Characteristic: Usufruct Is Temporary
Defining the temporary nature of usufruct and its legal effects on bequests

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the qawida 'al-manfa'ah mawqut ghayr da'im' to distinguish between a valid bequest of usufruct and a waqf when the usufruct is bequeathed perpetually.

Core Idea
Usufruct is temporary; a perpetual bequest of usufruct is a waqf, not a bequest.

The Hanafi legal tradition recognizes that ownership of usufruct (manfa'ah) is inherently temporary, unlike ownership of the corpus (raqabah). This distinction has profound implications for bequests: when someone attempts to bequeath usufruct to a perpetual entity (like 'the poor forever'), the law reclassifies it as an endowment (waqf) attached to after death. In this lesson, we will unpack this first characteristic of ownership and see how it governs the boundary between wasiyyah and waqf.

Guided Path
1
State the qawida and its meaning
Repeat the maxim: 'al-manfa'ah mawqut ghayr da'im' – usufruct is temporary, not perpetual. Understand that this is a foundational characteristic of ownership in Islamic law.
The temporary nature arises because usufruct is consumed or extinguished over time.
2
Identify the scope of the qawida
This qawida governs cases where usufruct is separated from the corpus, such as in bequests (wasaya) and endowments (awqaf). It limits the duration for which usufruct can be owned independently.
Complete ownership (milkiyyah tammah) unites corpus and usufruct, but separation creates temporal constraints.
3
Apply the qawida to the case of a perpetual bequest of usufruct
If someone bequeaths the usufruct of an asset to a perpetual entity (e.g., the poor forever), the qawida tells us that usufruct cannot be owned perpetually. Therefore, the legal arrangement must be reinterpreted: since the usufruct is effectively permanent for the heirs, the corpus is no longer meaningfully owned by them. Hence, it becomes a waqf.
The transformation occurs by necessity (darurah) because perpetual usufruct negates the heirs' ownership of the corpus.
4
Note the thamarah (practical consequence)
The thamarah is that such a bequest is not subject to the rules of wasiyyah (e.g., the one-third limitation) but is governed by the rules of waqf (e.g., irrevocability, perpetuity of benefit for the designated cause).
If the bequest is to a specific person for life, it remains a valid bequest because the duration is temporary (until death).

The chapter (25) Characteristics of Ownership: Usufruct is temporary, not perpetual, and the ownership of the heirs or the legatee of the corpus will eventually become complete ownership in the end, so this separation (of corpus and usufruct) is overlooked momentarily out of respect for the bequest, because the bequest is built on tolerance and contrary to analogy in many of its rulings, as encouragement for doing good at the end of life. And because of this, the bequest of usufruct alone forever to perpetual entities, not to a specific person, departs from the meaning of bequest, and is considered in the meaning of a perpetual waqf, in which private ownership of the corpus falls away and is dedicated forever to the benefit of the permanent charitable entity for which the waqf designator designated it. So if a person bequeaths the fruit of his vineyard or the rent of his house to the poor forever, or to a specific person and after him to the poor, that is considered a waqf attached to after death by necessity due to perpetuity, even if it is in the wording of a bequest, since there remains no meaning for the ownership of the heirs in the corpus as long as the usufruct is for others forever. (See: Fath al-Qadir, beginning of Waqf, 5/419, and Radd al-Muhtar, 3/359).
Source pages p. 338

Commentary
This passage presents the first characteristic of ownership in Islamic law: that usufruct (manfa'ah) is by nature temporary and not perpetual. The author explains the legal implication: when a bequest of usufruct alone is made to a perpetual entity (e.g., the poor forever), it effectively becomes a waqf (endowment). This is because the bequest (wasaya) is built on tolerance and often deviates from strict analogy (qiyas) to encourage good deeds at the end of life. However, if the usufruct is bequeathed perpetually, the heirs' ownership of the corpus becomes meaningless, so it is treated as a waqf attached to after death. The author provides an example: if someone bequeaths the fruit of his vineyard or the rent of his house to the poor forever, it is considered a waqf, not a bequest. The footnote references Fath al-Qadir and Radd al-Muhtar for further details on waqf rulings.

Key Terms
منفعة (manfa'ah): The benefit or usufruct of a thing, which is temporary by nature.
ملكية (milkiyyah): Ownership, which can be complete (shamil for both corpus and usufruct) or incomplete.
وصية (wasiyyah): A bequest, which is built on tolerance and often deviates from analogy.

Key Takeaway
Usufruct is inherently temporary; a perpetual bequest of usufruct to a perpetual entity is reclassified as a waqf in Hanafi law.

Second Characteristic: First Ownership Is Always Complete
Defining the second characteristic of ownership: first ownership is always complete.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the second characteristic of ownership in Hanafi law and apply it to distinguish between creative and transfer causes of ownership.

Core Idea
First ownership of any thing is always complete — it includes both corpus and usufruct.

In this passage, Mustafa Ahmad al-Zarqa explains the second characteristic of ownership in Islamic law: the first acquisition of an object grants complete ownership, covering both the thing itself and its benefits. This principle has profound implications for understanding how property rights are created and transferred. The distinction between creative causes (asbab munshi'ah) and transfer causes (asbab naqilah) is central to the Hanafi theory of property.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Qawida and Its Scope
Extract the exact wording of the qawida from the text: 'The first ownership established on a previously unowned thing is always complete ownership.' Note that it applies to all objects that were not owned before, and it governs both the corpus (raqabah) and usufruct (manfa'ah).
The qawida is absolute; no exceptions are mentioned.
2
Distinguish Creative vs. Transfer Causes
From the text, list the creative causes (asbab munshi'ah): acquisition of ownerless property (ihraz al-mubahat) and generation from owned property (tawallud min al-mamluk). These establish the first complete ownership. Then list transfer causes (asbab naqilah): sale, gift, inheritance, etc. These modify pre-existing ownership.
Creative causes create ownership ex nihilo; transfer causes change the owner of already owned property.
3
Apply the Qawida to a Case
Consider a concrete case: a person hunts a wild animal (ownerless). According to the qawida, the first ownership obtained is complete — the hunter owns both the animal's body and its benefits (meat, skin, etc.). No incomplete ownership is possible at this stage.
This illustrates that subsequent transfers may split the ownership, but the first is always whole.
4
Understand the Relationship to Incomplete Ownership
The passage explains that incomplete ownership (e.g., a lease or a life interest) can only arise after a complete ownership existed. The previous owner transfers either both corpus and usufruct (complete) or only one (incomplete). Thus, incomplete ownership is always derivative.
This means incomplete ownership cannot be created by a creative cause — it requires a transfer.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
٣/٢٥ - الخاصية الثانية :
إن أول ملكية تثبت على الشيء الذي لم يكن مملوكاً قبلها إنما تكون دائماً ملكية تامة . أي إنها تكون شاملة لرقبة الشيء ومنافعه معاً. فلا يمكن أن تثبت الملكية الأولى على الشيء ناقصة مقصورة على رقبته دون منافعه أو على منافعه دون رقبته.
والملكية الأولى التي تثبت على الشيء بسبب منشيء، أي بطريق إحراز المباحات أو بالتولد من المملوك.
ومتى ثبتت تلك الملكية الأولى على شيء بسببها المنشيء لا يتصور بعدها أن يتعلق بذلك الشيء ملك جديد إلا بالسبب الناقل للملكية دون المنشيء، لأن كل ملك يثبت على شيء مملوك قبلاً إنما يكون من قبيل تبدل المالك، وهذا هو معنى نقل الملكية.
ثم إنَّ المالك السابق إما أن تنتقل عنه ملكية العين والمنفعة معاً فتثبت لغيره ملكية تامة كما في البيع والهبة ونحوهما من الأسباب التي توجب ملكاً تاماً؛ وإما أن تنتقل عنه ملكية المنفعة وحدها أو الرقبة وحدها، فتثبت لغيره ملكية ناقصة بأحد أسبابها المشروعة التي تقدم بيانها في تقسيم الملك.
ويستنتج من ذلك أنَّ الملكية التامة تتعلق بها الأسباب المنشِئَة والناقلة، فتنشأ أولاً بالإحراز أو التولد من المملوك، ثم تنتقل بالعقود أو الخلفية.
أما الملكية الناقصة فلا يتعلق بها من أسباب التملك إلا السبب الناقل عن مالك سابق، لأن ملكية منافع الشيء وحدها أو رقبته وحدها تكون مسبوقة دائماً بملكية تامة فيه .
3/25 - The Second Characteristic: The first ownership that is established on a thing that was not owned before it is always complete ownership. That is, it includes both the corpus of the thing and its usufructs together. So it is not possible for the first ownership to be established on a thing as incomplete, confined to its corpus without its usufructs, or to its usufructs without its corpus. And the first ownership that is established on a thing is by a creative cause (sabab munshi'), i.e., by way of acquisition of permissible things (ihraz al-mubahat) or by generation (tawallud) from owned property. And once that first ownership is established on a thing by its creative cause, it is inconceivable thereafter that a new ownership should attach to that thing except by a transfer cause (sabab naqil) without the creative cause, because every ownership that is established on something previously owned is only of the type of change of owner, and this is the meaning of transfer of ownership. Then, the previous owner either transfers both the ownership of the corpus and the usufruct together, so that complete ownership is established for the other, as in sale, gift, and similar causes that necessitate complete ownership; or he transfers only the ownership of the usufruct or only the corpus, so that incomplete ownership is established for the other by one of its legitimate causes that were previously explained in the division of ownership. And it is deduced from this that complete ownership is associated with creative and transfer causes; it arises first by acquisition or generation from owned property, then it is transferred by contracts or inheritance (khalafiyyah). As for incomplete ownership, none of the causes of acquisition are associated with it except the transfer cause from a previous owner, because the ownership of the usufructs of a thing alone or its corpus alone is always preceded by complete ownership in it.
Source pages p. 338

Commentary
This passage presents the second characteristic of ownership: the first ownership that arises on a previously unowned object is always complete, encompassing both the corpus (raqabah) and the usufruct (manfa'ah). The author distinguishes between creative causes (asbab munshi'ah) such as seizing ownerless property (ihraz al-mubahat) or offspring from owned property, and transfer causes (asbab naqilah) such as sale, gift, or inheritance. Once complete ownership is established, any subsequent ownership change must result from a transfer cause. The passage then explains that transfer can either pass both corpus and usufruct (resulting in complete ownership for the new owner) or just one of them (resulting in incomplete ownership). Thus, incomplete ownership (e.g., a life interest) always presupposes a prior complete ownership. This principle is foundational to understanding the Hanafi theory of property rights.

Key Terms
ملكية تامة (milkiyyah tammah): Ownership that includes both the corpus (raqabah) and the usufruct (manfa'ah) of a thing.
ملكية ناقصة (milkiyyah naqisah): Ownership that is confined to either the corpus or the usufruct alone.
سبب منشيء (sabab munshi'): A cause that creates original ownership, such as acquisition of ownerless property or generation from owned property.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the second characteristic, incomplete ownership (e.g., owning only the usufruct) can be established only by:
A) A creative cause like ihraz al-mubahat.
B) A transfer cause from a previous owner.
C) Either a creative or a transfer cause.
D) Neither, as it is impossible.

Key Takeaway
The first ownership of any object is always complete; incomplete ownership is derivative and requires a prior complete ownership.

Qawida: Ownership of Corpus is Perpetual, Usufruct is Temporary
Defining a legal maxim and illustrating it with a hadith on 'Umra.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi legal maxim regarding the temporality of corpus vs. usufruct ownership, identify its scope and exceptions, and apply it to the case of 'Umra.

Core Idea
Ownership of a physical thing is permanent; only the right to use it can be temporary.

This passage introduces a foundational legal maxim in Hanafi property law: ownership of the corpus (al-'ayn) is inherently permanent, while ownership of usufruct (al-manfa'ah) is originally temporary. This principle governs many transactions, from gifts to endowments. The text illustrates it with the case of 'Umra (life grant), where a donor tries to make ownership conditional on the donee's lifetime, but the Prophet ﷺ invalidated that condition, affirming that corpus ownership cannot be made temporary.

Guided Path
1
State the maxim
Identify the two-part rule: corpus ownership does not accept temporal limitation (ملكية العين لا تقبل التوقيت), whereas usufruct ownership is originally temporary (ملكية المنفعة فالأصل فيها التوقيت).
The maxim is expressed in the opening sentence.
2
Understand the rationale
Corpus ownership, once established by a cause, continues indefinitely unless a new transfer cause arises (contract, inheritance). It cannot spontaneously terminate. Usufruct, being a benefit, is naturally limited by time or usage.
The author explains that ownership continues until a new cause transfers it.
3
Examine the dalil
The hadith on 'Umra is the primary evidence: the Prophet ﷺ ruled that a gift conditional on return upon death is invalid, making the gift permanent for the donee and his heirs. This proves that corpus ownership cannot be temporary.
The hadith is narrated by Muslim and others; its ruling is clear.
4
Identify the exception
Waqf (endowment) is not a counterexample because the corpus is alienated from private ownership permanently; it is not a case of temporary ownership but of permanent dedication to Allah.
The exception is in the istithna'at of the qawida.
5
Apply the maxim
In any transaction where someone tries to impose a time limit on corpus ownership (e.g., 'I give you this for 10 years, then it returns'), the condition is void and the transfer becomes permanent. Only usufruct can be limited (e.g., lease, loan for use).
The furuat of the qawida include 'Umra and similar cases.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
٤/٢٥ - الخاصة الثالثة :
إن ملكية العين لا تقبل التوقيت أما ملكية المنفعة فالأصل فيها التوقيت ..
فملكية العين متى ثبتت بأحد أسبابها تثبت مؤيَّدة، وإنما يتصور أن يطرأ عليها بعد ذلك انتقال بسبب جديد ناقل فإن لم يطرأ عليها ناقل من عقد جديد أو خلفية لا تنتهي الملكية التي سبق ثبوتها.
وعلى ذلك أثبت الرسول الله في العُمرى (1) الملك الدائم بقوله :
أيَّما رجلٍ أُعمِرَ عُمْرى فهي له ، ولعقبه (۲) .
(1) العُمْرى (بضم العين والألف المقصورة وزان صغرى :) هي أن يهب إنسان الآخر شيئاً مدى عمره، أي على أنه إذا مات الموهوب له عاد الشيء إلى الواهب.

4/25 - The Third Characteristic: Ownership of the corpus does not accept temporal limitation, whereas ownership of usufruct is originally temporary. So the ownership of the corpus, whenever it is established by one of its causes, is established as permanent; it is only conceivable that thereafter a transfer occurs by a new transfer cause. If no transfer from a new contract or inheritance (khalafiyyah) occurs, the ownership that was previously established does not terminate. And accordingly, the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) established permanent ownership in 'Umra by his saying: 'Any man who gives a gift on the basis of 'Umra, it belongs to him and his descendants.' (1) 'Umra (with damma on 'ayn and alif maqsura, like sughra) is that a person gives another something for the duration of his life, meaning that if the donee dies, the thing returns to the donor. And it is by his saying: 'I give it to you as 'Umra' or 'I give it to you for life' and similar expressions. (2) Narrated by Muslim on the authority of Jabir in the Book of Gifts (1625), and al-Tirmidhi in the Book of Rulings (1350) and said: hasan sahih, and Ahmad in al-Musnad 3/399.
Source pages p. 339

Commentary
This passage presents the third characteristic of ownership: ownership of the corpus (al-'ayn) is perpetual and does not accept temporal limitation, while ownership of usufruct (al-manfa'ah) is inherently temporary. The author explains that once ownership of a physical object is established, it continues indefinitely unless transferred by a new cause (e.g., sale, gift, inheritance). It cannot be made to expire automatically after a certain time. To illustrate this, the author cites the case of 'Umra (life grant), where the donor gives property to someone for the recipient's lifetime on condition that it returns to the donor upon the recipient's death. The hadith from the Prophet (peace be upon him) rules that such a condition is invalid; the 'Umra gift becomes permanent property of the donee and is inherited by his heirs. This demonstrates that ownership of the corpus cannot be made temporary; any attempt to limit it temporally is overridden by the principle of permanent ownership. The footnote defines 'Umra and provides the hadith references.

Key Terms
عين ('ayn): The physical object or corpus of property, as opposed to its usufruct.
منفعة (manfa'ah): Usufruct or benefit derived from the 'ayn, which is naturally temporary.
عمرى ('umra): A life grant of property, conditional on return to the donor upon the donee's death.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) Corpus ownership can be made to expire by agreement.
B) Usufruct ownership is naturally temporary.
C) The condition of return in 'Umra is valid.
D) Ownership of 'ayn ends upon the death of the owner.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi maxim that corpus ownership is perpetual, while usufruct is temporary, is exemplified by the invalidity of conditional 'Umra gifts.

Revocation of Temporary Loan of Land: Cultivation vs. Building
Detailed ruling on revocation of loan for agricultural land with conditions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi legal maxim on revocation of temporary loans (ʿāriyah) to distinguish between cases of cultivation and building/planting, and determine the appropriate remedy including compensation (ḍamān) when taghrīr occurs.

Core Idea
A temporary loan of land for cultivation cannot be revoked until harvest; for planting or building, revocation is allowed but may require compensation if the term is breached.

This passage from Dr. Mustafa al-Zarqa's Al-Madkhal al-Fiqhi al-Amm presents a detailed ruling on the revocation of a temporary loan of land. It distinguishes between cultivation, which has a defined seasonal end, and planting or building, which do not. The ruling protects the borrower from harm while preserving the lender's ownership rights, and introduces the concept of taghrīr (deception) as a basis for compensation when the lender revokes early in a temporary loan for building or planting.

Guided Path
1
Identify the legal maxim and its scope
The underlying maxim is that the owner of an object has the right to revoke a loan (ʿāriyah) at any time, unless revocation would cause harm to the borrower that outweighs the lender's interest. This maxim is subject to exceptions based on the nature of the borrower's use.
The Hanafi school gives weight to preventing harm (ḍarar) as a overriding consideration.
2
Distinguish between the furuat: cultivation vs. planting/building
In the case of cultivation, the lender cannot revoke until the harvest is complete, because the crop has a fixed seasonal end and the borrower would suffer irreparable loss. In the case of planting or building, the lender can revoke at any time because these activities have no fixed end; the borrower must remove them at his own cost.
The passage explicitly compares the seasonal nature of cultivation to the indefinite nature of trees and buildings.
3
Apply the istithna: reward compensation when the loan was temporary and revocation is early
If the loan for planting or building was for a specified term and the lender revokes before the term ends, the lender has deceived the borrower (taghrīr) and must compensate the borrower for the difference in value between immediate removal and remaining until the term ends.
This compensates the borrower for the loss of the expected benefit.
4
Work through the calculation: an example
The passage gives an example: if the value at removal is 12 dinars and the value if allowed to remain until term end is 20 dinars, the lender pays 8 dinars. This shows that compensation is based on the diminution in value caused by the premature revocation.
This is a concrete application of the principle of ḍamān arising from taghrīr.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الباب الخامس: نظرية الملكية في الفقه
الرجوع عن توقيت العارية من ضرر للمستعير، كما لو كانت العارية أرضاً أعارها صاحبها إلى غيره للزرع أو الغرس أو البناء مدة معينة، فَزَرَع فيها المستعير أو غرس أو بنى، ثم رجع المعير وطلب استرداد الأرض قبل انقضاء المدة المضروبة .
فقد قرَّرَ الفقهاء في ذلك تفصيلاً:
لأن للزرع أمداً موسمياً ينتهي فيه.
:أولاً : ففي صورة الزرع لا يحق للمعير استرداد الأرض قبل استحصاد الزرع، ولا فرق في صورة إعارة الأرض لأجل الزرع بين أن تكون هذه الإعارة موقتة أو مطلقة بل يبقى الزرع إلى أن يستحصد على الأرض بأجر مثلها عن المدة التي بين تاريخ الرجوع والاستحصاد. وفي ذلك حفظ المعير ومنع الضرر عن المستعير .
ثانياً : وفي صورتي الغرس والبناء يحق للمعير أن يرجع في الأرض. المعارة للغرس والبناء مطلقاً في أي وقت شاء، سواء أكانت الإعارة مطلقة أو موقوتة. وعندئذ يكلف المستعير قلع الغراس أو البناء، وتسليم الأرض إلى المعير خالية عنه وذلك لأنَّ الغرس أو البناء ليس لهما أمد ينتهيان إليه :
كأمد الحصاد للزرع . ولكنها إذا كانت موقوتة فرجع المعير قبل الأجل الذي ضربه للمستعير الغارس أو الباني، فإنَّ المعير يكون قد غَرَّر بالمستعير تغريراً أضر به، فيحق للمستعير تضمين المعير مقدار تفاوت قيمة البناء أو الغراس بين وقت القلع وانتهاء المدة التي كانت مضروبة للإعارة.
فلو كان البناء أو الشجر المقلوع قيمته حين الرجوع والقلع اثني عشر ديناراً، وقيمته في ذلك الحين على أساس بقائه إلى انتهاء الأجل المضروب للإعارة عشرون ديناراً يضمن المعير للمستعير ثمانية دنانير (ر:
المجلة / ۸۳۱ - ۸۳۲ والدر المختار ٥٠٥/٤) .
Chapter 5: Theory of Property in Jurisprudence. Revocation of the timing of a loan (ariyah) is harmful to the borrower, such as if the loan is land that the owner lent to another for cultivation, planting, or building for a specified period, and the borrower planted, built, or cultivated, then the lender revoked and demanded return of the land before the expiry of the stipulated term. The jurists have detailed this: Because cultivation has a seasonal end. First: In the case of cultivation, the lender is not entitled to reclaim the land before the harvest, and there is no difference whether the loan of land for cultivation was temporary or absolute; rather, the crop remains until it is harvested on the land with its rental equivalent for the period between the date of revocation and harvest. In this is preservation of the lender and prevention of harm to the borrower. Second: In the cases of planting and building, the lender has the right to reclaim the land lent for planting or building at any time he wishes, whether the loan was absolute or temporary. Then the borrower is required to remove the trees or building and deliver the land free of them, because planting and building have no definite end like the harvest season. But if it was temporary and the lender revoked before the term he set for the borrower who planted or built, then the lender has deceived the borrower with damaging deception, so the borrower is entitled to claim from the lender the difference in value of the building or trees between the time of removal and the end of the stipulated term. For example, if the building or removed trees were worth twelve dinars at the time of revocation and removal, and their value at that time based on remaining until the end of the loan term was twenty dinars, the lender guarantees the borrower eight dinars. (Refer: Majalla articles 831-832 and al-Durr al-Mukhtar 4/505).
Source pages p. 341

Commentary
This passage from Dr. Mustafa al-Zarqa's Al-Madkhal al-Fiqhi al-Amm discusses the legal ruling on the revocation of a temporary loan (ariyah) of land. The passage type is a detailed fiqh ruling with conditions and consequences. The author presents two distinct scenarios based on the type of use: cultivation vs. planting/building. For cultivation, the lender cannot revoke until the harvest is complete, because cultivation has a defined season and the borrower would suffer irreparable harm if forced to remove the crop early. The borrower may retain the land by paying rent for the remaining period until harvest. This protects the lender's ownership while preventing harm to the borrower. For planting (trees) or building, however, the lender can revoke at any time, because these have no fixed end date. The borrower must remove his property at his own expense. But if the loan was for a specified term and the lender revokes early, he has deceived the borrower (taghrir), and the borrower can claim compensation for the difference in value of the building/trees between the time of removal and the end of the term. This is a manifestation of the Hanafi principle of preventing harm (darar) and ensuring justice.

Key Terms
العارية (al-ʿāriyah): A loan of a specific object (often land) for temporary use without rent.
المعير (al-muʿīr): The lender who gives the object as a loan.
المستعير (al-mustaʿīr): The borrower who receives the loaned object.
تغرير (taghrīr): Deception or misrepresentation that causes harm to the other party.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The lender can reclaim the land immediately.
B) The borrower must leave the land but can claim compensation for the crop value.
C) The lender cannot reclaim the land until the harvest is complete, and the borrower pays rent for the remaining period.
D) The borrower may keep the land without paying any rent.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, the right to revoke a loan of land is balanced against the harm to the borrower, leading to different rules for cultivation (delayed revocation) and planting/building (immediate revocation with possible compensation for early termination).
